Technical Curricular Documentation: Week 1 Lesson Plan
Course Identification
Unit Title:
 Week 1: Molecular Rizz - Why Stuff Acts Different
 
Grade Level:
 7th Grade Science
 
Instructional Time:
 ~60 minutes (within 75-minute class period)
 
Total Point Value:
 70 points
 
Institution:
 Kairos Academies
 
Instructor:
 Louis Rosche
NGSS Three-Dimensional Learning Framework
Primary Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-1
 (Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
 
Develop models to describe the atomic composition of simple molecules and extended structures.
Disciplinary Core Idea (DCI)
PS1.A: Structure and Properties of Matter
Focus: Atomic composition and molecular arrangement
Scope: Simple molecules (H‚ÇÇO, CO‚ÇÇ) and basic extended structures
Conceptual anchor: Relationship between molecular structure and observable properties
Science and Engineering Practice (SEP)
SEP-2: Developing and Using Models
Element 1:
 Create physical or digital models to represent molecular structures
Element 2:
 Use models to visualize and predict atomic arrangements in 3D space
Application:
 PhET simulation-based molecular construction with evidence documentation via screenshots
Crosscutting Concept (CCC)
CCC-7: Structure and Function
Element 1:
 Structures serve particular functions based on material properties and arrangement
Central Theme:
 Molecular structural arrangement determines macroscopic properties and functional behavior
Reinforcement:
 Explicit structure ‚Üí property causation throughout all stations
Instructional Objectives (Standards-Based Learning Targets)
Target 1 (Foundational - DOK 1)
Build Molecular Models
Objective:
 Construct minimum 2 molecules (H‚ÇÇO, CO‚ÇÇ) using digital modeling tools
Success Criteria:
 Correct atom count, accurate bonding connections, valid 3D spatial arrangements, screenshot evidence
Alignment:
 Direct correlation to SEP-2 (model development)
Target 2 (Application - DOK 2)
Identify Structural Patterns
Objective:
 Recognize minimum 2 patterns in atomic bonding configurations and molecular geometries
Success Criteria:
 Match molecular formulas to 3D shapes; explain bonding arrangement rationale
Molecular Geometries Addressed:
 Linear, bent, tetrahedral
Alignment:
 Bridges SEP-2 and CCC-7
Target 3 (Analysis - DOK 3)
Explain Structure-Property Relationships
Objective:
 Articulate causal connections between molecular structure and observable properties
Success Criteria:
 Predict properties from structural features; construct complete CER (Claim-Evidence-Reasoning) argument
Alignment:
 Full integration of DCI (PS1.A), SEP-2, and CCC-7
Lesson Sequence and Pedagogical Structure
Phase 1: Phenomenological Hook (10 points, ~5 minutes)
Instructional Purpose:
 Establish cognitive dissonance through observable phenomenon
 
Phenomenon Presented:
 Three substances (water, salt, sugar) composed of atoms exhibit disparate properties
 
Driving Question:
 "If they're ALL made of atoms... why do they look and act SO different?"
 
Student Activity:
 Theory formulation via Google Form submission
 
Assessment Type:
 Formative (pre-instruction reasoning baseline)
 
3D Alignment:
 Minimal explicit 3D instruction; establishes need for DCI understanding
Phase 2: Station Rotation Model (~36 minutes total, 45 points)
Pedagogical Model:
 Differentiated station rotation with 1:1 Chromebook integration
 
Rotation Time:
 ~12 minutes per station
 
Instructional Design:
 Blended learning with digital tools and guided inquiry
Station 1: Molecule Builder (15 points, ~12 minutes)
Primary Tool:
 PhET Interactive Simulation (Molecule Shapes)
 
Target Molecules:
 H‚ÇÇO (water), CO‚ÇÇ (carbon dioxide)
 
Student Actions:
Navigate to "Model" tab in PhET simulation
Construct molecules through digital atom manipulation
Document 3D molecular geometry via Chromebook screenshot (Ctrl+Shift+Show Windows)
Submit visual evidence via embedded Google Form
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Developing and Using Models)
Students create computational models
Models represent 3D spatial relationships
Evidence documentation through visual artifacts
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A focus on atomic composition and structural arrangement
 
CCC Integration:
 Implicit structure observation; function connection deferred to later stations
Scaffolding Provided:
Screenshot tutorial with visual guide
"Real Molecules" tab reference for verification
Explicit atom count specifications (H‚ÇÇO: 2H + 1O; CO‚ÇÇ: 1C + 2O)
Bonding emphasis (atoms must be connected, not proximal)
What Was NOT Taught:
Bond angle measurements
Electronegativity or polarity concepts
Bonding theory (ionic/covalent distinction)
Electron configuration
Station 2: Matching Challenge (15 points, ~12 minutes)
Instructional Focus:
 Pattern recognition in molecular geometry
 
Student Actions:
Analyze molecular formulas
Visualize 3D atomic arrangements
Match formulas to geometric representations
Identify structural patterns across molecule types
Molecular Geometries Explicitly Addressed:
Linear:
 Atoms in straight line (example: CO‚ÇÇ)
Bent:
 Atoms at angle (example: H‚ÇÇO)
Tetrahedral:
 3D pyramid arrangement (example: CH‚ÇÑ)
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Using Models) - interpreting existing molecular representations
 
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A structural patterns
 
CCC Integration:
 CCC-7 (Structure) - recognition that specific structures recur based on atomic composition
Scaffolding Provided:
Reference to prior Station 1 experiences
Explicit geometry vocabulary introduction
Pattern-seeking prompts (central atom consistency)
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with formula-to-shape matching items
What Was NOT Taught:
VSEPR theory or electron pair repulsion
Hybridization concepts
Quantitative bond angles
Molecular orbital theory
Station 3: Property Detective (15 points, ~12 minutes)
Instructional Focus:
 Structure-property causal relationships
 
Properties Examined:
Solubility in water
Gas phase at room temperature
Crystal formation
Electrical conductivity
Flammability
Student Actions:
Observe molecular structures (teacher-provided on board)
Predict properties based on structural features
Match molecules to property profiles
Justify predictions with structural reasoning
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Using Models for Prediction)
 
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A (structure determines properties) - core concept application
 
CCC Integration:
 CCC-7 (Structure and Function) - explicit causal connections
Heuristic Rules Provided (Qualitative):
Small, simple molecules ‚Üí often gases
Charged molecules (ions) ‚Üí conduct electricity, form crystals
Bent/asymmetric molecules ‚Üí dissolve polar substances
Carbon-hydrogen molecules ‚Üí often flammable
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with structure-property matching and reasoning
What Was NOT Taught:
Intermolecular force types (hydrogen bonding, London dispersion, dipole-dipole)
Quantitative property predictions
Thermodynamic principles
Phase diagram interpretation
Detailed ionic bonding mechanisms
Phase 3: Sense-Making Discussion (~10 minutes)
Instructional Purpose:
 Synthesize station experiences into coherent conceptual framework
 
Central Claim:
 "Molecular structure determines properties"
 
Pedagogical Approach:
 Guided class discourse with worked example
Worked Example: Water (H‚ÇÇO)
Structure:
 Bent molecular geometry, 1 oxygen + 2 hydrogens
Structural Feature:
 Bent shape creates charge asymmetry (polarity)
Resulting Property:
 Solubility of ionic compounds (e.g., salt dissolution)
Causal Chain:
 Structure ‚Üí charge distribution ‚Üí interaction capability ‚Üí macroscopic property
Student Writing Task:
 CER (Claim-Evidence-Reasoning) construction in science notebook
Claim:
 Specific property of one molecule from stations
Evidence:
 Structural description (shape, atoms, arrangement)
Reasoning:
 Mechanistic explanation of how structure causes property
Scaffolding Provided:
Sentence starters for each CER component
Modeled example with complete causal chain
Explicit connection to professional chemistry practices
3D Integration:
 Full synthesis of DCI (PS1.A), SEP-2 (model-based reasoning), and CCC-7 (structure-function causation)
Phase 4: Exit Ticket (15 points, ~8 minutes)
Assessment Type:
 Formative summative (checks all three targets)
 
Delivery Mechanism:
 Google Form with embedded questions
 
Differentiation Strategy:
 Three challenge levels aligned to DOK progression
Level 1 (DOK 1):
 Identify molecular structures from formulas
 
Level 2 (DOK 2):
 Match structures to properties
 
Level 3 (DOK 3):
 Explain structure-property causation with scientific reasoning
Success Criteria Assessed:
Target 1: Atomic connection description
Target 2: Pattern identification in molecular shapes
Target 3: Structure-property reasoning with evidence
What Was NOT Assessed:
Quantitative calculations
Complex molecule prediction beyond simple examples
Laboratory technique application
Chemical equation balancing
Real-World Application Connections
Explicitly Mentioned Career/Application Domains:
Pharmaceutical Science:
 Drug molecule design with receptor-specific shapes
Materials Engineering:
 Polymer design for specific applications (water bottles, bulletproof vests)
Environmental Science:
 Pollutant-water system interactions
Food Science:
 Flavor molecule dissolution mechanics
Everyday Phenomena Explained:
Fire suppression (water) vs. combustion acceleration (gasoline)
Pharmaceutical efficacy (Tylenol) vs. nutritional supplements (vitamins)
Digestion selectivity (pizza vs. cardboard)
Cleaning mechanisms (soap vs. water for grease)
Instructional Technology Integration
Primary Digital Tools:
PhET Interactive Simulation
 (University of Colorado Boulder) - Molecule Shapes module
Google Forms
 (4 embedded forms for assessment)
Chromebook Screenshot Functionality
 (Ctrl+Shift+Show Windows)
Canvas LMS
 (hosting platform for HTML lesson)
Blended Learning Model:
 Station rotation with digital-physical integration
 
Device Ratio:
 1:1 Chromebook access assumed
 
Accessibility Features:
 ARIA labels, responsive design, multiple representation modes
Assessment Architecture
Point Distribution
Hook:
 10 points (14.3%)
Station 1:
 15 points (21.4%)
Station 2:
 15 points (21.4%)
Station 3:
 15 points (21.4%)
Exit Ticket:
 15 points (21.4%)
Total:
 70 points
Assessment Types
Formative:
 Hook question, station activities, class discussion participation
Formative Summative:
 Exit ticket (checks learning but informs instruction)
Evidence Types:
 Digital artifacts (screenshots), written responses (CER), selected responses (matching)
Grading Philosophy
Standards-based approach referenced
Process-oriented feedback ("HOW you arrived at conclusions")
Scientific uncertainty normalized
Curricular Scope: What Was Explicitly EXCLUDED
NGSS Standards NOT Addressed
MS-PS1-2 (Conservation of matter in reactions)
MS-PS1-3 (Thermal energy in reactions)
MS-PS1-4 (Synthetic materials)
MS-PS1-5 (Reactants and products)
MS-PS1-6 (Temperature and reaction rates)
Chemistry Concepts NOT Taught
Bonding Theory:
 No explicit ionic vs. covalent distinction; no electronegativity scale; no bond polarity calculations
Quantitative Chemistry:
 No stoichiometry; no molar calculations; no formula mass
Atomic Structure:
 No electron configuration; no quantum numbers; no orbital theory
Reaction Chemistry:
 No chemical equations; no balancing; no reaction types
Thermodynamics:
 No enthalpy; no entropy; no Gibbs free energy
Kinetics:
 No reaction rates; no collision theory; no activation energy
Advanced Molecular Theory:
 No VSEPR quantitative predictions; no molecular orbital diagrams; no resonance structures
Laboratory Practice:
 No hands-on manipulation; no safety protocols (digital simulation substituted)
SEPs NOT Explicitly Addressed
SEP-1 (Asking Questions)
SEP-3 (Planning and Carrying Out Investigations)
SEP-4 (Analyzing and Interpreting Data - minimal)
SEP-5 (Using Mathematics and Computational Thinking)
SEP-6 (Constructing Explanations - partially via CER)
SEP-7 (Engaging in Argument from Evidence - minimal)
SEP-8 (Obtaining, Evaluating, and Communicating Information - minimal)
CCCs NOT Explicitly Addressed
CCC-1 (Patterns) - implicit but not focal
CCC-2 (Cause and Effect) - implicit in structure-property
CCC-3 (Scale, Proportion, and Quantity)
CCC-4 (Systems and System Models)
CCC-5 (Energy and Matter)
CCC-6 (Stability and Change)
Universal Design for Learning (UDL) Features
Multiple Means of Representation:
Visual gradients and color coding
Emoji-based navigation
Text descriptions with visual supports
Digital simulation (kinesthetic/visual)
Multiple Means of Action and Expression:
Station choice autonomy (rotation ensures all experience)
Exit ticket differentiation (3 challenge levels)
CER writing option
Screenshot evidence submission
Multiple Means of Engagement:
Student-relevant language ("rizz," "peak," "no cap")
Real-world application anchoring
Gamification language ("boss fight," "unlock")
Clear success criteria transparency
Pedagogical Framework Summary
Instructional Model:
 5E Variation (Engage-Explore-Explain-Elaborate-Evaluate)
Engage:
 Hook phenomenon
Explore:
 Station rotations 1-3
Explain:
 Class discussion and CER construction
Elaborate:
 Implicit in differentiated exit ticket
Evaluate:
 Exit ticket formative summative
Cognitive Load Management:
Chunked instruction (12-minute stations)
Scaffolded progression (build ‚Üí identify ‚Üí predict)
Visual navigation aids
Explicit success criteria at each phase
Evidence-Based Practices Referenced:
Station rotation model (documented in research for middle school science)
Digital simulation for abstract concepts (PhET research base)
CER argumentation framework (NGSS-aligned practice)
Formative assessment embedded throughout

 Claude (Anthropic)

Technical Curricular Documentation: Week 2 Lesson Plan
Course Identification
Unit Title:
 Week 2: Chemical Bonding - The Electron Game
 
Grade Level:
 7th Grade Science
 
Instructional Time:
 ~50 minutes (within 75-minute class period)
 
Total Point Value:
 90 points
 
Institution:
 Kairos Academies
 
Instructor:
 Louis Rosche
 
Cycle Designation:
 Cycle 2, Week 2
NGSS Three-Dimensional Learning Framework
Primary Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-1
 (Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
 
Develop models to describe the atomic composition of simple molecules and extended structures.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can explain how atoms bond together through electron transfer (ionic) or sharing (covalent) and predict compound properties."
Connected Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-4
 
Develop a model that predicts and describes changes in particle motion, temperature, and state of a pure substance when thermal energy is added or removed.
Disciplinary Core Idea (DCI)
PS1.A: Structure and Properties of Matter
Focus: Electron behavior in chemical bonding
Scope: Ionic bonding (electron transfer), covalent bonding (electron sharing), bond-property relationships
Conceptual anchor: Bond type determines macroscopic physical properties
Science and Engineering Practice (SEP)
SEP-2: Developing and Using Models
Element 1:
 Create models to represent electron transfer in ionic bonding
Element 3:
 Use models to describe electron sharing in covalent bonding
Application:
 PhET "Build an Atom" simulation, electron arrangement descriptions, Lewis structure representations (implied)
Crosscutting Concept (CCC)
CCC-7: Structure and Function
Element 1:
 Structures (bonding arrangements) determine functions (physical properties)
Central Theme:
 Bond type (ionic vs. covalent) causally determines property profiles
Application:
 Predicting melting point, electrical conductivity, state of matter, and crystal formation from bonding type
Instructional Objectives (Standards-Based Learning Targets)
Target 1 (Classification - DOK 2)
Distinguish Bond Types
Objective:
 Identify ionic vs. covalent bonds by analyzing element types and electron behavior
Success Criteria:
 "CaCl‚ÇÇ is ionic because calcium (metal) + chlorine (nonmetal) means electron transfer creates charged ions"
Assessment Method:
 Element pair analysis, bond type identification from formulas
Alignment:
 Direct correlation to DCI (PS1.A) bonding classification
Target 2 (Explanation - DOK 2)
Explain Electron Behavior
Objective:
 Articulate when/why electrons transfer (ionic) versus share (covalent), including octet rule motivation
Success Criteria:
 "Atoms bond to achieve full outer shells‚Äîmetals lose electrons to form positive ions, nonmetals gain electrons to form negative ions, or nonmetals share electrons"
Mechanistic Focus:
 Valence electron configuration drives bonding behavior
Alignment:
 SEP-2 (model-based explanation) and DCI (PS1.A)
Target 3 (Prediction - DOK 3)
Predict Properties from Bond Type
Objective:
 Predict physical properties (melting point, electrical conductivity, solubility, state) from ionic vs. covalent classification
Success Criteria:
 "High melting point + conducts electricity when dissolved = ionic compound, because strong attractions between charged ions require lots of energy to break"
Properties Addressed:
 Melting point, electrical conductivity, crystalline structure, state at room temperature
Alignment:
 Full integration of DCI and CCC-7 (structure determines function)
Target 4 (Modeling - DOK 2)
Model Chemical Bonds
Objective:
 Draw or describe accurate electron arrangements showing transfer (ionic) and sharing (covalent) mechanisms
Success Criteria:
 Creating Lewis structures or detailed descriptions of NaCl formation (electron transfer) and H‚ÇÇO formation (electron sharing)
Representation Types:
 Electron dot diagrams (implied), charge notation (e.g., Mg¬≤‚Å∫, O¬≤‚Åª), verbal descriptions
Alignment:
 Primary SEP-2 application
Phenomenological Framing
Anchoring Mystery
Driving Question:
 Why do salt (NaCl) and sugar (C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ) look identical but behave completely differently?
Observable Contrasts Presented:
Property
Salt (NaCl)
Sugar (C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ)
Melting Point
801¬∞C
186¬∞C
Electrical Conductivity
Conducts when dissolved
Does not conduct
Crystal Structure
Cubic crystals
Irregular crystals
Appearance
White crystalline solid
White crystalline solid
Pedagogical Purpose:
 Create cognitive dissonance requiring mechanistic explanation (bond type causation)
Resolution Strategy:
 Sequential investigation (ionic ‚Üí covalent ‚Üí comparison) culminating in explanatory synthesis
Prior Knowledge Activation and Scaffolding
Explicit Connection to Week 1
Week 1 Content:
 Molecular structure determines properties (focus on 3D arrangements)
 
Week 2 Content:
 WHY atoms stick together and HOW they form different structures (focus on electron behavior)
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that molecules have different structures and that structure determines properties. This week you're discovering WHY atoms stick together in the first place and HOW they form those different structures."
Retrieval Practice Elements
Do Now Component:
 Review of atomic structure fundamentals required for bonding concepts
Three subatomic particles and charges (protons +, electrons ‚àí, neutrons neutral)
Atomic number significance (proton count determines element identity)
Element identification from proton count
Particle charge and location matching
Prior knowledge about atomic combination
Metacognitive Prompting:
 Students asked to record initial hypothesis about salt vs. sugar differences before instruction, then revisit after learning
Lesson Sequence and Pedagogical Structure
Phase 1: Do Now - Atoms &amp; Elements Review (10 points, ~5-7 minutes)
Instructional Purpose:
 Activate prerequisite atomic structure knowledge essential for bonding comprehension
Content Reviewed:
Subatomic particle identification (protons, neutrons, electrons)
Charge assignments (protons positive, electrons negative, neutrons neutral)
Atomic number interpretation (proton count)
Element identification by proton count
Preliminary atomic combination concepts
Cognitive Science Rationale (Explicitly Stated to Students):
 "When you activate your prior knowledge before learning new material, your brain creates 'hooks' to attach new information to. Even if you don't remember everything perfectly, the act of trying to recall strengthens your memory pathways!"
Readiness Criteria Provided:
Can name three subatomic particles
Knows protons determine element identity
Understands electrons have negative charge and participate in bonding
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with foundational questions
3D Alignment:
 Minimal direct 3D instruction; establishes atomic structure foundation for DCI application
What Was NOT Taught:
Electron shell/orbital structure
Quantum mechanical electron configuration
Detailed periodic table organization
Nuclear chemistry concepts
Phase 2: Station 1 - Ionic Bonding (20 points, ~8-10 minutes)
Central Concept:
 Ionic bonds form through COMPLETE electron transfer
Instructional Focus:
 Metal-nonmetal interactions resulting in ion formation
Worked Example: Magnesium Oxide (MgO) Formation
Step-by-Step Expert Reasoning Provided:
"Magnesium is a metal with 2 valence electrons. It's easier to lose 2 than gain 6."
"Oxygen is a nonmetal with 6 valence electrons. It needs 2 more to have 8 (full outer shell)."
"Mg transfers its 2 electrons to O. Now Mg has 12p but only 10e‚Åª, so it's Mg¬≤‚Å∫ (positive 2)."
"O gains 2 electrons. Now it has 8p but 10e‚Åª, so it's O¬≤‚Åª (negative 2)."
"Opposite charges attract! Mg¬≤‚Å∫ and O¬≤‚Åª form a STRONG ionic bond. Formula: MgO."
Key Conceptual Elements Taught:
Ionic Bond Definition:
 Complete electron transfer (not sharing)
Element Pattern:
 Metal + Nonmetal = Ionic compound
Ion Formation Mechanism:
 Metals lose electrons ‚Üí positive ions; nonmetals gain electrons ‚Üí negative ions
Electrostatic Attraction:
 Opposite charges create strong bonding forces
Property Profile:
 High melting point, conducts electricity when dissolved (ions mobile in solution), forms crystalline structures
Digital Tool Integration
Primary Tool:
 PhET "Build an Atom" Simulation (University of Colorado Boulder)
 
Student Actions:
 Construct atoms, manipulate electron counts, observe charge changes
Scaffolding Provided
Common Struggles Addressed:
Confusion: Positive vs. Negative Ion Formation
Heuristic Provided:
 "When an atom LOSES electrons (which are negative), it becomes MORE positive. When it GAINS electrons (negative), it becomes MORE negative. Metals lose ‚Üí become +. Nonmetals gain ‚Üí become ‚àí."
Question: Electrical Conductivity Mechanism
Explanation Provided:
 "In solid form, ions are locked in place in the crystal‚Äîthey can't move. When dissolved, ions separate and can move freely through the water to carry electrical charge. Moving charges = electricity!"
Self-Check Questions (Student Metacognition Support)
Can I explain what an ionic bond is? (electron transfer, not sharing)
Do I know which types of elements form ionic bonds? (metal + nonmetal)
Can I explain why an atom becomes positive or negative? (lose vs. gain electrons)
Can I predict which pair will form an ionic compound?
Do I understand why ionic compounds conduct when dissolved but not when solid?
Success Indicators Listed
Define ionic bonds as electron transfer creating oppositely charged ions
Identify metal + nonmetal combinations
Explain ion formation using electron loss/gain
List ionic compound properties (high melting point, conducts when dissolved, crystalline)
Write chemical formulas by balancing charges (Mg¬≤‚Å∫ + O¬≤‚Åª ‚Üí MgO)
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with embedded questions (20 points)
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Developing Models) - electron transfer representations
 
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A ionic bonding mechanisms
 
CCC Integration:
 CCC-7 (Structure determines Function) - ionic structure ‚Üí properties
Reflection Prompt:
 "What's the ONE key idea about ionic bonding you want to remember? Say it out loud or write it down before moving to Station 2!"
What Was NOT Taught:
Quantitative lattice energy calculations
Detailed crystal structure geometry (beyond "cubic" mention)
Ionic radius trends
Polyatomic ion structures
Electronegativity numerical values
Born-Haber cycles
Solubility rules (specific)
Phase 3: Station 2 - Covalent Bonding (20 points, ~8-10 minutes)
Central Concept:
 Covalent bonds form through electron SHARING
Instructional Focus:
 Nonmetal-nonmetal interactions resulting in molecular formation
Worked Example: Water (H‚ÇÇO) Formation
Step-by-Step Expert Reasoning Provided:
"Hydrogen has 1 valence electron. It needs 1 more to fill its shell (only needs 2 total)."
"Oxygen has 6 valence electrons. It needs 2 more to have 8 (full outer shell)."
"Both are nonmetals, so neither wants to give up electrons. They'll SHARE instead!"
"One H shares its electron with O, and O shares one of its electrons back. That's one bond."
"A second H does the same thing. Now O has 8 electrons (6 + 2 shared), and each H has 2 (1 + 1 shared). Formula: H‚ÇÇO."
Key Conceptual Elements Taught:
Covalent Bond Definition:
 Electron sharing (not transfer)
Element Pattern:
 Nonmetal + Nonmetal = Covalent compound
Molecular Formation:
 Sharing creates neutral molecules (no charged ions)
Bonding Metaphor:
 "Like sharing a blanket" - both atoms hold electrons together
Property Profile:
 Lower melting point, does not conduct electricity, can be solid/liquid/gas
Conceptual Contrast Emphasized
Retrieval from Station 1:
 "What happens in ionic bonding? (Hint: transfer or share?) Keep this in mind‚Äîcovalent bonding is different!"
Scaffolding Provided
Think-Pair-Share Activity:
 
Question:
 "Why do you think covalent compounds don't conduct electricity? Think about it for 30 seconds, then discuss with a partner."
 
Hint Provided:
 "Remember that electricity needs moving charges. Do covalent molecules have charged particles?"
Common Struggles Addressed:
Confusion: Sharing vs. Transfer Distinction
Clarification Provided:
 "Transfer = one atom gives electrons away completely (ionic). Sharing = both atoms hold onto the electrons together, like sharing a blanket (covalent). Neither atom fully loses or gains electrons in covalent bonds."
Question: Why Nonmetals Don't Transfer
Explanation Provided:
 "Nonmetals have high electronegativity‚Äîthey strongly WANT electrons. When two nonmetals meet, both want electrons, so neither will give them up. The compromise? They share!"
Note:
 Electronegativity term introduced but not quantified; used as qualitative concept only
Self-Check Questions
Can I explain what a covalent bond is? (electron sharing, not transfer)
Do I know which types of elements form covalent bonds? (nonmetal + nonmetal)
Can I explain why atoms share instead of transfer?
Can I predict which pair will form a covalent compound?
Do I understand why covalent compounds don't conduct electricity?
Success Indicators Listed
Define covalent bonds as electron sharing between atoms
Identify nonmetal + nonmetal combinations
Explain that sharing creates molecules (not ions)
List covalent compound properties (lower melting point, doesn't conduct, can be gas/liquid/solid)
Describe electron sharing in simple molecules (H‚ÇÇO, CO‚ÇÇ)
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with embedded questions (20 points)
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Developing Models) - electron sharing representations
 
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A covalent bonding mechanisms
 
CCC Integration:
 CCC-7 - molecular structure ‚Üí properties
Reflection Prompt:
 "How would you explain the difference between ionic and covalent bonding to a younger student? Try it in one sentence!"
What Was NOT Taught:
Multiple bond types (single, double, triple)
Formal charge calculations
Resonance structures
Molecular orbital theory
Quantitative bond energy values
Polar vs. nonpolar covalent distinction (detailed)
VSEPR theory (mentioned in Week 1 but not here)
Hybridization
Phase 4: Station 3 - Comparing Bonds &amp; Predicting Properties (20 points, ~10-12 minutes)
Central Concept:
 Bond type classification enables property prediction
Instructional Focus:
 Systematic comparison and application to phenomenon (salt vs. sugar mystery)
Comprehensive Comparison Table Provided
Feature
Ionic Bonds
Covalent Bonds
Electron behavior
Transfer (complete)
Sharing
Elements involved
Metal + Nonmetal
Nonmetal + Nonmetal
Particles formed
Charged ions (+/‚àí)
Neutral molecules
Melting point
HIGH (400-3000¬∞C)
Lower (often &lt;300¬∞C)
Conductivity
Conducts when dissolved
Does NOT conduct
State at room temp
Usually solid crystals
Can be solid, liquid, or gas
Examples
NaCl, CaO, MgCl‚ÇÇ
H‚ÇÇO, CO‚ÇÇ, C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ
Quantitative Property Ranges:
 Explicit temperature values provided for melting points, establishing empirical basis for "high" vs. "low" classification
Decision Tree Algorithm Provided
Bond Type Identification Protocol:
Look at the elements in the compound
Is there a METAL present?
YES ‚Üí 
IONIC
 (metal + nonmetal = transfer)
NO ‚Üí 
COVALENT
 (nonmetal + nonmetal = sharing)
Supporting Information:
 "Location on periodic table tells you metal vs nonmetal. Metals are on the LEFT, nonmetals on the RIGHT."
Worked Example: Solving Salt vs. Sugar Mystery
Salt (NaCl) Analysis:
Na = metal, Cl = nonmetal ‚Üí IONIC BOND
Prediction: High melting point ‚úì (801¬∞C)
Prediction: Conducts when dissolved ‚úì (ions move)
Sugar (C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ) Analysis:
C, H, O = all nonmetals ‚Üí COVALENT BOND
Prediction: Lower melting point ‚úì (186¬∞C)
Prediction: Doesn't conduct ‚úì (no charged particles)
Synthesis Statement:
 "Mystery solved! The bond type determines EVERYTHING about a compound's properties."
Scaffolding Provided
Common Struggles Addressed:
Confusion: Metal vs. Nonmetal Identification
Strategy Provided:
 "Look at the periodic table! There's a 'staircase' line. Elements to the LEFT are metals (except hydrogen). Elements to the RIGHT are nonmetals. When in doubt, check your periodic table."
Edge Case: H‚ÇÇ Classification
Clarification:
 "H‚ÇÇ is two hydrogen atoms (both nonmetals) sharing electrons. Even though hydrogen is on the left side of the periodic table, it's a nonmetal. Two nonmetals = covalent."
Self-Check Questions
Can I identify if a compound is ionic or covalent just by looking at its formula?
Can I explain WHY ionic compounds have high melting points?
Can I explain WHY covalent compounds don't conduct electricity?
Can I predict at least 3 properties of an unknown compound based on its bond type?
Do I understand the salt vs sugar mystery now?
Success Indicators Listed
Classify any compound as ionic or covalent using element types
List and explain key differences between ionic and covalent bonds
Predict melting point, conductivity, and state based on bond type
Connect bond type to properties (e.g., "high melting point because strong ionic attractions")
Solve real-world puzzles using bond type knowledge
Assessment Mechanism:
 Google Form with embedded questions (20 points)
SEP Integration:
 SEP-2 (Using Models) - applying bonding models to predict properties
 
DCI Integration:
 PS1.A - comprehensive bond-property relationships
 
CCC Integration:
 CCC-7 - full structure-function causation
Metacognitive Reflection:
 "Go back to your initial guess about salt vs sugar from the beginning. How has your thinking changed? What surprised you most?"
What Was NOT Taught:
Metallic bonding (third bond type)
Hydrogen bonding as intermolecular force
Network covalent structures (e.g., diamond, graphite mentioned in context but not detailed)
Allotropes concept
Detailed solubility mechanisms
Specific heat capacity differences
Hardness/brittleness explanations beyond general principles
Phase 5: Exit Ticket - Comprehensive Assessment (20 points, ~15-20 minutes)
Assessment Type:
 Summative formative (checks all four learning targets)
Delivery Mechanism:
 Google Form with multi-section structure
Content Sections Specified
Section 1:
 Identify which elements form ionic vs. covalent bonds
Section 2:
 Explain when electrons transfer vs. share and why
Section 3:
 Predict properties (melting point, conductivity) from bond type
Section 4:
 Draw or describe electron behavior in different bond types
Section 5:
 Apply knowledge to real-world scenarios

 Section 1
 
Target 2 (Explain Electron Behavior):
 Section 2

 Section 3
 
Target 4 (Model Bonds):
 Section 4
 
Integrated Application:
 Section 5
Readiness Check Provided
‚òê Identify which elements form ionic vs. covalent bonds?
‚òê Explain when electrons transfer vs. share?
‚òê Predict properties like melting point and conductivity from bond type?
‚òê Draw or describe how electrons behave in different bond types?
Guidance:
 "If you checked all boxes, you're ready! If not, quickly review the relevant station(s)."
Success Criteria for Exit Ticket
Completed all sections
Can explain at least one complete example of both ionic and covalent bonding
Used specific evidence from stations in answers
Reasoning clear and based on electron behavior (transfer vs. sharing)
Metacognitive Reflection Component
Final Reflection Questions:
What was the most challenging concept, and how did you work through it?
What strategy helped you learn best today?
How would you explain ionic vs. covalent bonds to someone who missed class?
Philosophical Statement to Students:
 "Understanding bonding isn't about memorizing‚Äîit's about thinking like a chemist. You can now look at any substance and predict how it will behave based on how its atoms are connected. That's real scientific reasoning!"
Assessment Philosophy Reinforced:
 "This is about understanding, not just getting 'right answers'‚Äîchemists value the thinking process and scientific reasoning. If you need to revise or retake any component, that's completely normal and encouraged! Science is iterative."
Real-World Application Connections
Explicitly Mentioned Career/Application Domains
Pharmaceutical Chemistry:
 Drug molecule design with specific ionic/covalent bonds targeting cellular receptors; dissolution timing control
Materials Science:
 Polymer design with specific bond types for applications (flexible phone screens, bulletproof vests)
Environmental Engineering:
 Pollutant behavior prediction (water solubility vs. solid persistence) based on bonding
Battery Engineering:
 Ionic conductivity exploitation for electric vehicle range improvement
Food Science:
 Texture control (crispy to creamy) through bonding manipulation
Everyday Phenomena Explained
Medicine function (targeting specific cells through molecular bonding)
Battery operation (ionic compound electrical conductivity)
Road salt ice-melting mechanism (ionic vs. covalent property differences)
Diamond hardness vs. graphite softness (both pure carbon with different bonding structures)
Combustibility variation (bond strength determines reactivity)

Chemistry Concepts Introduced
Ionic Bonding:
Electron transfer mechanism
Metal-nonmetal interaction pattern
Ion formation (cation/anion)
Electrostatic attraction
Charge notation (e.g., Mg¬≤‚Å∫, O¬≤‚Åª)
Formula writing by charge balance
Covalent Bonding:
Electron sharing mechanism
Nonmetal-nonmetal interaction pattern
Molecular formation (neutral particles)
Octet rule motivation (qualitative)
Property Prediction:
Melting point trends (ionic: 400-3000¬∞C; covalent: often &lt;300¬∞C)
Electrical conductivity mechanisms (ionic: mobile ions; covalent: no charged particles)
State of matter at room temperature
Crystal structure formation (ionic)
Classification Systems:
Decision tree for bond type identification
Element position on periodic table (metal vs. nonmetal)
Compound formula analysis

Comparison Table: Ionic vs. Covalent (Complete Curricular Content)
Electron Behavior
Ionic:
 Complete transfer from metal to nonmetal
Covalent:
 Sharing between nonmetals
Element Combinations
Ionic:
 Metal + Nonmetal
Covalent:
 Nonmetal + Nonmetal
Particles Formed
Ionic:
 Charged ions (cations +, anions ‚àí)
Covalent:
 Neutral molecules
Melting Point
Ionic:
 High (400-3000¬∞C range specified)
Covalent:
 Lower (often &lt;300¬∞C)
Electrical Conductivity
Ionic:
 Conducts when dissolved (ions mobile in solution); does not conduct as solid (ions fixed in lattice)
Covalent:
 Does not conduct (no charged particles)
State at Room Temperature
Ionic:
 Usually solid crystals
Covalent:
 Can be solid, liquid, or gas
Examples Provided
Ionic:
 NaCl, CaO, MgCl‚ÇÇ
Covalent:
 H‚ÇÇO, CO‚ÇÇ, C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ
Worked Examples Content Analysis
Example 1: Magnesium Oxide (MgO) - Ionic Bonding
Reasoning Chain:
Element classification (Mg = metal, O = nonmetal)
Valence electron inventory (Mg: 2, O: 6)
Energy minimization logic (Mg easier to lose 2 than gain 6)
Transfer description (Mg gives 2e‚Åª to O)
Charge calculation (Mg: 12p, 10e‚Åª ‚Üí Mg¬≤‚Å∫; O: 8p, 10e‚Åª ‚Üí O¬≤‚Åª)
Electrostatic attraction explanation
Formula determination (Mg¬≤‚Å∫ + O¬≤‚Åª ‚Üí MgO)
Pedagogical Note:
 "Notice: Transfer creates ions with opposite charges. This is KEY to understanding ionic bonding!"
Example 2: Water (H‚ÇÇO) - Covalent Bonding
Reasoning Chain:
Element classification (H, O both nonmetals)
Valence electron inventory (H: 1, O: 6)
Octet rule application (H needs 2 total, O needs 8)
Sharing decision (neither wants to give up electrons)
First bond formation (H shares 1 with O, O shares 1 back)
Second bond formation (second H repeats process)
Electron count verification (O: 6 + 2 shared = 8; each H: 1 + 1 shared = 2)
Formula determination (H‚ÇÇO)
Pedagogical Note:
 "Notice: Sharing means NO charged ions form. This is KEY to understanding covalent bonding!"
Example 3: Salt vs. Sugar Mystery Resolution
Comparative Analysis:
Salt (NaCl):
 Na (metal) + Cl (nonmetal) ‚Üí Ionic ‚Üí High MP (801¬∞C), conducts dissolved
Sugar (C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ):
 C, H, O (all nonmetals) ‚Üí Covalent ‚Üí Lower MP (186¬∞C), doesn't conduct
Synthesis:
 "Mystery solved! The bond type determines EVERYTHING about a compound's properties."
Metacognitive Supports Provided
Reflection Prompts (Distributed Throughout)
Pre-Instruction:
 "Before we start, what's your current best guess for why salt and sugar behave differently? Write it down somewhere‚Äîwe'll come back to it at the end to see how your thinking evolved!"
Post-Station 1:
 "What's the ONE key idea about ionic bonding you want to remember? Say it out loud or write it down before moving to Station 2!"
Post-Station 2:
 "How would you explain the difference between ionic and covalent bonding to a younger student? Try it in one sentence!"
Post-Station 3:
 "Go back to your initial guess about salt vs sugar from the beginning. How has your thinking changed? What surprised you most?"
Post-Exit Ticket:
 Three-part reflection on challenge, strategy, and explanation ability

 Week 2: Chemical Bonding - The Electron Game

Technical Curricular Documentation: Week 3 Lesson Plan
Course Identification
Unit Title:
 Week 3: Chemical Reactions - Bonds Breaking &amp; Forming
 
Grade Level:
 7th Grade Science
 
Instructional Time:
 ~98 minutes total (distributed across 75-minute class periods)
 
Total Point Value:
 90 points
 
Institution:
 Kairos Academies
 
Instructor:
 Louis Rosche
 
Cycle Designation:
 Cycle 2, Week 3
NGSS Three-Dimensional Learning Framework
Primary Performance Expectations
MS-PS1-2 (Depth of Knowledge Level 3)
Performance Expectation:
 Analyze and interpret data on the properties of substances before and after the substances interact to determine if a chemical reaction has occurred.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can look at evidence before and after a change to figure out if a chemical reaction happened and explain why new substances formed using observable clues."
Emphasis:
 Primary focus of lesson; directly addressed in Hook, Station 1, Station 3, and Exit Ticket
Alignment Components:
DCI:
 PS1.B Chemical Reactions
SEP:
 SEP-4 Analyzing and Interpreting Data (Elements 1, 3)
CCC:
 CCC-1 Patterns (Element 2)
MS-PS1-5 (Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
Performance Expectation:
 Develop and use a model to describe how the total number of atoms does not change in a chemical reaction and thus mass is conserved.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can track atoms through chemical reactions and prove that no atoms are created or destroyed, so the total mass stays exactly the same."
Emphasis:
 Primary focus of Station 2; reinforced throughout lesson
Alignment Components:
DCI:
 PS1.B Chemical Reactions
SEP:
 SEP-2 Developing and Using Models (Element 2)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Energy and Matter (Element 1)
Connected Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-4 (Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
Performance Expectation:
 Develop a model that predicts and describes changes in particle motion, temperature, and state of a pure substance when thermal energy is added or removed.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can explain how particles move differently at different temperatures and how that affects chemical reactions and reaction rates."
Emphasis:
 Connected through Station 3 (temperature effects on reaction rates); supports understanding of collision theory
Alignment Components:
DCI:
 PS1.A Structure and Properties of Matter
SEP:
 SEP-2 Developing and Using Models (Element 2)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Energy and Matter (Element 2)
Disciplinary Core Ideas (DCI)
PS1.B: Chemical Reactions (Primary)
Focus Areas:
Evidence of chemical reactions (color change, gas production, temperature change, light production)
Conservation of atoms and mass in chemical reactions
Distinction between physical and chemical changes at molecular level
Conceptual Anchors:
Chemical reactions involve bond breaking and bond forming
Atoms are rearranged but never created or destroyed
Observable evidence indicates new substances with different properties have formed
PS1.A: Structure and Properties of Matter (Connected)
Focus Areas:
Particle motion and temperature relationships
Collision frequency and energy as determinants of reaction rate
Conceptual Connection:
 Temperature affects particle kinetic energy, which influences collision frequency and energy, thereby affecting reaction rates
Science and Engineering Practices (SEP)
SEP-4: Analyzing and Interpreting Data (Primary)
Element 1:
 Construct, analyze, and/or interpret graphical displays of data to identify linear and nonlinear relationships
Application in Week 3:
Analyzing observable changes before/after reactions (color, temperature, gas production, light)
Interpreting evidence patterns to determine if chemical reactions occurred
Comparing properties of reactants vs. products
Element 3:
 Analyze and interpret data to determine similarities and differences in findings
Application in Week 3:
Distinguishing physical changes (same substance) from chemical changes (new substances)
Comparing multiple reactions to identify common evidence patterns
SEP-2: Developing and Using Models (Primary)
Element 2:
 Develop and/or use a model to predict and/or describe phenomena
Application in Week 3:
Modeling conservation of mass through atom counting in chemical equations
Using balanced equations to demonstrate atom conservation
Modeling particle collisions to explain reaction rate factors
Describing closed vs. open systems
Crosscutting Concepts (CCC)
CCC-1: Patterns (Primary)
Element 2:
 Patterns in rates of change and other numerical relationships can provide information about natural systems
Application in Week 3:
Recognizing four consistent evidence types across diverse chemical reactions
Identifying patterns in how temperature, surface area, concentration, and catalysts affect reaction rates
Observing that certain factor changes predictably increase/decrease reaction rates
CCC-5: Energy and Matter (Primary)
Element 1:
 Matter is conserved because atoms are conserved in physical and chemical processes
Application in Week 3:
Law of Conservation of Mass as fundamental principle
Tracking atoms through chemical equations
Understanding closed vs. open systems and apparent vs. actual mass changes
Element 2:
 The transfer of energy can be tracked as energy flows through a designed or natural system
Application in Week 3:
Exothermic reactions (energy released)
Endothermic reactions (energy absorbed)
Activation energy as energy barrier to reaction initiation
Energy changes as evidence of chemical reactions
Instructional Objectives (Standards-Based Learning Targets)
Target 1: Identify Evidence of Chemical Reactions (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Recognize and explain the four types of evidence that show a chemical reaction has occurred: color change, gas production (bubbles), temperature change (exothermic/endothermic), and light production.
Success Criteria:
Name all four evidence types
Explain why each type indicates new substances formed
Connect observable evidence to molecular-level bond breaking/forming
Identify evidence in real-world and laboratory scenarios
Example Success Statement:
 "When vinegar reacts with baking soda, I observe gas production (CO‚ÇÇ bubbles forming), temperature decrease (endothermic - feels cold), and fizzing sounds. These tell me bonds are breaking in the reactants and new bonds are forming to create different products (sodium acetate, water, and carbon dioxide gas)."
Assessment Methods:
Station 1 form responses (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 1 (Target 1 specific questions)
Application to anchor phenomenon (burning candle)
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-2 (analyzing data on properties before/after interaction)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing and Interpreting Data)
CCC:
 CCC-1 (Patterns in evidence types)
Target 2: Explain Conservation of Mass (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Use the Law of Conservation of Mass to explain why mass stays constant during reactions in closed systems; understand that atoms are rearranged but never created or destroyed.
Success Criteria:
State Law of Conservation of Mass
Explain difference between closed and open systems
Predict mass changes (or lack thereof) in different scenarios
Count atoms on each side of chemical equation
Explain why balanced equations demonstrate conservation
Example Success Statement:
 "In a closed system like a sealed balloon, mass before = mass after because atoms don't disappear‚Äîthey just rearrange into new combinations. That's why 2H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 2H‚ÇÇO is balanced: there are 4 hydrogen atoms and 2 oxygen atoms on each side. The atoms reorganize but the total count stays the same, so mass is conserved."
Assessment Methods:
Station 2 form responses (20 points)
Virtual balloon experiment analysis
Exit Ticket Section 2 (conservation questions)
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-5 (modeling atom conservation)
SEP:
 SEP-2 (Developing and Using Models)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Element 1 (Matter conservation)
Target 3: Analyze Factors Affecting Reaction Rates (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Explain how temperature, surface area, concentration, and catalysts affect how quickly chemical reactions happen by influencing the frequency and energy of particle collisions.
Success Criteria:
Name all four factors affecting reaction rates
Explain mechanism for each factor using collision theory
Predict whether changes will speed up or slow down reactions
Apply understanding to real-world scenarios (food storage, medicine, industrial processes)
Define and explain activation energy and catalyst function
Example Success Statement:
 "Increasing temperature makes particles move faster ‚Üí more frequent and more energetic collisions ‚Üí higher chance of breaking bonds ‚Üí faster reaction. That's why food spoils slower in the refrigerator‚Äîcold temperatures slow down the chemical reactions that break down food molecules."
Assessment Methods:
Station 3 form responses (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 3 (reaction rate scenarios)
Real-world application questions
Alignment:
Connected PE:
 MS-PS1-4 (particle motion and temperature)
SEP:
 SEP-2 (Using Models to predict)
CCC:
 CCC-1 (Patterns in rates of change)
Target 4: Distinguish Physical vs Chemical Changes (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Determine whether a change is physical (same substance, different form - no new bonds) or chemical (new substances formed with different properties - bonds break and form) and explain reasoning with molecular-level evidence.
Success Criteria:
Define physical change (same substance, different arrangement)
Define chemical change (new substances, different properties)
Describe what happens at molecular level in each type
Classify real-world examples correctly with justification
Explain reversibility differences
Example Success Statement:
 "Ice melting is physical‚Äîthe molecules are still H‚ÇÇO, they're just moving faster and farther apart. No bonds between hydrogen and oxygen atoms break. Burning wood is chemical‚Äîwood molecules (cellulose) break apart completely and their atoms recombine with oxygen atoms from air to form entirely different substances: CO‚ÇÇ and H‚ÇÇO with completely different properties than wood."
Assessment Methods:
Station 1 Section 4 responses
Exit Ticket Section 4 (classification tasks)
Comparison table completion
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-2 (analyzing properties before/after)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing and Interpreting Data)
CCC:
 CCC-1 (Patterns distinguishing change types)
Phenomenological Framing
Anchor Phenomenon: The Burning Candle Mystery
Observable Phenomenon:
Solid wax candle burns, becoming progressively shorter
Flame glows bright, producing light and heat
Wax appears to "disappear" - no visible residue proportional to consumed wax
Candle weighs less after burning
Surrounding air unchanged in appearance
Driving Questions:
Primary:
 "If atoms can't be created or destroyed, where do ALL the wax atoms go?"
Secondary:
 "Why does the candle weigh less if no atoms disappeared?"
Cognitive Dissonance Created:
Observation 1:
 Candle gets smaller (mass appears to decrease)
Observation 2:
 Law of Conservation of Mass states atoms can't be destroyed
Tension:
 Where did the wax atoms go if they can't be destroyed?
Pedagogical Purpose:
Establishes need for understanding chemical reactions at molecular level
Motivates learning about evidence of reactions (light, heat production)
Requires understanding of conservation of mass in open systems
Necessitates distinguishing reactants (wax, oxygen) from products (CO‚ÇÇ, H‚ÇÇO)
Phenomenon Resolution Strategy:
Week 3 Progression:
Hook (Beginning):
 Students record initial hypotheses about wax disappearance
Station 1:
 Learn to identify reaction evidence (light, heat = chemical change indicators)
Station 2:
 Understand mass conservation in closed vs. open systems
Station 3:
 (Implicit) Combustion as fast exothermic reaction
Synthesis (End):
 Complete CER explanation with molecular-level reasoning
Complete Solution Provided at Week End:
Claim:
 When a candle burns, the wax doesn't disappear‚Äîit undergoes a chemical reaction with oxygen from the air, producing carbon dioxide gas and water vapor that escape into the surrounding air.
Evidence:
Multiple pieces of evidence for chemical reaction: (1) Light produced (flame), (2) Heat released (exothermic‚Äîmeasurable temperature increase), (3) New gases form (CO‚ÇÇ and H‚ÇÇO vapor‚Äîinvisible), (4) Products are completely different substances than reactants
Chemical equation: C‚ÇÇ‚ÇÖH‚ÇÖ‚ÇÇ + 38O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 25CO‚ÇÇ + 26H‚ÇÇO (wax approximation)
Reasoning:
 At the molecular level, hydrocarbon molecules in wax (like C‚ÇÇ‚ÇÖH‚ÇÖ‚ÇÇ) react with oxygen molecules (O‚ÇÇ) from air. The bonds in these molecules break, atoms rearrange, and new bonds form to create CO‚ÇÇ and H‚ÇÇO. Although the candle appears smaller and wax seems to "disappear," mass is conserved‚Äîevery single atom from wax and oxygen is now part of carbon dioxide and water vapor in the air. This is an open system (gases escape), so the measured mass of the candle decreases, but total mass of the universe stays constant. That's the Law of Conservation of Mass in action!
Phenomenon Type Classification:
Macroscopic Observation:
 Candle burning, wax disappearing
Molecular-Level Explanation Required:
 Bond breaking, atom rearrangement, bond forming
System Type:
 Open system (complicates conservation understanding)
Reaction Type:
 Combustion (exothermic, rapid, produces light/heat)

Week 3 Application:
 Chemical reactions involve breaking existing bonds (ionic or covalent) and forming new bonds
Bridging Language:
 "You learned about the bonds that hold atoms together (both ionic and covalent). Week 3 (This Week): You're investigating what happens when those bonds BREAK and new ones FORM‚Äîthat's a chemical reaction! It's like taking Lego structures apart and rebuilding them into completely different things."

Phase 2: Station 1 - Evidence Lab (20 points, ~25 minutes)
Central Concept
Chemical reactions produce observable evidence: color change, gas production, temperature change, light production. These evidence types result from bond breaking and new bond formation creating substances with different properties.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Targets Addressed:
Target 1: Identify Evidence of Chemical Reactions (primary)
Target 4: Distinguish Physical vs Chemical Changes (primary)
Core Content:
Four types of chemical reaction evidence
Why each evidence type indicates new substances formed
Virtual lab investigation of four reactions
Physical vs. chemical change classification
Presentation Support
Embedded Video:
Title: "Chemical vs Physical Changes explained"
URL: 
https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1j1wKlAm2s7HBlGtJcF8g4j4pJJC3nZo8b-BaH0hl4ds/embed?start=false&amp;loop=false&amp;delayms=60000&amp;slide=id.g3996c67afb6_0_4&amp;rm=minimal
Viewing Recommendation: "View BEFORE starting the station"
Purpose: Visual explanation of evidence types and change distinctions
Station Structure
Form Organization (4 Sections):
Section 1:
 Introduction (email collection)
Section 2:
 Types of Chemical Evidence (color, gas, temperature, light)
Section 3:
 Virtual Lab Investigation (4 mystery reactions)
Section 4:
 Physical vs Chemical Changes (practice distinguishing)
Evidence Type #1: Color Change
Definition:
 When substances react, they often change color because NEW substances with different properties form
Example Provided:
 Iron (gray) + oxygen ‚Üí rust (reddish-brown)
Molecular-Level Explanation:
 Different molecules interact with light differently, producing different colors
Teaching Emphasis:
 Permanent color change (not reversible like mixing food coloring in water) indicates new substance
Evidence Type #2: Gas Production
Definition:
 Bubbles or gas forming shows atoms rearranging into new molecules
Example Provided:
 Baking soda + vinegar produces CO‚ÇÇ gas (bubbles)
Molecular-Level Explanation:
 New gas molecules form from rearrangement of reactant atoms
Contrast with Physical:
 NOT the same as boiling water (H‚ÇÇO stays H‚ÇÇO, just changes state)
Key Distinction Taught:
 "In TRUE chemical gas production (like baking soda + vinegar), NEW gas molecules form that didn't exist before. The CO‚ÇÇ gas comes from rearranging atoms, not just spreading out the same molecules."
Evidence Type #3: Temperature Change
Exothermic Reactions:
Definition: Reaction releases energy (usually as heat or light)
Observable: Feels warmer, temperature increases
Examples: Hand warmers, burning candles, fireworks
Mechanism: New bonds formed are MORE stable than old bonds broken ‚Üí extra energy escapes as heat
Endothermic Reactions:
Definition: Reaction absorbs energy from surroundings
Observable: Feels colder, temperature decreases
Examples: Instant cold packs, photosynthesis
Mechanism: New bonds need MORE energy than old bonds released ‚Üí pulls energy from surroundings
Energy Flow Explanation:
 "Chemical reactions involve energy changes. Exothermic reactions RELEASE energy (feel hot) because the new bonds formed are MORE stable than the old bonds broken‚Äîextra energy escapes as heat. Endothermic reactions ABSORB energy (feel cold) because the new bonds need MORE energy than the old bonds released‚Äîthey pull energy from the surroundings. Both are chemical reactions, just with opposite energy flows!"
Evidence Type #4: Light Production
Definition:
 Some reactions release energy as light (like flames, glow sticks, bioluminescence)
Examples Provided:
Burning candle flame
Fireflies
Fireworks
Molecular-Level Explanation:
 Energy released from bond formation emitted as photons (light particles)
Virtual Lab Investigation
Four Mystery Reactions Analyzed:
Reaction A: Steel Wool + Oxygen ‚Üí Rust
Observable Evidence: Color change (gray/silver ‚Üí orange-brown), temperature increase (exothermic)
Analysis Focus: Multiple evidence types, bond breaking/forming explanation
Reaction B: Baking Soda + Vinegar ‚Üí Bubbles
Observable Evidence: Gas production (CO‚ÇÇ bubbles), temperature decrease (endothermic), fizzing sound
Analysis Focus: Gas as evidence, energy absorption
Reaction C: Hydrogen Peroxide ‚Üí Water + Oxygen
Observable Evidence: Gas production (oxygen bubbles), possible catalyst (enzyme) presence
Analysis Focus: Decomposition reaction, catalyst role (if presented)
Reaction D: Burning Sugar ‚Üí Carbon + Water Vapor
Observable Evidence: Color change (white ‚Üí black char), gas production (water vapor), light production (flame), temperature increase (exothermic)
Analysis Focus: Combustion, multiple evidence types
Student Task for Each Reaction:
Identify all observable evidence types
Explain why each evidence type indicates chemical reaction
Describe what happened at molecular level (bonds breaking, atoms rearranging, new bonds forming)
Worked Example: Steel Wool + Oxygen Analysis
Expert Chemist Reasoning Modeled:
Observation 1:
 "The steel wool (gray/silver) turned orange-brown when exposed to air." ‚Üí Evidence: 
Color change
 indicates new substances formed
Observation 2:
 "The steel wool feels warm to the touch." ‚Üí Evidence: 
Temperature increase
 (exothermic reaction - energy released)
Analysis:
 "The iron (Fe) atoms in steel wool reacted with oxygen (O‚ÇÇ) molecules from air." ‚Üí Old bonds broke: Fe-Fe bonds in metal, O=O bonds in oxygen gas ‚Üí New bonds formed: Fe-O bonds creating iron oxide (rust)
Conclusion:
 "This is a 
chemical change
 because we have new substances (rust) with different properties than the original materials (iron and oxygen)."
Pedagogical Note:
 "Notice: The chemist identified TWO types of evidence and connected them to what happened at the molecular level (bonds breaking and forming)."
Physical vs. Chemical Change Distinction
Physical Change Definition:
Change in form or state, but substance itself stays the same
No new substances formed
Molecular identity unchanged (bonds between atoms stay intact)
Often reversible
Examples Provided:
Ice melting into water (still H‚ÇÇO molecules)
Cutting paper (still cellulose molecules)
Dissolving sugar in water (sugar molecules separate but don't change)
Chemical Change Definition:
New substances formed with different properties
Bonds break and new bonds form
Molecular identity changes
Usually irreversible (or requires different reaction to reverse)
Examples Provided:
Burning wood ‚Üí ash + CO‚ÇÇ + H‚ÇÇO (cellulose destroyed, new substances formed)
Iron rusting ‚Üí iron oxide (different molecule, different properties)
Cooking egg ‚Üí protein denaturation (structure permanently altered)
Molecular-Level Comparison:
Aspect
Physical Change
Chemical Change
Molecular identity
Same molecules
Different molecules
Chemical bonds
Intact
Break and reform
Reversibility
Often easy
Usually difficult/impossible
Energy changes
Small (state changes)
Large (bond energy)
Properties
Same substance, different form
Different substances, different properties
Scaffolding Provided
Vocabulary Section (Expandable):
 Contains detailed definitions with examples for:
Chemical Reaction
Physical Change
Evidence #1-4 (color, gas, temperature, light)
Exothermic Reaction
Endothermic Reaction
Format:
 Click-to-expand details box with color-coded borders
Common Struggles Section:
Struggle #1:
 "How do I know if a color change is chemical or just physical?"
Clarification:
 "If the color change is reversible and no new substance forms, it's physical (like food coloring in water). If the color change is permanent and you can't easily reverse it, it's probably chemical (like rust forming or fruit browning). Look for OTHER evidence too‚Äîdoes it also produce gas, heat, or light? Multiple evidence types = definitely chemical!"
Struggle #2:
 "Is boiling water a chemical change because it produces gas (steam)?"
Clarification:
 "No! This is a common misconception. Boiling is physical because the H‚ÇÇO molecules stay H‚ÇÇO‚Äîthey just move faster and spread out. The steam can condense back into liquid water. In a TRUE chemical gas production (like baking soda + vinegar), NEW gas molecules form that didn't exist before. The CO‚ÇÇ gas comes from rearranging atoms, not just spreading out the same molecules."
Struggle #3:
 "Why do some reactions feel cold? Isn't chemical energy always heat?"
Clarification:
 [Full energy flow explanation provided earlier]
Struggle #4:
 "Can a physical change produce heat? I've felt ice get warmer as it melts."
Clarification:
 "Yes, but there's a key difference! In phase changes (physical), heat moves INTO or OUT OF the substance to change its state, but no bonds break. The molecules are the same before and after. In chemical reactions, heat is PRODUCED or CONSUMED because bonds are breaking and forming. If you see temperature change WITH other evidence (color change, gas production), it's definitely chemical."
Struggle #5:
 "What if I only see ONE type of evidence? Is it still chemical?"
Clarification:
 "Maybe! Some chemical reactions only show one obvious sign. For example, rust forming shows mainly color change over time. The key question: 'Did a NEW substance with DIFFERENT properties form?' If yes, it's chemical even with just one evidence type. But multiple evidence types make you MORE confident it's chemical. One evidence type = might be chemical. Multiple evidence types = definitely chemical!"
Self-Check Questions
Pre-Submission Checklist:
‚òê Can I name all FOUR types of chemical reaction evidence?
‚òê Can I explain why each type of evidence indicates NEW substances formed?
‚òê Do I understand the difference between physical (same substance) and chemical (new substances)?
‚òê Can I describe what happens at the molecular level (bonds breaking, atoms rearranging, new bonds forming)?
‚òê Can I apply my knowledge to classify real-world examples correctly?
Success Indicators
You're successful when you can:
Identify all four types of evidence (color change, gas production, temperature change, light production)
Explain why each type of evidence indicates a new substance has formed
Distinguish between physical changes (same substance) and chemical changes (new substances)
Describe what happens at the molecular level during reactions (bonds breaking, atoms rearranging, new bonds forming)
Apply your knowledge to classify real-world examples with confidence

Phase 3: Station 2 - Conservation of Mass (20 points, ~25 minutes)
Central Concept
In chemical reactions, atoms are rearranged but never created or destroyed; total mass before reaction equals total mass after reaction. This principle is demonstrated through balanced chemical equations where atom counts on both sides are equal.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Target Addressed:
Target 2: Explain Conservation of Mass (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Reinforces Target 1 (evidence interpretation in conservation context)
Prepares for Target 3 (rates don't change conservation principle)
Core Content:
Law of Conservation of Mass
Closed vs. Open Systems
Virtual mass measurement experiment
Balancing chemical equations as conservation evidence
Presentation Support
Embedded Video:
Title: "Conservation of Mass explained"
URL: 
https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1j1wKlAm2s7HBlGtJcF8g4j4pJJC3nZo8b-BaH0hl4ds/embed?start=false&amp;loop=false&amp;delayms=60000&amp;slide=id.g3996c67afb6_0_17&amp;rm=minimal
Viewing Recommendation: "View BEFORE starting the station"
Purpose: Visual explanation of atom conservation and system types
Station Structure
Form Organization (3 Sections):
Section 1:
 The Law of Conservation of Mass (why mass stays constant)
Section 2:
 Virtual Mass Measurement Experiment (vinegar + baking soda in balloon)
Section 3:
 Balancing Chemical Equations (tracking atoms)
Law of Conservation of Mass
Formal Statement:
 In a chemical reaction, atoms are rearranged but never created or destroyed. Total mass before = total mass after.
Historical Attribution:
 Discovered by Antoine Lavoisier in 1789
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "Matter can't be created or destroyed in chemical reactions‚Äîit just gets rearranged into different combinations."
Molecular-Level Explanation:
Chemical reactions involve breaking bonds and forming new bonds
The ATOMS themselves are not created or destroyed
Same atoms exist before and after, just in different arrangements
Because the atoms stay the same, the total mass stays the same
Quantitative Principle:
 If you could capture and weigh everything before and after a reaction (including all gases), the mass would be identical
Closed vs. Open Systems
Closed System Definition:
System where no atoms can enter or leave
Example: Sealed balloon, sealed container, capped bottle
Conservation Observable: Mass measurement on scale stays constant throughout reaction
Example Provided: "Baking soda + vinegar in a sealed balloon‚Äîgas can't escape so mass stays same"
Open System Definition:
System where atoms can enter or leave
Example: Open beaker, burning candle in air, rusting nail outdoors
Conservation Observable: TOTAL universe mass conserved, but MEASURED mass of system may change
Example Provided: "Burning candle‚Äîgases escape so measured mass appears to decrease"
Critical Distinction Emphasized:
 "Mass is ALWAYS conserved in the universe, but we can only measure it easily in closed systems! In open systems, stuff escapes or enters, so the mass we measure might change‚Äîbut if we could capture EVERYTHING, it would still be conserved."
Pedagogical Clarification (Common Struggle):
 "If mass is conserved, why does a burning candle weigh less? The candle weighs less because it's an OPEN system! The carbon dioxide gas and water vapor escape into the air around you. If you could capture ALL the gases and weigh them along with the remaining candle and ashes, the total mass would be exactly the same as the original candle + the oxygen from air that reacted with it. Mass IS conserved‚Äîyou just can't see the gas products that left!"
Virtual Mass Measurement Experiment
Experimental Setup Described:
Materials (Virtual):
Balloon
Bottle
Baking soda (NaHCO‚ÇÉ)
Vinegar (acetic acid, CH‚ÇÉCOOH)
Scale
Procedure:
Put baking soda in balloon
Put vinegar in bottle
Attach balloon to bottle (don't mix yet)
Measure total mass: 
85 grams
Lift balloon so baking soda falls into vinegar
Reaction occurs: CO‚ÇÇ gas fills balloon
Observe: Balloon inflates, fizzing/bubbling visible
Student Prediction Question:
 "What will the mass be after the reaction? Why?"
Guiding Prompt:
 "Think about it: Is this a closed system or open system? Can atoms escape?"
Correct Answer with Reasoning:
 
Mass after reaction: 85 grams (unchanged)
Reasoning:
This is a CLOSED system‚Äîballoon is sealed on bottle
CO‚ÇÇ gas produced but trapped inside balloon
No atoms can escape to surroundings
All atoms that were in baking soda + vinegar are now in products (sodium acetate + water + CO‚ÇÇ)
Same atoms = same mass
Law of Conservation of Mass demonstrated
Alternative Scenario (Open System):
 "If we did this in an open beaker without the balloon, the CO‚ÇÇ gas would escape into the air. We would measure LESS mass after the reaction‚Äîbut that's only because the gas left! The total mass of (remaining liquid + escaped CO‚ÇÇ) would still equal the original mass."
Chemical Equations and Atom Conservation
Chemical Equation Definition:
Representation showing reactants transforming into products
Format: Reactants ‚Üí Products
Example: H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí H‚ÇÇO (unbalanced)
Reactants:
 Starting substances (left side of arrow)
Products:
 New substances formed (right side of arrow)
Balanced Equation Definition:
Chemical equation where the number of each type of atom is equal on both sides
Demonstrates Law of Conservation of Mass
Coefficients (big numbers) show how many molecules, subscripts (small numbers) show atoms in each molecule
Why Balance Equations:
 "Balancing equations PROVES conservation of mass! An unbalanced equation suggests atoms appeared or disappeared, which violates the law. Balanced equations show that every single atom on the left (reactants) ends up somewhere on the right (products). It's like a detailed receipt showing where every ingredient went in a recipe. Scientists need balanced equations to know exact amounts needed for reactions."
Worked Example: Balancing H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí H‚ÇÇO
Step-by-Step Expert Balancing Process:
Step 1: Write the unbalanced equation
H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí H‚ÇÇO
Step 2: Count atoms on each side
Left side: 2 H atoms, 2 O atoms
Right side: 2 H atoms, 1 O atom
‚ùå NOT BALANCED!
 Oxygen doesn't match.
Step 3: Add coefficient to balance oxygen
H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 2H‚ÇÇO
Reasoning:
 "Why 2? We need 2 O atoms on the right to match the 2 O atoms on the left."
Step 4: Recount atoms
Left side: 2 H atoms, 2 O atoms
Right side: 4 H atoms, 2 O atoms
‚ùå STILL NOT BALANCED!
 Now hydrogen doesn't match.
Step 5: Add coefficient to balance hydrogen
2H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 2H‚ÇÇO
Step 6: Final count
Left side: 4 H atoms, 2 O atoms
Right side: 4 H atoms, 2 O atoms
‚úì BALANCED!
 Same number of each atom type on both sides.
Pedagogical Note:
 "Notice: We added coefficients (the big numbers) to make atoms equal on both sides. We NEVER change subscripts (the small numbers) because that would change the actual molecules!"
Critical Rule Emphasized:
 "Can I just change the subscripts to balance an equation? 
NO!
 This is the #1 mistake students make. Subscripts tell you what the molecule IS. H‚ÇÇO is water. If you change it to H‚ÇÇO‚ÇÇ, that's hydrogen peroxide‚Äîa completely different substance! Only add COEFFICIENTS (the big numbers in front) to balance. Coefficients tell you how many molecules, but subscripts tell you which molecule. Never change subscripts!"
Scaffolding Provided
Vocabulary Section (Expandable):
Law of Conservation of Mass (with Lavoisier attribution)
Closed System (with example)
Open System (with example)
Chemical Equation (reactants ‚Üí products format)
Balanced Equation (atom count equality)
Common Struggles Section:
Struggle #1:
 "If mass is conserved, why does a burning candle weigh less?" [Full open vs. closed system explanation provided earlier]
Struggle #2:
 "Can I just change the subscripts to balance an equation?" [Full prohibition with molecular identity explanation provided earlier]
Struggle #3:
 "Why do I need to balance equations? Isn't the arrow enough?" [Full scientific reasoning and recipe analogy provided earlier]
Struggle #4:
 "What's the difference between a closed and open system again?"
Clarification:
 "Think of it like this: CLOSED system = sealed container (nothing enters or leaves). Example: baking soda + vinegar in a sealed balloon‚Äîthe gas fills the balloon but can't escape, so the total mass on a scale stays constant. OPEN system = can interact with surroundings (gases can enter or escape). Example: burning candle in open air‚ÄîCO‚ÇÇ and H‚ÇÇO gases float away, so the measured mass decreases even though total mass in the universe is conserved. Mass is ALWAYS conserved, but we can only measure it in closed systems!"
Struggle #5:
 "How do I know where to start when balancing?"
Strategy:
 "Pro tip: Start with the element that appears in the fewest molecules. Usually balance single elements last (like O‚ÇÇ or H‚ÇÇ). For H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí H‚ÇÇO, oxygen appears in all three spots, so start there! Also, if you have a polyatomic ion (like SO‚ÇÑ¬≤‚Åª) that stays together on both sides, count it as a unit instead of counting S and O separately. This makes balancing much faster!"
Virtual Experiment Analysis
Pre-Experiment Setup:

 Baking soda in balloon, vinegar in bottle, balloon attached but not mixed

 85 grams
System Type:
 Closed (balloon seals system)
During Reaction:
Observable Changes:
 Balloon inflates, fizzing/bubbling, possible temperature change

 NaHCO‚ÇÉ + CH‚ÇÉCOOH ‚Üí NaCH‚ÇÉCOO + H‚ÇÇO + CO‚ÇÇ
Evidence:
 Gas production (CO‚ÇÇ fills balloon)
Post-Experiment Prediction:

Correct Answer:
 85 grams (unchanged)
Reasoning Required:
 Closed system ‚Üí no atom escape ‚Üí mass conserved
