Phase 4: Station 3 - Reaction Rates &amp; Energy (20 points, ~25 minutes)
Central Concept
The speed of chemical reactions (reaction rate) is controlled by four factors‚Äîtemperature, surface area, concentration, and catalysts‚Äîall of which influence the frequency and energy of particle collisions. Reactions require activation energy to begin, which can be provided or lowered by various means.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Target Addressed:
Target 3: Analyze Factors Affecting Reaction Rates (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Reinforces Target 1 (reaction evidence in rate context)
Connects to Target 2 (rates don't affect conservation)
Relates to Target 4 (rate differences between physical/chemical)
Core Content:
Reaction rate definition and spectrum (fast to slow)
Four factors affecting reaction rates
Collision theory as explanatory mechanism
Activation energy and catalysts
Real-world applications
Presentation Support
Embedded Video:
Title: "Factors affecting reaction rates explained"
URL: 
https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1j1wKlAm2s7HBlGtJcF8g4j4pJJC3nZo8b-BaH0hl4ds/embed?start=false&amp;loop=false&amp;delayms=60000&amp;slide=id.g3996c67afb6_0_23&amp;rm=minimal
Viewing Recommendation: "View BEFORE starting the station"
Purpose: Visual explanation of collision theory and rate factors
Station Structure
Form Organization (3 Sections):
Section 1:
 What Are Reaction Rates? (fast vs slow reactions)
Section 2:
 Factors That Affect Reaction Rates (temperature, surface area, concentration, catalysts)
Section 3:
 Energy in Chemical Reactions (activation energy, collision theory)
Reaction Rate Fundamentals
Definition:
 How fast a chemical reaction happens (measured in time)
Spectrum Examples:
Very Fast (milliseconds-seconds):
 Explosions, combustion, neutralization reactions
Fast (seconds-minutes):
 Baking soda + vinegar, bleaching, cooking
Moderate (hours-days):
 Bread rising, concrete hardening, fruit ripening
Slow (days-years):
 Rusting, rock weathering, fossil formation
Observable Indicators of Rate:
How quickly reactants disappear
How quickly products appear
How quickly evidence manifests (color change speed, bubble production rate)
Student-Friendly Concept:
 "Some reactions happen BOOM instantly (fireworks), while others take forever (rust forming). What makes the difference?"
Factor #1: Temperature Effect
General Principle:
 Higher temperature ‚Üí faster particles ‚Üí more collisions ‚Üí faster reaction
Molecular-Level Mechanism:
Temperature Increase ‚Üí Kinetic Energy Increase
Particles move faster at higher temperatures
Average particle velocity increases
Faster Movement ‚Üí More Frequent Collisions
Particles travel farther distances per unit time
Greater probability of particles encountering each other
Faster Movement ‚Üí More Energetic Collisions
Higher kinetic energy means harder collisions
More collisions exceed activation energy threshold
More + Harder Collisions ‚Üí Faster Reaction
More successful bond-breaking events per unit time
Reaction rate increases
Example Provided:
 Food spoilage
Room Temperature (25¬∞C):
 Milk spoils in ~2 hours
Bacteria molecules move FAST
Frequent collisions between bacteria and milk molecules
Energetic collisions break milk molecule bonds
Refrigerator (4¬∞C):
 Milk stays fresh 1-2 weeks
Bacteria molecules move SLOWLY
Fewer collisions
Less energy per collision ‚Üí fewer bonds break
Result: Slower spoilage reaction
Real-World Application:
 "That's why refrigeration works! Lower temperature = slower molecular motion = fewer + less energetic collisions = slower reaction rate."
Quantitative Note (Implicit):
 Generally, reaction rate approximately doubles for every 10¬∞C temperature increase (not explicitly stated numerically but illustrated through examples)
Factor #2: Surface Area Effect
General Principle:
 More surface area exposed ‚Üí more places for reactions ‚Üí faster reaction
Molecular-Level Mechanism:
Reactions Occur at Surfaces
Chemical reactions happen where particles TOUCH
Only surface molecules are accessible to reactants
Large Solid Piece = Low Surface Area
Most molecules buried inside
Only outer layer accessible
Broken into Smaller Pieces = High Surface Area
Same total mass, but more molecules exposed
Same molecules now on multiple surfaces
More Exposed Molecules ‚Üí More Reaction Sites ‚Üí Faster Reaction
Analogy Provided:
 "Think about it like this: Reactions happen where particles TOUCH. A big sugar cube has most of its molecules buried inside where water can't reach them. But if you crush that cube into powder, now ALL those same molecules are exposed on the surface where water CAN reach them. More exposed molecules = more places for reactions = faster dissolving. That's why powdered sugar dissolves almost instantly while a sugar cube takes time!"
Example Provided:
 Sugar dissolution
Sugar Cube:
 Low surface area ‚Üí slow dissolution (~minutes)
Granulated Sugar:
 Medium surface area ‚Üí moderate dissolution (~seconds)
Powdered Sugar:
 High surface area ‚Üí very fast dissolution (~instant)
Real-World Applications:
Chewing food increases digestion rate
Flour dust explosions (high surface area of particles)
Catalytic converters use porous surfaces
Pharmaceutical pills vs. powder forms
Factor #3: Concentration Effect
General Principle:
 Higher concentration ‚Üí more particles in same space ‚Üí more collisions ‚Üí faster reaction
Molecular-Level Mechanism:
Concentration = Particles Per Unit Volume
High concentration: many particles crowded together
Low concentration: few particles spread out
Crowded Particles ‚Üí More Collisions
Shorter average distance between particles
Higher probability of collision per unit time
More Collisions ‚Üí More Reactions ‚Üí Faster Rate
Example Provided:
 Steel wool burning
In Air (21% O‚ÇÇ):
 Burns slowly, glows orange
Lower oxygen concentration
Fewer O‚ÇÇ molecules available for collisions
In Pure Oxygen (100% O‚ÇÇ):
 Bursts into bright flame immediately
Higher oxygen concentration
Many more O‚ÇÇ molecules available
Dramatically faster combustion reaction
Everyday Example:
 Hydrogen peroxide concentration
3% H‚ÇÇO‚ÇÇ (drugstore):
 Slow decomposition, gentle bubbling on wounds
30% H‚ÇÇO‚ÇÇ (industrial):
 Rapid decomposition, vigorous reaction, dangerous
Real-World Applications:
Industrial chemical processes optimize concentrations
Pollution levels affect atmospheric reaction rates
Medicine dosing (concentration affects reaction speed in body)
Factor #4: Catalysts
Definition:
 Substance that speeds up a reaction without being used up by lowering activation energy
Key Characteristics:
Not Consumed:
 Catalyst emerges unchanged after reaction
Lowers Activation Energy:
 Provides alternate pathway requiring less energy
Speeds Up Reaction:
 Increases rate without changing equilibrium
Specific:
 Different catalysts for different reactions
Contrast with Reactants:
 "REACTANTS get used up and transformed into products‚Äîthey're consumed by the reaction. CATALYSTS help the reaction happen faster but come out unchanged at the end‚Äîthey're NOT consumed. Think of a catalyst like a teacher: the teacher helps you learn (makes the reaction faster) but doesn't get 'used up' in the process."
Mechanism Explanation:
 "Catalysts work by providing an ALTERNATE PATHWAY for the reaction‚Äîlike finding a shortcut over a hill instead of climbing straight over the top. The catalyst brings particles together in just the right orientation so bonds can break and form more easily. It's like having a key (catalyst) that makes opening a lock (starting a reaction) much easier than trying to force it open. The lock still opens either way, but the key makes it require less effort (energy)!"
Examples Provided:
Enzymes in Body:
 Digestive enzymes speed up food breakdown; without them, digestion would take weeks
Catalytic Converter:
 Platinum/palladium catalysts convert harmful exhaust gases to less harmful ones
Yeast in Bread:
 Catalyzes sugar breakdown to produce CO‚ÇÇ (bread rises)
Manganese Dioxide + Hydrogen Peroxide:
 Dramatically speeds decomposition to water + oxygen
Visual Representation (Described):
Without Catalyst:
 High activation energy barrier (tall hill to climb)
With Catalyst:
 Lower activation energy barrier (shorter hill with easier path)
Result:
 More particles have sufficient energy to react at given temperature
Collision Theory
Central Principle:
 For a reaction to occur, particles must:
Collide
 (come into contact)
Collide with sufficient ENERGY
 (exceed activation energy)
Collide with proper ORIENTATION
 (correct angle/position)
Why Not All Collisions Cause Reactions:
 "Not all collisions have enough ENERGY to break bonds. Think of it like trying to break a stick: if you tap it gently (low-energy collision), nothing happens. You need to hit it HARD (high-energy collision) to break it. Similarly, particles need to collide with enough energy to overcome the activation energy barrier. That's why temperature matters so much‚Äîhigher temperature means faster-moving particles with MORE energy per collision, so more collisions are successful!"
Three Collision Requirements Explained:
1. Collision Frequency
Affected by: Concentration (more particles = more collisions)
Affected by: Temperature (faster particles = more frequent encounters)
2. Collision Energy
Affected by: Temperature (higher T = higher average kinetic energy)
Must exceed: Activation energy threshold
3. Collision Orientation
Affected by: Catalysts (proper orientation positioning)
Geometry: Reactive sites must align properly
Activation Energy
Definition:
 Energy needed to START a reaction (like pushing a boulder over a hill)
Analogy:
 "You need a spark to start a fire, but then it keeps burning on its own. The spark provides the activation energy to break initial bonds; once started, the reaction's released energy can sustain itself (in exothermic reactions)."
Energy Diagram Components (Described):
Reactants:
 Starting energy level
Activation Energy (Ea):
 Energy barrier to overcome (hill to climb)
Transition State:
 Highest energy point (top of hill)
Products:
 Final energy level
Energy Change (ŒîE):
 Difference between reactants and products
Exothermic:
 Products lower than reactants (energy released)
Endothermic:
 Products higher than reactants (energy absorbed)
Why Activation Energy Matters:
Even exothermic reactions (which release energy overall) need initial energy input
Activation energy determines how easily reaction starts
Catalysts lower activation energy, making reactions start more easily
Temperature provides kinetic energy to overcome activation barrier
Worked Example: Temperature Effect on Food Spoiling
Scenario:
 Milk left on counter vs. milk in refrigerator
At Room Temperature (25¬∞C):
Bacteria molecules move FAST
More frequent collisions between bacteria and milk molecules
More energetic collisions can break bonds
Result:
 Milk spoils in ~2 hours
In Refrigerator (4¬∞C):
Bacteria molecules move SLOWLY
Fewer collisions between bacteria and milk molecules
Less energy per collision means fewer bonds break
Result:
 Milk stays fresh for ~1-2 weeks
Conclusion:
 "Lower temperature = slower molecular motion = fewer + less energetic collisions = slower reaction rate. That's why refrigeration works!"
Pedagogical Note:
 "Notice: The expert connected particle motion at different temperatures to collision frequency and energy, then explained how that affects the reaction rate."
Four Factors Summary Table
Factor
Mechanism
Example
Effect on Rate
Temperature
‚Üë Temp ‚Üí Faster particles ‚Üí More + harder collisions
Food spoils faster at room temp than refrigerated
‚Üë Temp = ‚Üë Rate

‚Üë Surface ‚Üí More exposed molecules ‚Üí More reaction sites
Powdered sugar dissolves faster than cube
‚Üë Surface = ‚Üë Rate

‚Üë Concentration ‚Üí More particles ‚Üí More collisions
Steel wool burns faster in pure O‚ÇÇ than air
‚Üë Concentration = ‚Üë Rate

Enzymes speed digestion; catalytic converter
Catalyst present = ‚Üë Rate
Scaffolding Provided
Vocabulary Section (Expandable):
Reaction Rate
Temperature Effect
Surface Area Effect
Concentration Effect
Catalyst
Activation Energy
Common Struggles Section:
Struggle #1:
 "How does surface area actually speed up reactions?" [Full explanation with sugar cube analogy provided earlier]
Struggle #2:
 "What's the difference between a catalyst and a reactant?" [Full distinction with teacher analogy provided earlier]
Struggle #3:
 "If collisions make reactions happen, why don't all collisions cause reactions?" [Full energy and orientation explanation provided earlier]
Struggle #4:
 "How do catalysts lower activation energy without adding energy?" [Full alternate pathway and key-lock analogy provided earlier]
Struggle #5:
 "Can you have too much of one factor? Like TOO much surface area or TOO high temperature?"
Clarification:
 "Yes! There are practical limits. Surface area: you can only grind something so fine before it's impossible to grind further (individual molecules!). Temperature: if you make it TOO hot, you might decompose your reactants before they can react, or in living systems, you'll denature enzymes (proteins that help reactions) and they'll stop working. That's why fever above 106¬∞F is dangerous‚Äîit starts destroying the enzymes your body needs! There's usually an optimal range for each factor."

 White background boxes with concise mechanism summaries

Pre-Submission Checklist:
‚òê Can I name and explain all FOUR factors that affect reaction rates?
‚òê Can I predict whether a change will speed up or slow down a reaction?
‚òê Do I understand what activation energy is and why it matters?
‚òê Can I describe how catalysts work to speed up reactions?
‚òê Can I connect particle collisions to reaction rates using collision theory?
‚òê Can I apply my knowledge to real-world scenarios (food storage, medicine, digestion)?
Success Indicators
You're successful when you can:
Identify and explain all four factors that affect reaction rates
Predict whether a change will speed up or slow down a reaction
Explain what activation energy is and why it matters
Describe how catalysts work to speed up reactions without being consumed
Apply your knowledge to real-world scenarios (food storage, medicine, digestion, industrial processes)
Connect particle collisions to reaction rates using collision theory

Instructional Purpose

Identify areas needing remediation or extension
Provide data for standards-based grading reporting
Facilitate student metacognitive reflection on growth
Assessment Philosophy Reinforcement:
 "This is about understanding, not just getting 'right answers'‚Äîchemists value the thinking process and scientific reasoning. If you need to revise or retake any component, that's completely normal and encouraged! Science is iterative."

Target 2

All targets integrated

Real-World Application Connections
Explicitly Mentioned Career/Application Domains
1. Pharmaceutical Chemistry
Drug molecule design with specific reaction rates for controlled release
Understanding side effects through unwanted chemical reactions
Preservative systems that slow degradation reactions
2. Chemical Engineering
Industrial process optimization through reaction rate control
Temperature, pressure, and catalyst selection for maximum efficiency
Large-scale production of plastics, medicines, materials
3. Environmental Science
Understanding pollution breakdown through chemical reactions
Cleanup methods using catalysts to speed decomposition
Preventing contamination through reaction rate control
4. Food Science
Preservation techniques (refrigeration, freezing, vacuum-sealing) that slow spoilage reactions
Cooking processes optimized for desired reaction rates
Food additives that prevent or slow oxidation reactions
5. Forensic Science
Analyzing chemical reactions at crime scenes
Determining time of death through decomposition rates
Explosive residue analysis (combustion reactions)
Blood chemistry analysis
6. Materials Science
Creating new substances through designed chemical reactions
Polymer synthesis for specific applications
Alloy formation and composite material creation
7. Biochemistry
Millions of chemical reactions in living cells
Enzyme function as biological catalysts
Developing treatments for diseases by targeting specific reactions
8. Battery Engineering
Designing reversible chemical reactions for energy storage
Optimizing reaction rates for fast charging
Temperature management in electric vehicles
9. Fire Science
Understanding combustion reactions
Fire suppression techniques (removing heat, oxygen, or fuel)
Controlled burns and fire prevention
10. Medicine/Pharmacology
Controlling reaction rates for proper medicine timing
Understanding metabolism as series of chemical reactions
Designing drugs that work at specific rates in body
Everyday Phenomena Explained
Biological Systems:
Cellular Respiration:
 Chemical reaction breaking down food into energy
Evidence: Heat production (exothermic), gas production (CO‚ÇÇ exhaled)
Rate factors: Temperature (fever speeds metabolism), enzymes as catalysts
Digestion:
 Enzyme-catalyzed breakdown of food molecules
Rate factors: Temperature, surface area (chewing), enzyme concentration
Photosynthesis:
 Plant chemical reaction converting CO‚ÇÇ + H‚ÇÇO + light ‚Üí glucose + O‚ÇÇ
Evidence: Gas production (O‚ÇÇ), endothermic (requires light energy)
Household Chemistry:
Cooking:
 Temperature control for desired reaction rates
High heat ‚Üí fast reactions (searing, caramelization)
Low heat ‚Üí slow reactions (slow-cooking, braising)
Soap Function:
 Chemical reactions breaking apart grease molecules
Evidence: Emulsification (grease disperses in water)
Batteries:
 Chemical reactions releasing electrical energy
Evidence: Current flow, heat production
Rate factors: Temperature (cold batteries work poorly)
Environmental:
Rust Formation:
 Iron + oxygen ‚Üí iron oxide
Evidence: Color change (gray ‚Üí reddish-brown)
Rate factors: Water presence (catalyst), salt concentration
Decomposition:
 Breaking down of organic matter
Evidence: Color change, gas production, temperature change
Rate factors: Temperature, presence of decomposers (catalysts)
Food-Related:
Fruit Browning:
 Enzymatic oxidation
Evidence: Color change (yellow/white ‚Üí brown)
Prevention: Lower temperature (refrigeration), remove oxygen (lemon juice)
Bread Rising:
 Yeast producing CO‚ÇÇ through fermentation
Evidence: Gas production (dough expands), temperature increase
Rate factors: Temperature, yeast concentration
Food Preservation:
Refrigeration slows bacterial growth (temperature factor)
Freezing nearly stops reactions (very low temperature)
Vacuum sealing removes oxygen (concentration factor)
Preservatives act as inhibitors (negative catalysts)
Automotive:
Car Engines:
 Combustion reactions (gasoline + oxygen)
Evidence: Light (spark), heat, gas production (exhaust)
Rate control: Controlled explosions in cylinders
Airbags:
 Rapid gas-producing reaction (sodium azide decomposition)
Evidence: Extremely fast gas production
Rate: Designed for millisecond inflation
Catalytic Converters:
 Cleaning exhaust through catalyzed reactions
Catalysts: Platinum, palladium
Function: Convert harmful gases to less harmful ones
Safety:
Fireworks:
 Combustion producing colored light
Evidence: Light production (specific colors from specific elements), heat, gas
Rate: Fast, controlled explosions
Hand Warmers:
 Exothermic oxidation reaction
Evidence: Temperature increase (heat release)
Rate control: Sealed pouch (starts when exposed to air)
Instant Cold Packs:
 Endothermic dissolution reaction
Evidence: Temperature decrease (heat absorption)
Rate: Immediate when inner pouch broken

Expandable sections for scaffolding (details/summary tags)
Responsive sizing for different screens
What Technology Was NOT Used
Absent Technologies:
No hands-on virtual labs (contrast with Week 1 PhET)
No collaborative digital tools (Google Docs, Padlet, Jamboard)
No video creation by students
No digital flashcards or quiz games (Quizlet, Kahoot, Blooket)
No augmented/virtual reality
No data logging or graphing software
No computational modeling beyond presentations
Rationale:
Focus on conceptual understanding through reading/writing
Forms provide sufficient interaction for assessment
Presentations offer visualization without simulation complexity

Grading Philosophy
Standards-Based Approach:
 "This week focused on understanding chemical reactions from multiple angles‚Äîevidence, conservation, rates, and distinguishing change types."
Target-Level Reporting:
Each of 4 targets assessed separately
Specific feedback per target
Opportunities for targeted remediation
Mastery Threshold:
 "If you score below 80% on any component, you'll get personalized feedback and opportunities to improve through targeted review or retakes."
Process Over Product:
 "We care about mastery, not whether you got everything perfect the first time!"
Growth Orientation:
 "You'll receive immediate feedback on your work with specific comments on each learning target."
Iterative Nature:
 "Science is iterative" - revision and retakes normalized and encouraged
Differentiation in Assessment
Exit Ticket Differentiation (Described but not detailed in document):
Reference to "three challenge levels" or differentiated questions (not explicitly shown in HTML)
Potential DOK progression from recall to application to synthesis
Station Scaffolding Levels:
Vocabulary sections (optional support)
Common Struggles (anticipatory scaffolding)
Worked examples (expert modeling)
Self-check questions (metacognitive support)
Assessment Validity and Alignment
Target 1 Assessment:
Station 1: Direct evidence type identification, explanation, application
Exit Ticket: Novel scenarios requiring evidence recognition
Target 2 Assessment:
Station 2: Conservation principle explanation, equation balancing, system classification
Exit Ticket: Mass prediction scenarios
Target 3 Assessment:
Station 3: Factor identification, mechanism explanation, rate prediction
Exit Ticket: Real-world rate control scenarios
Target 4 Assessment:
Station 1: Physical vs. chemical classification with justification
Exit Ticket: Novel change classification with molecular-level reasoning
Three-Dimensional Assessment:
SEP (Science Practices):
 Data analysis (Target 1), model development (Target 2), model use (Target 3)
DCI (Core Ideas):
 PS1.B chemical reactions comprehensively addressed
CCC (Crosscutting Concepts):
 Patterns (evidence types), Energy/Matter (conservation)
Pedagogical Strategies Employed
Cognitive Science Applications
1. Retrieval Practice
Implementation:
"Building on What You Know" section activates Week 1 &amp; 2 content
30-second recall prompts for prior concepts
Self-check questions before each station submission
Reflection prompts requiring recall of learned content
Rationale Shared with Students:
 "When you activate your prior knowledge before learning new material, your brain creates 'hooks' to attach new information to. Even if you don't remember everything perfectly, the act of trying to recall strengthens your memory pathways!"
2. Metacognitive Monitoring
Implementation:
Self-check checklists before submission (each station)
Success indicators clearly stated
Reflection prompts after each station
Pre-exit ticket readiness check
Initial hypothesis vs. final understanding comparison
Purpose:
Students assess own understanding
Identify knowledge gaps
Regulate learning strategies
Develop self-awareness
3. Worked Examples
Examples Provided:
Station 1:
Steel Wool + Oxygen analysis (expert chemist reasoning modeled)
Step-by-step observation ‚Üí evidence ‚Üí molecular-level ‚Üí conclusion
Station 2:
Balancing H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí H‚ÇÇO (6-step process)
Explicit reasoning for each coefficient choice
Common mistakes identified
Station 3:
Temperature effect on food spoiling (comparative analysis)
Particle motion ‚Üí collision frequency/energy ‚Üí rate outcome
Format:
 "Let's watch an expert chemist/scientist analyze..." with step-by-step reasoning verbalized
4. Productive Struggle Normalization
Common Struggles Sections:
Anticipate 5 misconceptions per station
Provide clarifications before students encounter confusion
Normalize that "this is what most students wonder"
Reduce anxiety about asking questions
Language Used:
"Great question!"
"Excellent question!"
"This is a common misconception"
"Don't worry if you can't remember everything perfectly"
5. Spaced Repetition
Core Concepts Revisited:
Bond breaking/forming mentioned in Hook, Station 1, Station 2, Station 3, Exit Ticket
Evidence types introduced Station 1, applied throughout
Conservation principle (Station 2) connected to rates (Station 3) and evidence (Station 1)
Progressive Complexity:
Week 1: Structure exists
Week 2: Bonds hold structures together
Week 3: Bonds break and reform in reactions
6. Dual Coding
Text + Visual Pairings:
Embedded presentations for each station
Color-coded navigation
Tables comparing concepts (ionic vs. covalent in context, physical vs. chemical)
Emoji visual anchors (üî¨, ‚öñÔ∏è, ‚ö°, üéì)
Multiple Representation Modes:
Verbal descriptions
Visual diagrams (in presentations)
Analogies (blanket sharing, key-lock, teacher-student)
Real-world examples
7. Elaborative Interrogation
Prompting "Why" Explanations:
"Explain why each type of evidence indicates NEW substances formed"
"WHY atoms stick together" (Week 2 bridge)
"WHAT HAPPENS when bonds break" (Week 3 focus)
Success criteria require causal reasoning, not just facts
8. Concrete-to-Abstract Progression
Sequence:
Concrete Observable:
 Candle burning (Hook)
Evidence Patterns:
 Color, gas, temperature, light (Station 1)
Abstract Principle:
 Conservation of mass (Station 2)
Mechanistic Reasoning:
 Collision theory (Station 3)
Integration:
 Complete phenomenon explanation (Synthesis)
Scaffolding Architecture
1. Vocabulary Support
Format:
 Expandable 
&lt;details&gt;
 sections with color-coded borders
Content:
Definition
Example
Molecular-level explanation (when applicable)
Accessibility:
 Click-to-expand prevents cognitive overload but provides on-demand support
2. Worked Examples
Structure:
Expert reasoning verbalized step-by-step
Observations explicitly labeled
Connections to molecular level made explicit
Pedagogical notes explain what to notice
3. Common Struggles Sections
Anticipatory Scaffolding:
Addresses misconceptions before they derail learning
Provides correct reasoning preemptively
Normalizes confusion
Offers multiple explanation approaches
4. Self-Check Questions
Function:
Student self-assessment before submission
Identifies knowledge gaps
Promotes metacognition
Reduces submission anxiety
5. Success Indicators
Transparency Features:
Explicit performance criteria
Observable/measurable outcomes
Connected to learning targets
Actionable for students
6. Gradual Release of Responsibility
Progression:
Hook:
 No instruction, pure exploration
Station 1:
 Vocabulary + worked example + practice
Station 2:
 Worked example + virtual experiment + application
Station 3:
 Multiple examples + integration + prediction
Exit Ticket:
 Independent application without scaffolds
7. Visual Hierarchy
Design Elements:
Color gradients distinguish sections (purple Hook, blue Station 1, orange Station 2, purple Station 3, green Exit Ticket)
Font size responsive (clamp() functions)
Navigation buttons prominent
Headers clearly structured (h2, h3 hierarchy)

Multiple Means of Representation:
Text descriptions with visual table organization

Color coding by section
Emoji visual anchors

Real-world examples
Multiple explanation approaches
Multiple Means of Action and Expression:
Written responses in forms
Short answer and extended response options
Reflection prompts (verbal or written)
Self-paced progression through stations
Optional presentation viewing timing
Multiple Means of Engagement:
Phenomenological mystery framing
Student-relevant language ("That's real chemistry! üóø")
Real-world career connections
Self-check autonomy
Growth mindset messaging
Retake opportunities
Think-Pair-Share Activity
Location:
 Station 2 (implicit in Common Struggles)
Question:
 "Why do you think covalent compounds don't conduct electricity? Think about it for 30 seconds, then discuss with a partner."
Hint:
 "Remember that electricity needs moving charges. Do covalent molecules have charged particles?"
Purpose:
Collaborative reasoning
Peer discussion
Activate prior knowledge about ions vs. molecules
Social construction of understanding
Analogies and Metaphors Used
1. Lego Structures (Week 3 Introduction)
 "It's like taking Lego structures apart and rebuilding them into completely different things."
Bonds = connections between Legos
Chemical reaction = disassembly and reassembly
Same pieces (atoms), different structure (molecules)
2. Blanket Sharing (Covalent Bonding Context)
 "Sharing electrons is like sharing a blanket‚Äîboth atoms hold onto the electrons together, like sharing a blanket."
3. Teacher as Catalyst (Station 3)
 "Think of a catalyst like a teacher: the teacher helps you learn (makes the reaction faster) but doesn't get 'used up' in the process."
4. Key and Lock (Catalyst Mechanism)
 "It's like having a key (catalyst) that makes opening a lock (starting a reaction) much easier than trying to force it open. The lock still opens either way, but the key makes it require less effort (energy)!"
5. Boulder Over Hill (Activation Energy)
 "Energy needed to START a reaction (like pushing a boulder over a hill)"
6. Breaking a Stick (Collision Energy)
 "Think of it like trying to break a stick: if you tap it gently (low-energy collision), nothing happens. You need to hit it HARD (high-energy collision) to break it."
7. Recipe Receipt (Balanced Equations)
 "Balanced equations show that every single atom on the left (reactants) ends up somewhere on the right (products). It's like a detailed receipt showing where every ingredient went in a recipe."
Curricular Scope: What Was Explicitly INCLUDED (New to Week 3)
Chemistry Concepts Introduced
1. Chemical Reactions:
Definition: Process where bonds break and new bonds form, creating new substances
Evidence types: Color change, gas production, temperature change, light production
Molecular-level description: Atom rearrangement
Distinction from physical changes
2. Physical Changes:
Definition: Same substance, different form or state
Molecular identity unchanged
Bonds intact (no breaking/forming)
Often reversible
3. Law of Conservation of Mass:
Formal statement (Lavoisier 1789)
Atoms rearranged but never created/destroyed
Total mass before = total mass after
System type importance (closed vs. open)
4. Chemical Equations:
Reactants ‚Üí Products notation
Balanced equations showing atom conservation
Coefficients vs. subscripts distinction
Formula writing basics
5. Reaction Rates:
Definition: Speed of chemical reaction
Spectrum from fast (milliseconds) to slow (years)
Factors affecting rates: Temperature, surface area, concentration, catalysts
Real-world rate control applications
6. Collision Theory:
Reactions require particle collisions
Three requirements: Frequency, energy, orientation
Temperature affects collision frequency and energy
Explains all four rate factors
7. Activation Energy:
Energy barrier to reaction initiation
Even exothermic reactions need initial energy input
Catalysts lower activation energy
Energy diagrams (described verbally)
8. Catalysts:
Speed up reactions without being consumed
Lower activation energy
Provide alternate pathway
Examples: Enzymes, industrial catalysts
9. Exothermic Reactions:
Release energy (usually heat/light)
Feel warm
Products more stable than reactants
Examples: Combustion, hand warmers
10. Endothermic Reactions:
Absorb energy from surroundings
Feel cold
Products less stable than reactants
Examples: Cold packs, photosynthesis
11. System Types:
Closed System:
 No atoms enter/leave (sealed container)
Open System:
 Atoms can enter/leave (open to environment)
Conservation observable in closed, masked in open
Quantitative Information Provided
Temperature Values:
Refrigerator: 4¬∞C
Room temperature: 25¬∞C
Food spoiling contrast: ~2 hours vs. 1-2 weeks
Melting Points (Context from Week 2):
Salt (NaCl): 801¬∞C (ionic, high)
Sugar (C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ): 186¬∞C (covalent, lower)
Chemical Formulas Used:
H‚ÇÇO (water)
CO‚ÇÇ (carbon dioxide)
NaCl (sodium chloride)
C‚ÇÅ‚ÇÇH‚ÇÇ‚ÇÇO‚ÇÅ‚ÇÅ (sucrose/sugar)
C‚ÇÇ‚ÇÖH‚ÇÖ‚ÇÇ (wax approximation)
O‚ÇÇ (oxygen gas)
H‚ÇÇ (hydrogen gas)
NaHCO‚ÇÉ (baking soda)
CH‚ÇÉCOOH (acetic acid/vinegar)
Fe (iron)
Fe‚ÇÇO‚ÇÉ (iron oxide/rust)
Balanced Equations:
2H‚ÇÇ + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 2H‚ÇÇO (hydrogen combustion)
C‚ÇÇ‚ÇÖH‚ÇÖ‚ÇÇ + 38O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí 25CO‚ÇÇ + 26H‚ÇÇO (candle combustion approximation)
Scientific Practices Emphasized
SEP-2: Developing and Using Models
Describing electron arrangements (Week 2 connection)
Balancing chemical equations to model conservation
Using collision theory to predict rate changes
Modeling closed vs. open systems
SEP-4: Analyzing and Interpreting Data
Identifying evidence patterns across reactions
Comparing properties before/after reactions
Interpreting evidence to determine if reactions occurred
Distinguishing physical vs. chemical based on data
Implicit SEP-6: Constructing Explanations
CER framework implied (Claim-Evidence-Reasoning)
Causal explanations required (bond breaking ‚Üí new substances ‚Üí evidence)
Molecular-level reasoning expected
Crosscutting Concepts Emphasized
CCC-1: Patterns
Four evidence types consistently indicate reactions
Predictable rate changes based on factor manipulation
Pattern recognition in distinguishing change types
CCC-5: Energy and Matter
Matter conservation through atom conservation
Energy transfer in exothermic/endothermic reactions
Energy barrier (activation energy) concept
CCC-7: Structure and Function (Implicit)
Bond structure determines reaction likelihood
Molecular structure influences reactivity

Vertical Coherence (Cross-Week Integration)
Week 1 ‚Üí Week 2 ‚Üí Week 3 Progression
Conceptual Building Sequence:
Week 1: WHAT structures exist
‚Üì
Week 2: WHY atoms bond (HOW structures form)
‚Üì
Week 3: WHAT HAPPENS when bonds break/form (reactions)
‚Üì
Week 4 (Preview): APPLY ALL to engineering design
Explicit Bridging Language:
Week 1 to Week 3:
 "You learned that molecules have different structures. This week you're investigating what happens when those bonds BREAK and new ones FORM‚Äîthat's a chemical reaction!"
Week 2 to Week 3:
 "You learned about the bonds that hold atoms together (both ionic and covalent). This week you're investigating what happens when those bonds BREAK and new ones FORM‚Äîthat's a chemical reaction! It's like taking Lego structures apart and rebuilding them into completely different things."
Week 3 to Week 4:
 "Next week (Week 4), you'll apply EVERYTHING you've learned to design your own chemical heating or cooling device for emergency situations‚Äîlike the hand warmers and cold packs that save lives!"
Concept Scaffolding Across Weeks
Atoms and Molecules (Week 1 ‚Üí Week 3):
Week 1: Atoms compose molecules; molecules have 3D structures
Week 2: Bonds hold atoms in molecules
Week 3: Reactions rearrange atoms by breaking and forming bonds
Properties (Week 1 ‚Üí Week 2 ‚Üí Week 3):
Week 1: Structure determines properties
Week 2: Bond type (ionic/covalent) determines properties
Week 3: Chemical reactions create substances with different properties
Evidence (Week 1 ‚Üí Week 3):
Week 1: Observable properties (shape, color, state)
Week 3: Observable evidence of reactions (color change, gas, temperature, light)
Energy (Week 2 ‚Üí Week 3):
Week 2: Energy involved in bond formation (implicitly)
Week 3: Energy released/absorbed in reactions; activation energy required
Skills Progression
Model Development (SEP-2):
Week 1: Build 3D molecular models (PhET)
Week 2: Describe electron arrangements in bonds
Week 3: Use balanced equations to model conservation; describe collision model
Data Analysis (SEP-4):
Week 1: Analyze molecular structures for patterns
Week 3: Analyze evidence before/after reactions
Explanation Construction (Implicit SEP-6):
Week 1: CER framework introduced (structure ‚Üí property)
Week 2: Causal explanations (electron behavior ‚Üí bond type ‚Üí properties)
Week 3: Causal explanations (bond breaking/forming ‚Üí evidence; factor changes ‚Üí rate changes)

Horizontal Coherence (Within Week 3)
Concept Building Sequence Within Week 3
Logical Progression:
Hook:
 Phenomenon (candle burning) establishes mystery
Station 1:
 How to recognize reactions happened (evidence)
Station 2:
 Atoms conserved even though substances change (conservation)
Station 3:
 What controls how fast reactions happen (rates)
Exit Ticket:
 Integration and application of all concepts
Conceptual Dependencies:
Evidence Types (Station 1)
    ‚Üì [Must recognize reactions to study conservation]
Conservation (Station 2)
    ‚Üì [Conservation principle applies to fast and slow reactions]
Reaction Rates (Station 3)
    ‚Üì [All factors control reactions identified in Station 1]
Comprehensive Application (Exit Ticket)
Consistent Threads Across Week 3
Thread 1: Bond Breaking and Forming
Hook: Implied in candle mystery
Station 1: Explicit - "bonds breaking and new bonds forming"
Station 2: Atoms rearrange (bonds break/reform) but aren't destroyed
Station 3: Collision energy must overcome bonds
Exit Ticket: Central to all explanations
Thread 2: Molecular-Level Reasoning
All stations require "what happens at the molecular level" explanations
Success criteria emphasize connecting observable to molecular
Worked examples model molecular-level thinking
Thread 3: Real-World Applications
Each station includes "Why Should You Care" section
Career connections distributed throughout
Everyday phenomena explained at each station
Thread 4: Evidence and Observation
Station 1: Four evidence types
Station 2: Mass measurement as evidence of conservation
Station 3: Observable rate changes as evidence of factor effects
Phenomenon Resolution Arc
Beginning (Hook):
Mystery: Where does candle wax go?
Student hypothesis generation
Middle (Stations):
Station 1: Burning produces evidence (light, heat)
Station 2: Atoms can't be destroyed (conservation)
Station 3: Combustion is fast exothermic reaction
End (Synthesis):
Complete CER explanation provided
Wax + O‚ÇÇ ‚Üí CO‚ÇÇ + H‚ÇÇO (all atoms accounted for)
Open system explains apparent mass loss
All four targets integrated in explanation

Time Allocation Rationale
Hook (8 min):
Minimal instruction needed
Primarily student thinking/writing
Establishes investment quickly
Stations (25 min each):
Presentation viewing: ~5-7 min
Reading/vocabulary exploration: ~5 min
Form completion: ~10-12 min
Self-check and reflection: ~2-3 min
Exit Ticket (15 min):
Shorter than stations (comprehensive but not new learning)
Sufficient for all four targets
Allows completion within class period
Pacing Flexibility
Built-In Flexibility:
Presentation viewing optional (students can skip if already understand)
Vocabulary sections expandable (use as needed)
Self-pacing through digital forms
Time Management Supports:
Clear time estimates provided
Navigation buttons allow quick movement
No external dependencies (all materials embedded)

 Week 3 likely distributed across multiple class sessions or extended time block
Language and Tone Analysis
Formality Level

 Accessible academic - maintains scientific vocabulary while remaining conversational
Comparison to Previous Weeks:

Week 3: Highest formality (minimal slang, maximum scientific vocabulary)

Reflection Prompts
Distributed Throughout Week 3:

 "Before we start, what's your current best guess about where the wax goes? Write it down somewhere private‚Äîwe'll come back to it at the end of the week to see how your thinking evolved!"
Post-Station 1:
 "üß† Quick Reflection (30 seconds): What's the ONE most important thing you learned about identifying chemical reactions? Say it out loud or write it down before moving to Station 2!"
Post-Station 2:
 "üß† Quick Reflection (30 seconds): Why is conservation of mass important in chemistry? How does it help us understand chemical reactions? Think about it before moving to Station 3!"
Post-Station 3:
 "üß† Quick Reflection (30 seconds): Think of ONE real-world situation where controlling reaction rate matters. How would you speed it up or slow it down? Be ready to share with a partner!"

 "When you're done, think back to what you wrote in the Hook at the beginning of the week. How much have you learned about chemical reactions? What changed in your thinking about where the candle wax goes?"

Self-Assessment Checklists
Format:
 "Self-Check Before Submitting" sections with checkbox lists
Station 1 Example:
‚òê Can I name all FOUR types of chemical reaction evidence?
‚òê Can I explain why each type of evidence indicates NEW substances formed?
‚òê Do I understand the difference between physical (same substance) and chemical (new substances)?
‚òê Can I describe what happens at the molecular level (bonds breaking, atoms rearranging, new bonds forming)?
‚òê Can I apply my knowledge to classify real-world examples correctly?
Station 2 Example:
‚òê Can I explain the Law of Conservation of Mass in my own words?
‚òê Do I know the difference between closed and open systems?
‚òê Can I predict whether mass will stay the same, increase, or decrease in different scenarios?
‚òê Can I count atoms on each side of a chemical equation?
‚òê Do I understand that we use coefficients (not subscripts) to balance equations?
‚òê Can I explain WHY we need to balance chemical equations (to show conservation)?
Station 3 Example:
‚òê Can I name and explain all FOUR factors that affect reaction rates?
‚òê Can I predict whether a change will speed up or slow down a reaction?
‚òê Do I understand what activation energy is and why it matters?
‚òê Can I describe how catalysts work to speed up reactions?
‚òê Can I connect particle collisions to reaction rates using collision theory?
‚òê Can I apply my knowledge to real-world scenarios (food storage, medicine, digestion)?
Exit Ticket Readiness Check:
‚òê Name all FOUR types of chemical reaction evidence?
‚òê Explain the Law of Conservation of Mass?
‚òê Describe how temperature, surface area, concentration, and catalysts affect rates?
‚òê Tell the difference between physical and chemical changes at the molecular level?
Purpose:
Student self-monitoring before submission
Gap identification
Autonomy in learning regulation
Reduced submission anxiety
Success Indicators
Format:
 "Success Indicators" or "You're successful when you can:" sections
Characteristics:
Observable/measurable criteria
Action verbs (identify, explain, distinguish, predict, describe, apply)
Specific to learning targets
Transparent expectations
Example (Station 1):
Identify all four types of evidence (color change, gas production, temperature change, light production)
Explain why each type of evidence indicates a new substance has formed
Distinguish between physical changes (same substance) and chemical changes (new substances)
Describe what happens at the molecular level during reactions
Apply knowledge to classify real-world examples with confidence
Purpose:
Transparency about assessment expectations
Clear performance criteria
Self-evaluation benchmarks
Common Struggles Sections
Anticipatory Scaffolding Function:
Addresses misconceptions before they derail learning
Provides correct reasoning preemptively
Normalizes confusion ("Great question!")
Offers multiple explanation approaches
Pattern:
Question or confusion phrased as student might ask
Clarification with detailed explanation
Often includes analogy or concrete example
Example Structure (Station 1, Struggle #2):
 
Question:
 "Is boiling water a chemical change because it produces gas (steam)?" 
Clarification:
 "No! This is a common misconception. Boiling is physical because the H‚ÇÇO molecules stay H‚ÇÇO‚Äîthey just move faster and spread out. The steam can condense back into liquid water. In a TRUE chemical gas production (like baking soda + vinegar), NEW gas molecules form that didn't exist before. The CO‚ÇÇ gas comes from rearranging atoms, not just spreading out the same molecules."
Coverage:
 5 common struggles per station (15 total across 3 stations)

Concept 3 mastery (Rates - Station 3)

Each concept builds on previous
Mastery expected before progression
Formative assessment embedded at each stage
Summative assessment integrates all concepts

 Burning Candle Mystery

Establishes cognitive need for concepts
Revisited throughout week
Resolved at end using integrated knowledge

Generative (supports multiple lines of inquiry)
Problem-centered (requires explanation)
Video-enhanced (presentation support)

Technical Curricular Documentation: Week 4 Lesson Plan
Course Identification
Unit Title:
 Week 4: Chemical Heating &amp; Cooling D
Technical Curricular Documentation: Week 4 Lesson Plan
Course Identification
Unit Title:
 Week 4: Chemical Heating &amp; Cooling Device Design
 
Grade Level:
 7th Grade Science
 
Instructional Time:
 ~70 minutes (within 75-minute class period)
 
Total Point Value:
 90 points
 
Institution:
 Kairos Academies
 
Instructor:
 Louis Rosche
 
Cycle Designation:
 Cycle 2, Week 4
NGSS Three-Dimensional Learning Framework
Primary Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-6 (Depth of Knowledge Level 3)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Undertake a design project to construct, test, and modify a device that either releases or absorbs thermal energy by chemical processes.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can design, build, and test a device that uses a chemical reaction to heat up or cool down."
Emphasis:
 Primary focus of entire lesson; comprehensive engineering design project integrating all prior learning from Weeks 1-3
Alignment Components:
DCI:
 PS1.B Chemical Reactions
SEP:
 SEP-6 Constructing Explanations and Designing Solutions (Elements 1, 3)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Energy and Matter (Element 2)
Integrated Performance Expectations
MS-ETS1-1 (Engineering Design - Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Define the criteria and constraints of a design problem with sufficient precision to ensure a successful solution, taking into account relevant scientific principles and potential impacts on people and the natural environment.
Application in Week 4:
Students define specific criteria for heating/cooling devices
Constraints identified: materials, cost, size, time, safety
Scientific principles: Bond energy, exothermic/endothermic reactions, conservation of energy
Impact considerations: Emergency relief applications, safety, practicality
MS-ETS1-2 (Engineering Design - Depth of Knowledge Level 3)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Evaluate competing design solutions using a systematic process to determine how well they meet the criteria and constraints of the problem.
Application in Week 4:
Analysis of six different chemical reactions (Station 1)
Systematic comparison using quantitative data (ŒîT values)
Trade-off analysis between temperature change, rate, safety, cost
Version 1 vs. Version 2 comparative evaluation (Station 3)
Disciplinary Core Ideas (DCI)
PS1.B: Chemical Reactions (Primary)
 
Focus Areas:
Chemical reactions involve energy transfer between system and surroundings
Energy changes result from bond breaking (requires energy) and bond forming (releases energy)
Net energy change determines if reaction is exothermic (releases heat) or endothermic (absorbs heat)
Magnitude of energy transfer can be measured quantitatively (ŒîT)
Conceptual Anchors:
Bond breaking requires energy input
Bond forming releases energy
Difference between energy required and energy released determines net energy transfer
Energy is conserved (transferred, not created or destroyed)
PS1.A: Structure and Properties of Matter (Connected)
 
Focus Areas:
Particle motion relates to temperature
Thermal energy transfer between substances
Material properties affect heat transfer efficiency
Conceptual Connection:
 Material selection for device containers and insulation affects thermal energy transfer rates and efficiency
Science and Engineering Practices (SEP)
SEP-6: Constructing Explanations and Designing Solutions (Primary)
 
Element 1:
 Construct an explanation using models or representations
 
Application in Week 4:
Explain device function using bond energy model
Construct explanations for temperature changes based on molecular-level processes
Use chemical equations and energy diagrams to support design reasoning
Element 3:
 Undertake a design project, engaging in the design cycle, to construct and/or implement a solution that meets specific design criteria and constraints
 
Application in Week 4:
Define design problem with criteria and constraints (Station 2)
Create initial prototype design (Station 2)
Test and collect quantitative data (Station 2)
Analyze results and identify improvements (Station 3)
Modify design based on evidence (Station 3)
Iterate through design cycle (Stations 2-3)
SEP-4: Analyzing and Interpreting Data (Secondary)
 
Element 1:
 Construct, analyze, and/or interpret graphical displays of data and/or large data sets to identify linear and nonlinear relationships
 
Application in Week 4:
Analyze temperature change data from six reactions (Station 1)
Compare quantitative ŒîT values to select optimal reaction
Interpret data patterns (exothermic vs. endothermic)
Element 3:
 Analyze and interpret data to determine similarities and differences in findings
 
Application in Week 4:
Compare exothermic reactions (A, B, C) for heating applications
Compare endothermic reactions (D, E, F) for cooling applications
Evaluate Version 1 vs. Version 2 device performance
Crosscutting Concepts (CCC)
CCC-5: Energy and Matter (Primary)
 
Element 1:
 Matter is conserved because atoms are conserved in physical and chemical processes
 
Application in Week 4:
Atoms rearrange during chemical reactions but aren't created/destroyed
Chemical equations show conservation of atoms
Connection to Week 3 conservation of mass principle
Element 2:
 The transfer of energy can be tracked as energy flows through a designed or natural system
 
Application in Week 4 - CENTRAL THEME:
Energy transfers from chemical system to surroundings (exothermic heating)
Energy transfers from surroundings to chemical system (endothermic cooling)
Thermal energy flow measurable via temperature changes
Energy conservation: Energy released from bond forming must come from somewhere (reactant bonds)
System boundaries important: Device (system) exchanges energy with surroundings (air, target object)
CCC-2: Cause and Effect (Secondary)
 
Element 1:
 Cause and effect relationships may be used to predict phenomena in natural or designed systems
 
Application in Week 4:
Bond energy differences CAUSE temperature changes
Increased reactant quantity CAUSES larger ŒîT
Insulation modifications CAUSE improved heat retention/transfer
Design modifications CAUSE predictable performance changes
Instructional Objectives (Standards-Based Learning Targets)
Target 1: Investigate Chemical Energy Transfer (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Conduct systematic analysis of exothermic and endothermic reactions, interpreting quantitative temperature data and explaining energy transfer at the molecular level through bond breaking and bond forming.
Success Criteria:
Analyze ŒîT data from minimum 6 reactions (3 exothermic, 3 endothermic)
Classify reactions as exothermic or endothermic based on temperature change direction
Explain energy transfer using bond energy concepts: "CaCl‚ÇÇ + H‚ÇÇO is exothermic with ŒîT = +25¬∞C because the energy released when new bonds form between calcium ions and water molecules is GREATER than the energy needed to break apart the CaCl‚ÇÇ ionic lattice and H-O-H bonds. The extra energy escapes as heat, raising the temperature."
Compare magnitude of energy transfer across reactions using quantitative data
Select optimal reaction for specific application (heating vs. cooling) with justified reasoning
Assessment Methods:
Station 1 form responses analyzing six reactions (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 1: Investigation Summary
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (understanding chemical processes for thermal energy device)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing and Interpreting Data - Elements 1, 3)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Chemical reactions and energy transfer)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Element 2 (Energy transfer tracking)
Target 2: Define Design Problem (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Define specific, measurable criteria and realistic constraints for a chemical heating or cooling device, identifying trade-offs and justifying decisions using chemistry concepts from Weeks 1-3.
Success Criteria:
State minimum 3 specific success criteria (e.g., "Must achieve ŒîT ‚â• ¬±10¬∞C within 5 minutes")
Identify minimum 5 constraints (materials, cost, size, time, safety)
Explain trade-offs: "Choosing iron + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C) over CaCl‚ÇÇ (ŒîT = +25¬∞C) gives stronger heating, BUT iron oxidation takes longer and is harder to control, so there's a speed vs. magnitude trade-off."
Connect criteria/constraints to real-world emergency relief context
Justify decisions using chemistry principles (bond energy, reaction rates, safety)
Example Success Statement:
 "My heating device must reach 45¬∞C within 5 minutes (criterion) using only materials available in our lab (constraint). I chose CaCl‚ÇÇ + water because it provides ŒîT = +25¬∞C, which meets the criterion, is safe to handle, activates quickly, and uses affordable materials. The trade-off is that iron + salt produces higher ŒîT (+30¬∞C), but safety concerns with iron powder make CaCl‚ÇÇ the better choice for emergency deployment."
Assessment Methods:
Station 2 design documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 2: Design Problem Definition
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-1 (defining criteria and constraints)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Element 3 (Design project engagement)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Applying chemical knowledge to engineering problem)
CCC:
 CCC-5 (Energy and matter considerations in design)
Target 3: Design and Construct Device (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Design, document, and test a prototype device that uses chemical reactions to transfer thermal energy, documenting all design decisions with scientific reasoning based on bond energy principles.
Success Criteria:
Create detailed design specifications including:
Chemical reaction selected with justification
Container design and materials with heat transfer rationale
Activation method (how reactants mix)
Predicted performance based on Station 1 data
Conduct initial test and collect quantitative data (starting temp, final temp, ŒîT, time)
Calculate actual vs. predicted performance
Explain device function using molecular-level bond energy reasoning
Document safety features and practical deployment considerations
Example Success Statement:
 "My cooling device uses NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ + water in a double-chamber plastic bag. When activated by breaking the inner water pouch, the endothermic dissolution occurs (ŒîT = -15¬∞C predicted). The outer bag allows heat absorption from an injured area while preventing chemical contact. In my test, I achieved ŒîT = -12¬∞C in 3 minutes (slightly less than predicted, possibly due to heat loss to air). This works because breaking the NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ ionic lattice requires MORE energy than is released when new ion-water interactions form, so energy is absorbed from surroundings, lowering temperature."
Assessment Methods:
Station 2 prototype design and test documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 3: Design &amp; Testing
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (construct device using chemical processes)
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-2 (evaluate design solution)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Elements 1, 3 (Construct explanations and design solutions)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Energy transfer in chemical reactions)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Element 2 (Energy flow through designed system)
Target 4: Iterate Based on Evidence (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Analyze test data from initial prototype, identify specific design improvements based on peer feedback and quantitative evidence, modify device systematically, and evaluate whether modifications optimized performance for defined criteria.
Success Criteria:
Analyze Version 1 data to identify minimum 2 specific problems (e.g., "ŒîT only +18¬∞C, below +25¬∞C criterion" or "Reaction completed in 8 minutes, too slow for 5-minute criterion")
Gather and document peer feedback using Glow-Grow-Chemistry protocol
Plan minimum 2 evidence-based modifications with chemistry reasoning: "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g should increase ŒîT because more ionic bonds breaking and forming means more total energy released. Based on proportional relationship, I predict ŒîT will increase from +18¬∞C to approximately +27¬∞C."
Implement modifications and collect Version 2 quantitative data
Compare Version 1 vs. Version 2 performance using specific metrics (ŒîT, time, criteria met)
Evaluate trade-offs: "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ quantity improved ŒîT (+27¬∞C, now meets criterion), BUT increased cost per device and made mixture too viscous, reducing heat transfer rate. Need to balance quantity and practicality."
Propose Version 3 improvements based on evidence
Assessment Methods:
Station 3 iteration documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 4: Iteration &amp; Optimization
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-2 (evaluate and improve design solutions)
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (modify device based on testing)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Element 3 (Design cycle iteration)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing comparative data)
CCC:
 CCC-2 (Cause-effect relationships in modifications)
CCC:
 CCC-5 (Energy transfer optimization)
Phenomenological Framing
Anchor Phenomenon: Hurricane Relief Challenge
Observable Phenomenon:
 Hurricane Maria devastated Puerto Rico in 2017, leaving millions without electricity for months. Emergency responders required methods to heat food and cool medical supplies without power infrastructure. Chemical heating and cooling devices provided life-saving solutions in disaster scenarios.
Driving Questions:
Primary:
 How can chemistry save lives when there's no electricity?
Secondary:
 What chemical reactions can be harnessed to heat or cool on demand?
Application:
 Can you design a device that would actually work in a disaster scenario?
Cognitive Dissonance Created:
Observation 1:
 Emergency situations require heating/cooling
Observation 2:
 No power infrastructure available (no batteries, no plugs)
Tension:
 How can you generate temperature changes without electrical energy?
Resolution:
 Chemical energy stored in bonds can be converted to thermal energy through reactions
Pedagogical Purpose:
Establishes authentic, high-stakes context for engineering design
Motivates learning about exothermic and endothermic reactions
Requires integration of all Week 1-3 concepts (atoms, bonds, reactions, energy)
Demonstrates real-world application of chemistry knowledge
Creates emotional investment (humanitarian crisis response)
Phenomenon Type Classification:
Macroscopic Context:
 Disaster relief, emergency medical care
Molecular-Level Explanation Required:
 Bond breaking/forming, energy transfer
Engineering Application:
 Design functional device to meet human needs
Social Relevance:
 Hurricane preparedness, military applications, athletic training
Real-World Examples Provided:
Military:
 MREs (Meals Ready to Eat) with chemical heaters for soldiers in field
Athletic Training:
 Instant cold packs for immediate injury treatment
Disaster Relief:
 Chemical heating/cooling for emergency situations
Space Exploration:
 Chemical thermal management in space missions
Phenomenon Resolution Strategy
Week 4 Progression:
Hook:
 Introduce hurricane relief challenge; students hypothesize solutions
Station 1:
 Investigate which reactions produce largest ŒîT (identify useful reactions)
Station 2:
 Design device using selected reaction; predict performance
Station 3:
 Iterate design based on test data and feedback; optimize for criteria
Exit Ticket:
 Complete engineering report demonstrating device could address emergency need
Complete Solution Components:
Chemical Selection:
 Exothermic reactions (CaCl‚ÇÇ, iron oxidation) for heating; endothermic reactions (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ, baking soda reactions) for cooling
Energy Transfer Mechanism:
 Bond energy differences drive thermal energy transfer to/from surroundings
Design Considerations:
 Container materials, activation methods, insulation, safety features
Practical Deployment:
 Portable, activatable on demand, using accessible materials, safe for non-experts
Prior Knowledge Activation and Scaffolding
Explicit Connections to Weeks 1-3
Week 1 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 1 Content:
 Atoms and molecules exist; molecular structure determines properties; 3D spatial arrangements
Week 4 Application:
 Device design requires understanding that atoms rearrange during reactions (Week 1 foundation); properties of reactants vs. products determine device function
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that matter is made of atoms and molecules with specific structures. This week you're using those atoms in chemical reactions to build a device that transfers energy. The structure of your reactants and products matters for how well your device works!"
Week 2 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 2 Content:
 Chemical bonds (ionic and covalent); bond formation and electron behavior; bonding determines properties
Week 4 Application:
 Energy stored in chemical bonds is the source of thermal energy for devices; breaking bonds requires energy, forming bonds releases energy
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that atoms stick together through chemical bonds where electrons transfer (ionic) or share (covalent). This week you're using bond breaking and bond forming as your energy source. When bonds break, energy is needed. When bonds form, energy is released. That energy difference is what heats or cools your device!"
Week 3 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 3 Content:
 Chemical reactions; evidence of reactions; conservation of mass and energy; reaction rates; exothermic vs. endothermic
Week 4 Application:
 Direct application of Week 3 concepts - students engineer devices using exothermic/endothermic reactions; optimize using rate factors
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that chemical reactions transfer energy‚Äîexothermic reactions release heat, endothermic reactions absorb heat. Week 4 (This Week): You're ENGINEERING devices that use those energy transfers to solve real problems. You'll apply everything about reaction rates, energy changes, and evidence to design something that could actually save lives in emergencies!"
Sequential Concept Building Across All Four Weeks
Complete Progression:
Week 1:
 WHAT structures exist (atoms, molecules, 3D arrangements)
Week 2:
 WHY atoms bond; HOW bonds form (ionic/covalent)
Week 3:
 WHAT HAPPENS when bonds break and reform (chemical reactions, energy transfer)
Week 4:
 APPLY EVERYTHING to engineering design (harness chemical energy for practical purpose)
Retrieval Practice Elements
Embedded in "What You Already Know" Section:
 
Explicit Retrieval Prompts:
"Atoms &amp; Molecules: Matter is made of particles that can rearrange"
"Chemical Bonds: Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
"Chemical Reactions: Reactants transform into products, but mass is conserved"
"Energy Transfer: Energy can move between substances and their surroundings"
Purpose:
Activate all prior learning from Weeks 1-3
Establish foundation for engineering application
Show students how cumulative knowledge enables complex problem-solving
Reduce cognitive load by making connections explicit
Reassurance Provided:
 Implicit in language: "What You Already Know" framing emphasizes that students have the necessary foundation, reducing anxiety about complex engineering task
Metacognitive Priming
Hook Initial Hypothesis:
 Students asked to propose solutions to hurricane relief challenge BEFORE instruction
Purpose:
Baseline assessment of engineering thinking
Investment in problem-solving
Enables metacognitive reflection on growth (Hook ‚Üí Exit Ticket comparison)
Normalizes multiple valid solutions ("no single right answer")
Lesson Sequence and Pedagogical Structure
Phase 1: Hook - The Hurricane Relief Challenge (10 points, ~10 minutes)
Instructional Purpose
Establish authentic, high-stakes engineering challenge; activate prior knowledge from Weeks 1-3; create emotional investment through humanitarian crisis context; generate initial design hypotheses; introduce engineering design vocabulary (criteria, constraints, prototype)
Phenomenon Presentation
Historical Context Provided:
 "Hurricane Maria devastated Puerto Rico in 2017, leaving millions without power for months. Emergency responders needed ways to heat food and cool medical supplies without electricity."
Challenge Statement:
 "Your mission: Design a device that uses chemical reactions to either heat OR cool‚Äîwith no batteries, no plugs, and materials that could actually be deployed in emergency situations."
Significance Established:
U.S. military uses chemical heaters in MREs for soldiers in the field
Athletes and trainers rely on instant cold packs for immediate injury treatment
Chemical heating/cooling used in camping, disaster relief, space exploration
Understanding exothermic/endothermic reactions essential for chemical engineering
Emotional Hook:
 Framing as life-saving humanitarian application (not just academic exercise)
Prior Knowledge Activation
"What You Already Know" Section:
 Explicit connections to all three prior weeks:
Week 1 Connection:
"Atoms &amp; Molecules: Matter is made of particles that can rearrange"
Week 2 Connection:
"Chemical Bonds: Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
Week 3 Connection:
"Chemical Reactions: Reactants transform into products, but mass is conserved"
"Energy Transfer: Energy can move between substances and their surroundings"
Pedagogical Function:
 Shows students they possess necessary foundation; reduces anxiety about complex task; makes cumulative learning explicit
Vocabulary Introduction
Key Engineering Design Terms Introduced:
 Visual grid format with color-coded definitions:
Exothermic:
 Releases heat energy (red coding)
Endothermic:
 Absorbs heat energy (blue coding)
Thermal Energy:
 Heat energy transfer (purple coding)
Design Criteria:
 Requirements for success (gold coding)
Constraints:
 Limitations &amp; restrictions (teal coding)
Prototype:
 Initial test model (orange coding)
Format:
 White background boxes with colored borders and concise definitions
Purpose:
 Front-load technical vocabulary before engineering work; provide visual reference throughout lesson
Student Activities
Response Format:
 Google Form submission (embedded iframe, height: 2400px)
Question Types:
Initial design hypothesis (open-ended)
Device type preference (heating vs. cooling)

Phase 2: Station 1 - Investigate &amp; Analyze Reactions (20 points, ~14 minutes)
Central Concept
Systematic analysis of six chemical reactions (3 exothermic, 3 endothermic) using quantitative temperature data to identify optimal reactions for heating or cooling devices. Understanding energy transfer through bond breaking/forming lens.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Target Addressed:
Target 1:
 Investigate Chemical Energy Transfer (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Prepares for Target 2 (selecting reaction for design)
Foundation for Target 3 (explaining device function)
Core Content:
Quantitative analysis of temperature change data (ŒîT values)
Classification: exothermic vs. endothermic
Molecular-level explanation of energy transfer (bond breaking/forming)
Comparative evaluation for engineering application
Trade-off analysis (magnitude vs. safety vs. cost vs. speed)
Station Structure
Form Organization (Implied from HTML):
Section 1: Introduction and instructions
Section 2: Reaction data analysis
Section 3: Exothermic reaction comparison
Section 4: Endothermic reaction comparison
Section 5: Selection and justification
Section 6: Molecular-level explanation
Reaction Data Provided
Complete Dataset for Analysis:
EXOTHERMIC REACTIONS (Temperature Increases):
Reaction A: Calcium Chloride (CaCl‚ÇÇ) + Water
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 45¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +25¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Rapid (seconds to minutes)
Safety:
 Moderate (exothermic heat, wear gloves)
Cost:
 Low (calcium chloride widely available)
Practical Notes:
 Very common in commercial hand warmers; reliable and consistent
Reaction B: Iron Powder + Water + Salt
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 50¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +30¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Moderate (several minutes)
Safety:
 Low-moderate (iron powder can be messy; oxidation slow)
Cost:
 Low (all materials common)
Practical Notes:
 Highest ŒîT of tested reactions; used in military MRE heaters; requires oxygen from air
Reaction C: Vinegar + Steel Wool
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 30¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +10¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Slow (minutes to hours for full reaction)
Safety:
 High (both materials safe, common household items)
Cost:
 Very low (vinegar and steel wool very cheap)
Practical Notes:
 Lowest ŒîT; too slow for most applications; good for demonstrations
ENDOTHERMIC REACTIONS (Temperature Decreases):
Reaction D: Ammonium Nitrate (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ) + Water
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 5¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -15¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Very rapid (seconds)
Safety:
 Moderate (ammonium nitrate requires safety precautions; do not ingest)
Cost:
 Low-moderate (agricultural fertilizer)
Practical Notes:
 Most common instant cold pack chemical; very effective; dissolution process
Reaction E: Baking Soda + Vinegar
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 17¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -3¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Very rapid (immediate fizzing)
Safety:
 Very high (both materials food-safe)
Cost:
 Very low (kitchen staples)
Practical Notes:
 Smallest ŒîT; too weak for practical cooling; produces CO‚ÇÇ gas (bubbles); good for understanding concept
Reaction F: Citric Acid + Baking Soda
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 15¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -5¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Rapid (seconds to minutes)
Safety:
 Very high (both materials food-safe; citric acid in citrus fruits)
Cost:
 Low (both readily available)
Practical Notes:
 Moderate ŒîT; safe option for student-designed devices; produces CO‚ÇÇ gas
Analysis Framework Provided
"Your Analysis Tasks" Section:
Task 1: Compare ŒîT Values
 "Which reactions had the biggest temperature changes?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Exothermic: Iron + water + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C) produces largest heating
Endothermic: Ammonium nitrate + water (ŒîT = -15¬∞C) produces largest cooling
Ranking from highest to lowest |ŒîT|: B &gt; D &gt; A &gt; F &gt; C &gt; E
Task 2: Identify Patterns
 "What makes a reaction exothermic vs. endothermic?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Exothermic: Temperature increases (ŒîT positive); releases heat to surroundings
Endothermic: Temperature decreases (ŒîT negative); absorbs heat from surroundings
Pattern recognition: All exothermic ŒîT values are positive; all endothermic ŒîT values are negative
Task 3: Explain the Chemistry
 "Why do these reactions release or absorb energy?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
 Apply bond energy model (taught explicitly in section below)
Task 4: Make Engineering Decisions
 "Which reaction is best for heating? For cooling?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Must balance multiple factors:
Magnitude:
 Higher |ŒîT| = more effective heating/cooling
Speed:
 Faster reaction = quicker response (important for emergencies)
Safety:
 Safer materials = easier deployment (non-experts can use)
Cost:
 Cheaper materials = more accessible for disaster relief
Practicality:
 Easier to contain/activate = more reliable
Example Justification (Heating):
 "Iron + water + salt gives highest ŒîT (+30¬∞C), but CaCl‚ÇÇ (+25¬∞C) might be better because it's faster, easier to control, and more reliable. The trade-off is 5¬∞C less heating for better practicality."
Example Justification (Cooling):
 "NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ gives by far the best ŒîT (-15¬∞C) and is very fast. Even though it requires safety precautions, it's worth it for strong cooling effect. Citric acid + baking soda is safer but only gives ŒîT = -5¬∞C, which might not be enough for medical supply cooling."
Task 5: Consider Trade-offs
 "Think about temperature, duration, safety, and cost"
Trade-off Categories Identified:
Magnitude vs. Safety:
 Higher ŒîT reactions often less safe
Speed vs. Control:
 Faster reactions harder to control/contain
Cost vs. Effectiveness:
 Cheaper materials sometimes less effective
Safety vs. Practicality:
 Very safe reactions may be too weak
Molecular-Level Explanation: Bond Energy
"The Molecular Story: Bond Energy" Section:
For EXOTHERMIC Reactions (Temperature Increases - Heat Released):
Energy Relationship:
Energy needed to break reactant bonds 
&lt;
 Energy released forming product bonds
NET RESULT:
 EXTRA energy released to surroundings as heat
Energy Flow:
System must absorb energy to break old bonds (endothermic step)
System releases energy when forming new bonds (exothermic step)
If Step 2 releases MORE than Step 1 requires ‚Üí net energy release
Excess energy transfers to surroundings (air, container, target object)
Surroundings gain thermal energy ‚Üí temperature increases ‚Üí ŒîT is positive
Example: Calcium Chloride Dissolution (Reaction A)
Molecular Process:
Breaking Bonds (Requires Energy):
Break ionic bonds in CaCl‚ÇÇ crystal lattice (Ca¬≤‚Å∫-Cl‚Åª attractions)
Break hydrogen bonds between H‚ÇÇO molecules
Energy input required: Moderate (ionic lattice relatively strong)
Forming Bonds (Releases Energy):
Form ion-dipole interactions between Ca¬≤‚Å∫ and water molecules (hydration shell)
Form ion-dipole interactions between Cl‚Åª and water molecules (hydration shell)
Energy output: HIGH (strong ion-dipole attractions; each ion surrounded by multiple water molecules)
Net Energy Balance:
Energy released (Step 2) &gt; Energy absorbed (Step 1)
Excess energy = 25¬∞C temperature increase
Surroundings warmer because system gave away energy
For ENDOTHERMIC Reactions (Temperature Decreases - Heat Absorbed):
Energy Relationship:
Energy needed to break reactant bonds 
&gt;
 Energy released forming product bonds
NET RESULT:
 EXTRA energy absorbed from surroundings
Energy Flow:
System must absorb energy to break old bonds (endothermic step)
System releases energy when forming new bonds (exothermic step)
If Step 1 requires MORE than Step 2 releases ‚Üí net energy absorption
Deficit energy pulled from surroundings (air, container, target object)
Surroundings lose thermal energy ‚Üí temperature decreases ‚Üí ŒîT is negative
Example: Ammonium Nitrate Dissolution (Reaction D)
Molecular Process:
Breaking Bonds (Requires Energy):
Break ionic bonds in NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ crystal lattice (NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫-NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª attractions)
Break hydrogen bonds between H‚ÇÇO molecules
Energy input required: HIGH (strong ionic lattice in ammonium nitrate)
Forming Bonds (Releases Energy):
Form ion-dipole interactions between NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫ and water molecules (hydration shell)
Form ion-dipole interactions between NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª and water molecules (hydration shell)
Energy output: MODERATE (ion-dipole attractions weaker than lattice was strong)
Net Energy Balance:
Energy released (Step 2) &lt; Energy absorbed (Step 1)
Energy deficit = 15¬∞C temperature decrease
Surroundings cooler because system took energy from them
Scaffolding Provided
Vocabulary Section (Implied but Not Explicitly Expandable):
 Key terms defined in context throughout instructions:
ŒîT (delta T): Change in temperature (final temp - starting temp)
Exothermic: Releases heat (ŒîT positive)
Endothermic: Absorbs heat (ŒîT negative)
Bond breaking: Requires energy input
Bond forming: Releases energy
System: The chemical reaction itself
Surroundings: Everything outside the system (air, container, etc.)
Worked Example Structure:
 Explicit reasoning for bond energy is modeled in "Molecular Story" section but not labeled as "worked example"
Analysis Support:
 "üìä Your Analysis Tasks" provides structured framework for systematic comparison

Real-World Engineering Context

 "Now you get to think like a scientist‚Äîlook at the numbers, compare the results, and make smart decisions about which reactions would work best for different purposes."

Collect quantitative data
Compare options systematically
Consider multiple factors (not just one variable)
Make evidence-based decisions with justified trade-offs
