evice Design
 
Grade Level:
 7th Grade Science
 
Instructional Time:
 ~70 minutes (within 75-minute class period)
 
Total Point Value:
 90 points
 
Institution:
 Kairos Academies
 
Instructor:
 Louis Rosche
 
Cycle Designation:
 Cycle 2, Week 4
NGSS Three-Dimensional Learning Framework
Primary Performance Expectation
MS-PS1-6 (Depth of Knowledge Level 3)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Undertake a design project to construct, test, and modify a device that either releases or absorbs thermal energy by chemical processes.
Student-Friendly Translation:
 "I can design, build, and test a device that uses a chemical reaction to heat up or cool down."
Emphasis:
 Primary focus of entire lesson; comprehensive engineering design project integrating all prior learning from Weeks 1-3
Alignment Components:
DCI:
 PS1.B Chemical Reactions
SEP:
 SEP-6 Constructing Explanations and Designing Solutions (Elements 1, 3)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Energy and Matter (Element 2)
Integrated Performance Expectations
MS-ETS1-1 (Engineering Design - Depth of Knowledge Level 2)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Define the criteria and constraints of a design problem with sufficient precision to ensure a successful solution, taking into account relevant scientific principles and potential impacts on people and the natural environment.
Application in Week 4:
Students define specific criteria for heating/cooling devices
Constraints identified: materials, cost, size, time, safety
Scientific principles: Bond energy, exothermic/endothermic reactions, conservation of energy
Impact considerations: Emergency relief applications, safety, practicality
MS-ETS1-2 (Engineering Design - Depth of Knowledge Level 3)
 
Performance Expectation:
 Evaluate competing design solutions using a systematic process to determine how well they meet the criteria and constraints of the problem.
Application in Week 4:
Analysis of six different chemical reactions (Station 1)
Systematic comparison using quantitative data (ŒîT values)
Trade-off analysis between temperature change, rate, safety, cost
Version 1 vs. Version 2 comparative evaluation (Station 3)
Disciplinary Core Ideas (DCI)
PS1.B: Chemical Reactions (Primary)
 
Focus Areas:
Chemical reactions involve energy transfer between system and surroundings
Energy changes result from bond breaking (requires energy) and bond forming (releases energy)
Net energy change determines if reaction is exothermic (releases heat) or endothermic (absorbs heat)
Magnitude of energy transfer can be measured quantitatively (ŒîT)
Conceptual Anchors:
Bond breaking requires energy input
Bond forming releases energy
Difference between energy required and energy released determines net energy transfer
Energy is conserved (transferred, not created or destroyed)
PS1.A: Structure and Properties of Matter (Connected)
 
Focus Areas:
Particle motion relates to temperature
Thermal energy transfer between substances
Material properties affect heat transfer efficiency
Conceptual Connection:
 Material selection for device containers and insulation affects thermal energy transfer rates and efficiency
Science and Engineering Practices (SEP)
SEP-6: Constructing Explanations and Designing Solutions (Primary)
 
Element 1:
 Construct an explanation using models or representations
 
Application in Week 4:
Explain device function using bond energy model
Construct explanations for temperature changes based on molecular-level processes
Use chemical equations and energy diagrams to support design reasoning
Element 3:
 Undertake a design project, engaging in the design cycle, to construct and/or implement a solution that meets specific design criteria and constraints
 
Application in Week 4:
Define design problem with criteria and constraints (Station 2)
Create initial prototype design (Station 2)
Test and collect quantitative data (Station 2)
Analyze results and identify improvements (Station 3)
Modify design based on evidence (Station 3)
Iterate through design cycle (Stations 2-3)
SEP-4: Analyzing and Interpreting Data (Secondary)
 
Element 1:
 Construct, analyze, and/or interpret graphical displays of data and/or large data sets to identify linear and nonlinear relationships
 
Application in Week 4:
Analyze temperature change data from six reactions (Station 1)
Compare quantitative ŒîT values to select optimal reaction
Interpret data patterns (exothermic vs. endothermic)
Element 3:
 Analyze and interpret data to determine similarities and differences in findings
 
Application in Week 4:
Compare exothermic reactions (A, B, C) for heating applications
Compare endothermic reactions (D, E, F) for cooling applications
Evaluate Version 1 vs. Version 2 device performance
Crosscutting Concepts (CCC)
CCC-5: Energy and Matter (Primary)
 
Element 1:
 Matter is conserved because atoms are conserved in physical and chemical processes
 
Application in Week 4:
Atoms rearrange during chemical reactions but aren't created/destroyed
Chemical equations show conservation of atoms
Connection to Week 3 conservation of mass principle
Element 2:
 The transfer of energy can be tracked as energy flows through a designed or natural system
 
Application in Week 4 - CENTRAL THEME:
Energy transfers from chemical system to surroundings (exothermic heating)
Energy transfers from surroundings to chemical system (endothermic cooling)
Thermal energy flow measurable via temperature changes
Energy conservation: Energy released from bond forming must come from somewhere (reactant bonds)
System boundaries important: Device (system) exchanges energy with surroundings (air, target object)
CCC-2: Cause and Effect (Secondary)
 
Element 1:
 Cause and effect relationships may be used to predict phenomena in natural or designed systems
 
Application in Week 4:
Bond energy differences CAUSE temperature changes
Increased reactant quantity CAUSES larger ŒîT
Insulation modifications CAUSE improved heat retention/transfer
Design modifications CAUSE predictable performance changes
Instructional Objectives (Standards-Based Learning Targets)
Target 1: Investigate Chemical Energy Transfer (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Conduct systematic analysis of exothermic and endothermic reactions, interpreting quantitative temperature data and explaining energy transfer at the molecular level through bond breaking and bond forming.
Success Criteria:
Analyze ŒîT data from minimum 6 reactions (3 exothermic, 3 endothermic)
Classify reactions as exothermic or endothermic based on temperature change direction
Explain energy transfer using bond energy concepts: "CaCl‚ÇÇ + H‚ÇÇO is exothermic with ŒîT = +25¬∞C because the energy released when new bonds form between calcium ions and water molecules is GREATER than the energy needed to break apart the CaCl‚ÇÇ ionic lattice and H-O-H bonds. The extra energy escapes as heat, raising the temperature."
Compare magnitude of energy transfer across reactions using quantitative data
Select optimal reaction for specific application (heating vs. cooling) with justified reasoning
Assessment Methods:
Station 1 form responses analyzing six reactions (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 1: Investigation Summary
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (understanding chemical processes for thermal energy device)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing and Interpreting Data - Elements 1, 3)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Chemical reactions and energy transfer)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Element 2 (Energy transfer tracking)
Target 2: Define Design Problem (DOK 2)
Objective:
 Define specific, measurable criteria and realistic constraints for a chemical heating or cooling device, identifying trade-offs and justifying decisions using chemistry concepts from Weeks 1-3.
Success Criteria:
State minimum 3 specific success criteria (e.g., "Must achieve ŒîT ‚â• ¬±10¬∞C within 5 minutes")
Identify minimum 5 constraints (materials, cost, size, time, safety)
Explain trade-offs: "Choosing iron + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C) over CaCl‚ÇÇ (ŒîT = +25¬∞C) gives stronger heating, BUT iron oxidation takes longer and is harder to control, so there's a speed vs. magnitude trade-off."
Connect criteria/constraints to real-world emergency relief context
Justify decisions using chemistry principles (bond energy, reaction rates, safety)
Example Success Statement:
 "My heating device must reach 45¬∞C within 5 minutes (criterion) using only materials available in our lab (constraint). I chose CaCl‚ÇÇ + water because it provides ŒîT = +25¬∞C, which meets the criterion, is safe to handle, activates quickly, and uses affordable materials. The trade-off is that iron + salt produces higher ŒîT (+30¬∞C), but safety concerns with iron powder make CaCl‚ÇÇ the better choice for emergency deployment."
Assessment Methods:
Station 2 design documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 2: Design Problem Definition
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-1 (defining criteria and constraints)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Element 3 (Design project engagement)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Applying chemical knowledge to engineering problem)
CCC:
 CCC-5 (Energy and matter considerations in design)
Target 3: Design and Construct Device (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Design, document, and test a prototype device that uses chemical reactions to transfer thermal energy, documenting all design decisions with scientific reasoning based on bond energy principles.
Success Criteria:
Create detailed design specifications including:
Chemical reaction selected with justification
Container design and materials with heat transfer rationale
Activation method (how reactants mix)
Predicted performance based on Station 1 data
Conduct initial test and collect quantitative data (starting temp, final temp, ŒîT, time)
Calculate actual vs. predicted performance
Explain device function using molecular-level bond energy reasoning
Document safety features and practical deployment considerations
Example Success Statement:
 "My cooling device uses NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ + water in a double-chamber plastic bag. When activated by breaking the inner water pouch, the endothermic dissolution occurs (ŒîT = -15¬∞C predicted). The outer bag allows heat absorption from an injured area while preventing chemical contact. In my test, I achieved ŒîT = -12¬∞C in 3 minutes (slightly less than predicted, possibly due to heat loss to air). This works because breaking the NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ ionic lattice requires MORE energy than is released when new ion-water interactions form, so energy is absorbed from surroundings, lowering temperature."
Assessment Methods:
Station 2 prototype design and test documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 3: Design &amp; Testing
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (construct device using chemical processes)
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-2 (evaluate design solution)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Elements 1, 3 (Construct explanations and design solutions)
DCI:
 PS1.B (Energy transfer in chemical reactions)
CCC:
 CCC-5 Element 2 (Energy flow through designed system)
Target 4: Iterate Based on Evidence (DOK 3)
Objective:
 Analyze test data from initial prototype, identify specific design improvements based on peer feedback and quantitative evidence, modify device systematically, and evaluate whether modifications optimized performance for defined criteria.
Success Criteria:
Analyze Version 1 data to identify minimum 2 specific problems (e.g., "ŒîT only +18¬∞C, below +25¬∞C criterion" or "Reaction completed in 8 minutes, too slow for 5-minute criterion")
Gather and document peer feedback using Glow-Grow-Chemistry protocol
Plan minimum 2 evidence-based modifications with chemistry reasoning: "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g should increase ŒîT because more ionic bonds breaking and forming means more total energy released. Based on proportional relationship, I predict ŒîT will increase from +18¬∞C to approximately +27¬∞C."
Implement modifications and collect Version 2 quantitative data
Compare Version 1 vs. Version 2 performance using specific metrics (ŒîT, time, criteria met)
Evaluate trade-offs: "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ quantity improved ŒîT (+27¬∞C, now meets criterion), BUT increased cost per device and made mixture too viscous, reducing heat transfer rate. Need to balance quantity and practicality."
Propose Version 3 improvements based on evidence
Assessment Methods:
Station 3 iteration documentation (20 points)
Exit Ticket Section 4: Iteration &amp; Optimization
Alignment:
Primary PE:
 MS-ETS1-2 (evaluate and improve design solutions)
Primary PE:
 MS-PS1-6 (modify device based on testing)
SEP:
 SEP-6 Element 3 (Design cycle iteration)
SEP:
 SEP-4 (Analyzing comparative data)
CCC:
 CCC-2 (Cause-effect relationships in modifications)
CCC:
 CCC-5 (Energy transfer optimization)
Phenomenological Framing
Anchor Phenomenon: Hurricane Relief Challenge
Observable Phenomenon:
 Hurricane Maria devastated Puerto Rico in 2017, leaving millions without electricity for months. Emergency responders required methods to heat food and cool medical supplies without power infrastructure. Chemical heating and cooling devices provided life-saving solutions in disaster scenarios.
Driving Questions:
Primary:
 How can chemistry save lives when there's no electricity?
Secondary:
 What chemical reactions can be harnessed to heat or cool on demand?
Application:
 Can you design a device that would actually work in a disaster scenario?
Cognitive Dissonance Created:
Observation 1:
 Emergency situations require heating/cooling
Observation 2:
 No power infrastructure available (no batteries, no plugs)
Tension:
 How can you generate temperature changes without electrical energy?
Resolution:
 Chemical energy stored in bonds can be converted to thermal energy through reactions
Pedagogical Purpose:
Establishes authentic, high-stakes context for engineering design
Motivates learning about exothermic and endothermic reactions
Requires integration of all Week 1-3 concepts (atoms, bonds, reactions, energy)
Demonstrates real-world application of chemistry knowledge
Creates emotional investment (humanitarian crisis response)
Phenomenon Type Classification:
Macroscopic Context:
 Disaster relief, emergency medical care
Molecular-Level Explanation Required:
 Bond breaking/forming, energy transfer
Engineering Application:
 Design functional device to meet human needs
Social Relevance:
 Hurricane preparedness, military applications, athletic training
Real-World Examples Provided:
Military:
 MREs (Meals Ready to Eat) with chemical heaters for soldiers in field
Athletic Training:
 Instant cold packs for immediate injury treatment
Disaster Relief:
 Chemical heating/cooling for emergency situations
Space Exploration:
 Chemical thermal management in space missions
Phenomenon Resolution Strategy
Week 4 Progression:
Hook:
 Introduce hurricane relief challenge; students hypothesize solutions
Station 1:
 Investigate which reactions produce largest ŒîT (identify useful reactions)
Station 2:
 Design device using selected reaction; predict performance
Station 3:
 Iterate design based on test data and feedback; optimize for criteria
Exit Ticket:
 Complete engineering report demonstrating device could address emergency need
Complete Solution Components:
Chemical Selection:
 Exothermic reactions (CaCl‚ÇÇ, iron oxidation) for heating; endothermic reactions (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ, baking soda reactions) for cooling
Energy Transfer Mechanism:
 Bond energy differences drive thermal energy transfer to/from surroundings
Design Considerations:
 Container materials, activation methods, insulation, safety features
Practical Deployment:
 Portable, activatable on demand, using accessible materials, safe for non-experts
Prior Knowledge Activation and Scaffolding
Explicit Connections to Weeks 1-3
Week 1 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 1 Content:
 Atoms and molecules exist; molecular structure determines properties; 3D spatial arrangements
Week 4 Application:
 Device design requires understanding that atoms rearrange during reactions (Week 1 foundation); properties of reactants vs. products determine device function
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that matter is made of atoms and molecules with specific structures. This week you're using those atoms in chemical reactions to build a device that transfers energy. The structure of your reactants and products matters for how well your device works!"
Week 2 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 2 Content:
 Chemical bonds (ionic and covalent); bond formation and electron behavior; bonding determines properties
Week 4 Application:
 Energy stored in chemical bonds is the source of thermal energy for devices; breaking bonds requires energy, forming bonds releases energy
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that atoms stick together through chemical bonds where electrons transfer (ionic) or share (covalent). This week you're using bond breaking and bond forming as your energy source. When bonds break, energy is needed. When bonds form, energy is released. That energy difference is what heats or cools your device!"
Week 3 ‚Üí Week 4 Bridge:
 
Week 3 Content:
 Chemical reactions; evidence of reactions; conservation of mass and energy; reaction rates; exothermic vs. endothermic
Week 4 Application:
 Direct application of Week 3 concepts - students engineer devices using exothermic/endothermic reactions; optimize using rate factors
Bridging Statement:
 "You learned that chemical reactions transfer energy‚Äîexothermic reactions release heat, endothermic reactions absorb heat. Week 4 (This Week): You're ENGINEERING devices that use those energy transfers to solve real problems. You'll apply everything about reaction rates, energy changes, and evidence to design something that could actually save lives in emergencies!"
Sequential Concept Building Across All Four Weeks
Complete Progression:
Week 1:
 WHAT structures exist (atoms, molecules, 3D arrangements)
Week 2:
 WHY atoms bond; HOW bonds form (ionic/covalent)
Week 3:
 WHAT HAPPENS when bonds break and reform (chemical reactions, energy transfer)
Week 4:
 APPLY EVERYTHING to engineering design (harness chemical energy for practical purpose)
Retrieval Practice Elements
Embedded in "What You Already Know" Section:
 
Explicit Retrieval Prompts:
"Atoms &amp; Molecules: Matter is made of particles that can rearrange"
"Chemical Bonds: Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
"Chemical Reactions: Reactants transform into products, but mass is conserved"
"Energy Transfer: Energy can move between substances and their surroundings"
Purpose:
Activate all prior learning from Weeks 1-3
Establish foundation for engineering application
Show students how cumulative knowledge enables complex problem-solving
Reduce cognitive load by making connections explicit
Reassurance Provided:
 Implicit in language: "What You Already Know" framing emphasizes that students have the necessary foundation, reducing anxiety about complex engineering task
Metacognitive Priming
Hook Initial Hypothesis:
 Students asked to propose solutions to hurricane relief challenge BEFORE instruction
Purpose:
Baseline assessment of engineering thinking
Investment in problem-solving
Enables metacognitive reflection on growth (Hook ‚Üí Exit Ticket comparison)
Normalizes multiple valid solutions ("no single right answer")
Lesson Sequence and Pedagogical Structure
Phase 1: Hook - The Hurricane Relief Challenge (10 points, ~10 minutes)
Instructional Purpose
Establish authentic, high-stakes engineering challenge; activate prior knowledge from Weeks 1-3; create emotional investment through humanitarian crisis context; generate initial design hypotheses; introduce engineering design vocabulary (criteria, constraints, prototype)
Phenomenon Presentation
Historical Context Provided:
 "Hurricane Maria devastated Puerto Rico in 2017, leaving millions without power for months. Emergency responders needed ways to heat food and cool medical supplies without electricity."
Challenge Statement:
 "Your mission: Design a device that uses chemical reactions to either heat OR cool‚Äîwith no batteries, no plugs, and materials that could actually be deployed in emergency situations."
Significance Established:
U.S. military uses chemical heaters in MREs for soldiers in the field
Athletes and trainers rely on instant cold packs for immediate injury treatment
Chemical heating/cooling used in camping, disaster relief, space exploration
Understanding exothermic/endothermic reactions essential for chemical engineering
Emotional Hook:
 Framing as life-saving humanitarian application (not just academic exercise)
Prior Knowledge Activation
"What You Already Know" Section:
 Explicit connections to all three prior weeks:
Week 1 Connection:
"Atoms &amp; Molecules: Matter is made of particles that can rearrange"
Week 2 Connection:
"Chemical Bonds: Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
Week 3 Connection:
"Chemical Reactions: Reactants transform into products, but mass is conserved"
"Energy Transfer: Energy can move between substances and their surroundings"
Pedagogical Function:
 Shows students they possess necessary foundation; reduces anxiety about complex task; makes cumulative learning explicit
Vocabulary Introduction

 Visual grid format with color-coded definitions:
Exothermic:
 Releases heat energy (red coding)
Endothermic:
 Absorbs heat energy (blue coding)

 Heat energy transfer (purple coding)
Design Criteria:
 Requirements for success (gold coding)
Constraints:
 Limitations &amp; restrictions (teal coding)
Prototype:
 Initial test model (orange coding)
Format:
 White background boxes with colored borders and concise definitions
Purpose:
 Front-load technical vocabulary before engineering work; provide visual reference throughout lesson

 Pure problem identification and hypothesis generation; instruction follows in stations
Phase 2: Station 1 - Investigate &amp; Analyze Reactions (20 points, ~14 minutes)
Central Concept
Systematic analysis of six chemical reactions (3 exothermic, 3 endothermic) using quantitative temperature data to identify optimal reactions for heating or cooling devices. Understanding energy transfer through bond breaking/forming lens.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Target Addressed:
Target 1:
 Investigate Chemical Energy Transfer (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Prepares for Target 2 (selecting reaction for design)
Foundation for Target 3 (explaining device function)
Core Content:
Quantitative analysis of temperature change data (ŒîT values)
Classification: exothermic vs. endothermic
Molecular-level explanation of energy transfer (bond breaking/forming)
Comparative evaluation for engineering application
Trade-off analysis (magnitude vs. safety vs. cost vs. speed)
Station Structure
Form Organization (Implied from HTML):
Section 1: Introduction and instructions
Section 2: Reaction data analysis
Section 3: Exothermic reaction comparison
Section 4: Endothermic reaction comparison
Section 5: Selection and justification
Section 6: Molecular-level explanation
Reaction Data Provided
Complete Dataset for Analysis:
EXOTHERMIC REACTIONS (Temperature Increases):
Reaction A: Calcium Chloride (CaCl‚ÇÇ) + Water
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 45¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +25¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Rapid (seconds to minutes)
Safety:
 Moderate (exothermic heat, wear gloves)
Cost:
 Low (calcium chloride widely available)
Practical Notes:
 Very common in commercial hand warmers; reliable and consistent
Reaction B: Iron Powder + Water + Salt
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 50¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +30¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Moderate (several minutes)
Safety:
 Low-moderate (iron powder can be messy; oxidation slow)
Cost:
 Low (all materials common)
Practical Notes:
 Highest ŒîT of tested reactions; used in military MRE heaters; requires oxygen from air
Reaction C: Vinegar + Steel Wool
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 30¬∞C
ŒîT:
 +10¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Slow (minutes to hours for full reaction)
Safety:
 High (both materials safe, common household items)
Cost:
 Very low (vinegar and steel wool very cheap)
Practical Notes:
 Lowest ŒîT; too slow for most applications; good for demonstrations
ENDOTHERMIC REACTIONS (Temperature Decreases):
Reaction D: Ammonium Nitrate (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ) + Water
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 5¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -15¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Very rapid (seconds)
Safety:
 Moderate (ammonium nitrate requires safety precautions; do not ingest)
Cost:
 Low-moderate (agricultural fertilizer)
Practical Notes:
 Most common instant cold pack chemical; very effective; dissolution process
Reaction E: Baking Soda + Vinegar
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 17¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -3¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Very rapid (immediate fizzing)
Safety:
 Very high (both materials food-safe)
Cost:
 Very low (kitchen staples)
Practical Notes:
 Smallest ŒîT; too weak for practical cooling; produces CO‚ÇÇ gas (bubbles); good for understanding concept
Reaction F: Citric Acid + Baking Soda
Starting Temperature:
 20¬∞C
Final Temperature:
 15¬∞C
ŒîT:
 -5¬∞C
Reaction Time:
 Rapid (seconds to minutes)
Safety:
 Very high (both materials food-safe; citric acid in citrus fruits)
Cost:
 Low (both readily available)
Practical Notes:
 Moderate ŒîT; safe option for student-designed devices; produces CO‚ÇÇ gas
Analysis Framework Provided
"Your Analysis Tasks" Section:
Task 1: Compare ŒîT Values
 "Which reactions had the biggest temperature changes?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Exothermic: Iron + water + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C) produces largest heating
Endothermic: Ammonium nitrate + water (ŒîT = -15¬∞C) produces largest cooling
Ranking from highest to lowest |ŒîT|: B &gt; D &gt; A &gt; F &gt; C &gt; E
Task 2: Identify Patterns
 "What makes a reaction exothermic vs. endothermic?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Exothermic: Temperature increases (ŒîT positive); releases heat to surroundings
Endothermic: Temperature decreases (ŒîT negative); absorbs heat from surroundings
Pattern recognition: All exothermic ŒîT values are positive; all endothermic ŒîT values are negative
Task 3: Explain the Chemistry
 "Why do these reactions release or absorb energy?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
 Apply bond energy model (taught explicitly in section below)
Task 4: Make Engineering Decisions
 "Which reaction is best for heating? For cooling?"
Expected Student Reasoning:
Must balance multiple factors:
Magnitude:
 Higher |ŒîT| = more effective heating/cooling
Speed:
 Faster reaction = quicker response (important for emergencies)
Safety:
 Safer materials = easier deployment (non-experts can use)
Cost:
 Cheaper materials = more accessible for disaster relief
Practicality:
 Easier to contain/activate = more reliable
Example Justification (Heating):
 "Iron + water + salt gives highest ŒîT (+30¬∞C), but CaCl‚ÇÇ (+25¬∞C) might be better because it's faster, easier to control, and more reliable. The trade-off is 5¬∞C less heating for better practicality."
Example Justification (Cooling):
 "NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ gives by far the best ŒîT (-15¬∞C) and is very fast. Even though it requires safety precautions, it's worth it for strong cooling effect. Citric acid + baking soda is safer but only gives ŒîT = -5¬∞C, which might not be enough for medical supply cooling."
Task 5: Consider Trade-offs
 "Think about temperature, duration, safety, and cost"
Trade-off Categories Identified:
Magnitude vs. Safety:
 Higher ŒîT reactions often less safe
Speed vs. Control:
 Faster reactions harder to control/contain
Cost vs. Effectiveness:
 Cheaper materials sometimes less effective
Safety vs. Practicality:
 Very safe reactions may be too weak
Molecular-Level Explanation: Bond Energy
"The Molecular Story: Bond Energy" Section:
For EXOTHERMIC Reactions (Temperature Increases - Heat Released):
Energy Relationship:
Energy needed to break reactant bonds 
&lt;
 Energy released forming product bonds
NET RESULT:
 EXTRA energy released to surroundings as heat
Energy Flow:
System must absorb energy to break old bonds (endothermic step)
System releases energy when forming new bonds (exothermic step)
If Step 2 releases MORE than Step 1 requires ‚Üí net energy release
Excess energy transfers to surroundings (air, container, target object)
Surroundings gain thermal energy ‚Üí temperature increases ‚Üí ŒîT is positive
Example: Calcium Chloride Dissolution (Reaction A)
Molecular Process:
Breaking Bonds (Requires Energy):
Break ionic bonds in CaCl‚ÇÇ crystal lattice (Ca¬≤‚Å∫-Cl‚Åª attractions)
Break hydrogen bonds between H‚ÇÇO molecules
Energy input required: Moderate (ionic lattice relatively strong)
Forming Bonds (Releases Energy):
Form ion-dipole interactions between Ca¬≤‚Å∫ and water molecules (hydration shell)
Form ion-dipole interactions between Cl‚Åª and water molecules (hydration shell)
Energy output: HIGH (strong ion-dipole attractions; each ion surrounded by multiple water molecules)
Net Energy Balance:
Energy released (Step 2) &gt; Energy absorbed (Step 1)
Excess energy = 25¬∞C temperature increase
Surroundings warmer because system gave away energy
For ENDOTHERMIC Reactions (Temperature Decreases - Heat Absorbed):
Energy Relationship:
Energy needed to break reactant bonds 
&gt;
 Energy released forming product bonds
NET RESULT:
 EXTRA energy absorbed from surroundings
Energy Flow:
System must absorb energy to break old bonds (endothermic step)
System releases energy when forming new bonds (exothermic step)
If Step 1 requires MORE than Step 2 releases ‚Üí net energy absorption
Deficit energy pulled from surroundings (air, container, target object)
Surroundings lose thermal energy ‚Üí temperature decreases ‚Üí ŒîT is negative
Example: Ammonium Nitrate Dissolution (Reaction D)
Molecular Process:
Breaking Bonds (Requires Energy):
Break ionic bonds in NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ crystal lattice (NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫-NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª attractions)
Break hydrogen bonds between H‚ÇÇO molecules
Energy input required: HIGH (strong ionic lattice in ammonium nitrate)
Forming Bonds (Releases Energy):
Form ion-dipole interactions between NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫ and water molecules (hydration shell)
Form ion-dipole interactions between NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª and water molecules (hydration shell)
Energy output: MODERATE (ion-dipole attractions weaker than lattice was strong)
Net Energy Balance:
Energy released (Step 2) &lt; Energy absorbed (Step 1)
Energy deficit = 15¬∞C temperature decrease
Surroundings cooler because system took energy from them
Scaffolding Provided
Vocabulary Section (Implied but Not Explicitly Expandable):
 Key terms defined in context throughout instructions:
ŒîT (delta T): Change in temperature (final temp - starting temp)
Exothermic: Releases heat (ŒîT positive)
Endothermic: Absorbs heat (ŒîT negative)
Bond breaking: Requires energy input
Bond forming: Releases energy
System: The chemical reaction itself
Surroundings: Everything outside the system (air, container, etc.)
Worked Example Structure:
 Explicit reasoning for bond energy is modeled in "Molecular Story" section but not labeled as "worked example"
Analysis Support:
 "üìä Your Analysis Tasks" provides structured framework for systematic comparison

https://docs.google.com/forms/d/e/1FAIpQLSe1Aw_bLaNgpw0k6Q8uwI6xX6RqfW0IAHmKUg_hwsSbC3L7iA/viewform?embedded=true

Real-World Engineering Context

Make evidence-based decisions with justified trade-offs

Phase 3: Station 2 - Design &amp; Build Your Prototype (20 points, ~14 minutes)
Central Concept
Application of Station 1 analysis to comprehensive engineering design documentation. Students define design problem precisely (criteria and constraints), create detailed device specifications, predict performance using chemistry principles, and plan initial testing protocol. This is the "thinking" phase of engineering before physical construction.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Targets Addressed:
Target 2:
 Define Design Problem (primary)
Target 3:
 Design and Construct Device (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Applies Target 1 (reaction selection based on investigation)
Prepares for Target 4 (initial version for iteration)
Core Content:
Engineering design problem definition (criteria and constraints)
Systematic device design documentation
Chemistry-based performance prediction
Prototype design specifications
Testing protocol planning
Station Structure
Form Organization (from HTML):
Introduction: Engineering design documentation overview
Step 1: Define Your Design Problem
Device type selection (heating vs. cooling)
Success criteria specification
Constraints identification
Step 2: Design Your Prototype
Chemical reaction selection and justification
Container design and materials
Activation method
Temperature transfer mechanism
Safety features
Step 3: Predict Your Device's Performance
Starting temperature prediction
Maximum/minimum temperature prediction based on ŒîT
Duration estimation
Criterion evaluation (will it meet ¬±10¬∞C requirement?)
Problem anticipation and improvement planning
Engineering Design Documentation Rationale
"üí≠ Engineering Design Documentation" Explanation:
Professional Engineering Practice Context:
 "Professional engineers spend weeks planning and documenting before building prototypes. You'll create the same kind of detailed design specs they use‚Äîdescribing materials, predicting results using chemistry, and thinking through every detail."
Purpose:
Reduces costly prototyping failures through thorough planning
Enables communication with team members and stakeholders
Creates record of design decisions and reasoning
Facilitates systematic evaluation and iteration
Demonstrates understanding before hands-on work
Documentation Over Construction:
 Week 4 emphasizes design thinking and planning rather than physical construction (contrast with traditional "build and test" lab activities)
Rationale:
Deeper engagement with chemistry concepts
More students can "build" simultaneously (no material limits)
Focus on scientific reasoning rather than manual dexterity
Transferable to real engineering careers (most engineering is planning, not building)
Step 1: Define Your Design Problem
Device Type Selection:
Visual Format:
 Two-column grid with color-coded boxes
Option 1: üî• Heating Device
Background: Red (#FEE)
Border: Solid 2px red (#E74C3C)
Description: "Uses exothermic reactions to warm things up"
Option 2: ‚ùÑÔ∏è Cooling Device
Background: Blue (#EFF)
Border: Solid 2px blue (#3182CE)
Description: "Uses endothermic reactions to cool things down"
Decision-Making Prompt:
 Students must choose one and justify based on:
Station 1 reaction analysis (which reactions did they find most effective?)
Personal interest or application scenario
Available chemistry knowledge (feel more confident explaining exothermic vs. endothermic?)
Define Success Criteria:
Criteria Categories Provided:
Temperature Change:
 "How many degrees should it heat/cool?"
Minimum ¬±10¬∞C implied as threshold
Students specify target ŒîT based on application needs
Must be achievable given Station 1 reaction data
Time:
 "How quickly should the reaction occur?"
Emergency scenarios require rapid response
Trade-off between speed and control
Must specify maximum acceptable time
Safety:
 "Must be safe to handle with proper precautions"
All student devices must meet lab safety standards
Consider chemical hazards from Station 1 data
Identify specific safety features needed
Practicality:
 "Could this be deployed in a real emergency?"
Portability requirements
Ease of activation (untrained users?)
Shelf stability (can it be stored long-term?)
Reliability (works consistently?)
Amount:
 "How much heating/cooling is needed for the application?"
Specify target object (food, medical supplies, injury, etc.)
Estimate thermal mass (how much material to heat/cool?)
Determine scale (single use vs. multiple uses?)
Example Criteria Statement (Expected Quality):
 "My heating device must: (1) Reach minimum 40¬∞C temperature (ŒîT ‚â• +20¬∞C from 20¬∞C ambient), (2) Achieve target temperature within 5 minutes, (3) Be safe for untrained users to activate, (4) Be portable enough to fit in emergency kit, (5) Heat approximately 300mL of water or equivalent thermal mass."
Identify Constraints:
Constraint Categories Provided:
Materials:
 "Limited to lab-available chemicals and containers"
Only reactions from Station 1 dataset (A-F) allowed
Standard lab containers (bags, bottles, beakers)
Common household materials for activation/insulation
Cost:
 "Must use affordable, accessible materials"
Emergency relief requires low-cost solutions
Scalability consideration (could thousands be made?)
No exotic or expensive chemicals
Size:
 "Must be portable and practical"
Emergency responders need lightweight, compact devices
Storage space limited in disaster scenarios
Hand-held or small package ideal
Time:
 "Must produce measurable effect within 5 minutes"
Emergency situations can't wait for slow reactions
Immediate response critical for injuries, medical supplies
Reaction completion time constraint
Safety:
 "Cannot use hazardous chemicals beyond lab safety standards"
No explosives, corrosives beyond weak acids/bases
Non-toxic chemicals preferred
Safe disposal considerations
Example Constraints Statement (Expected Quality):
 "My device is constrained by: (1) Only chemicals from Station 1 reactions available, (2) Total material cost must be under $5 per device for scalability, (3) Must fit in 4-inch x 6-inch space for portability, (4) Reaction must complete within 5 minutes maximum, (5) Cannot use chemicals requiring specialized safety equipment beyond basic lab goggles and gloves."
Trade-Off Identification:
Trade-Off Examples Provided in HTML:
Example 1 (from HTML text):
 "Choosing iron + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C) over CaCl‚ÇÇ (ŒîT = +25¬∞C) gives stronger heating, BUT iron oxidation takes longer and is harder to control, so there's a speed vs. magnitude trade-off."
Trade-Off Categories Students Must Consider:
Magnitude vs. Control:
 Highest ŒîT reactions may be hardest to control
Safety vs. Effectiveness:
 Safest reactions may be weakest
Cost vs. Performance:
 Cheaper materials may perform worse
Speed vs. Duration:
 Fastest reactions may not last long enough
Simplicity vs. Optimization:
 Simple designs easier but less efficient
Pedagogical Emphasis:
 "Real engineering requires balancing competing priorities. There's no perfect solution‚Äîonly optimized trade-offs."
Step 2: Design Your Prototype
Required Design Elements:
Design Documentation Table Provided:
Design Element
Questions to Answer
Chemical Selection
Which reaction gave you the best ŒîT? Is it safe? Is it practical?
Quantity
How much of each chemical will you use? Why that amount?
Container
What material? How will it transfer heat effectively?
Activation
How will you mix the reactants? Can it be done safely and easily?
Element 1: Chemical Reaction Selection
Decision Framework:
Review Station 1 data (all six reactions)
Consider criteria defined in Step 1
Balance effectiveness (ŒîT magnitude) against constraints (safety, cost, speed)
Justify choice with chemistry reasoning
Example Justification (Expected Quality):
 "I selected Reaction A: CaCl‚ÇÇ + Water for my heating device because it provides ŒîT = +25¬∞C, which exceeds my criterion of ŒîT ‚â• +20¬∞C. While Reaction B (iron + salt) gives higher ŒîT (+30¬∞C), CaCl‚ÇÇ activates faster (seconds vs. minutes), is easier to control, and is commonly used in commercial hand warmers, making it more practical for emergency deployment. The 5¬∞C lower temperature is acceptable trade-off for better reliability and safety."
Element 2: Container Design
Design Considerations Provided:
Material Selection:
 Plastic bags (flexible, lightweight, cheap), bottles (rigid, reusable), pouches (compact)
Heat Transfer:
 Thin walls = faster transfer; insulation = heat retention
Durability:
 Must withstand reaction heat/cold without rupturing
Activation Mechanism:
 Separate compartments? Breakable barrier? External mixing?
Example Design (Expected Quality):
 "Double-chamber plastic bag: Outer bag (6 x 4 inches) holds water (100mL). Inner bag holds CaCl‚ÇÇ powder (15g). To activate, squeeze to break inner bag, allowing chemicals to mix. Plastic provides good heat transfer to target (food, body part). Seal prevents leaks. Total cost ~$0.50 per device."
Element 3: Activation Method
Activation Options:
Break-and-shake:
 Inner pouch breaks, releasing reactant into solution
External mixing:
 User adds liquid to powder from separate container
Twist-to-activate:
 Mechanical barrier removed by twisting
Pressure activation:
 Squeezing ruptures membrane
Safety Considerations:
Activation must be simple (untrained users)
No exposure to chemicals before activation
Irreversible once activated (can't accidentally reset)
Clear instructions possible
Example Activation Design:
 "Inner water pouch can be broken by firm squeeze. This allows untrained emergency responders to activate device without measuring, pouring, or handling chemicals directly. Once broken, immediate shaking distributes reactants and accelerates reaction."
Element 4: Temperature Transfer Mechanism
Heat Transfer Considerations:
Conduction:
 Direct contact between device and target
Insulation:
 Prevent energy loss to environment
Surface Area:
 Larger contact area = faster transfer
Time:
 How long must device contact target?
Example Transfer Design:
 "Device placed directly against water bottle needing heating. Thin plastic bag allows efficient conduction. Wrapping both device and bottle in towel provides insulation, reducing heat loss to air. Surface area of bag (24 square inches) provides good contact with bottle surface."
Element 5: Safety Features
Safety Requirements:
Chemical containment (no leaks)
Temperature protection (if needed - avoid burns or frostbite)
Disposal safety (how to safely discard after use?)
Warning labels (temperature, chemical hazards)
Example Safety Design:
 "Double-bag design prevents leaks. Maximum temperature 45¬∞C is warm but not burn risk. Clear label: 'CAUTION: Hot when activated. Do not puncture. Dispose in trash after use.' Chemicals (CaCl‚ÇÇ) are non-toxic even if bag leaks."
Step 3: Predict Your Device's Performance
Prediction Framework Provided:
"Based on the chemistry you know and the data from Station 1, predict how your device would perform."
Prediction Categories:
1. Starting Temperature:
 "What starting temperature would you expect?"
Ambient temperature assumption (typically 20¬∞C for room temperature)
Could be different if device stored cold/hot
Affects final temperature calculation
2. Maximum/Minimum Temperature:
 "Based on your chosen reaction's ŒîT, what max/min temperature would you reach?"
Calculation Required:
Final Temperature = Starting Temperature + ŒîT
Example: 20¬∞C + 25¬∞C = 45¬∞C (for CaCl‚ÇÇ heating device)
Example: 20¬∞C - 15¬∞C = 5¬∞C (for NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ cooling device)
3. Duration:
 "How long would the effect last? (Think about reaction rates and quantities)"
Considerations:
Reaction completion time (all reactants consumed)
Heat dissipation rate (how quickly does temperature return to ambient?)
Quantity affects duration (more reactants = longer-lasting effect)
Insulation affects duration (better insulation = slower cooling)
4. Criterion Evaluation:
 "Would it meet your ¬±10¬∞C change criterion?"
Self-Assessment:
Compare predicted ŒîT to defined criterion
Identify if design meets success criteria
If not, must revise design or explain why criterion should change
5. Problem Anticipation:
 "What problems might you run into? How could you improve it?"
Failure Mode Analysis:
Heat loss to environment (poor insulation)
Reaction too fast (hard to control)
Reaction too slow (doesn't meet time constraint)
Insufficient ŒîT (weak reaction or too little reactant)
Safety issues (temperature too extreme, chemical exposure)
Practical issues (hard to activate, bulky, expensive)
Example Prediction (Expected Quality):
 "Starting from 20¬∞C ambient temperature, my CaCl‚ÇÇ device should reach 45¬∞C (20 + 25 = 45). This easily meets my ‚â•40¬∞C criterion. The reaction completes in ~2 minutes, well within my 5-minute time constraint. Heat should last ~10 minutes before cooling back toward ambient. Potential problem: Heat might escape to air too quickly without insulation. Improvement: Wrap device in towel or foam to reduce heat loss and extend duration to ~20 minutes."
Chemistry-Based Reasoning Requirement:
Critical Reminder Provided (in HTML):
 "Remember: Use chemistry to back up your predictions. If you chose CaCl‚ÇÇ with ŒîT = +25¬∞C, your device should reach about 45¬∞C from a 20¬∞C start. Make sure your predictions make sense!"
Purpose:
 Ensures students connect abstract chemistry (bond energy, ŒîT values) to concrete engineering outcomes (actual temperatures, times)
"The Chemistry You're Applying" Section
Conceptual Foundation Emphasized:
Conservation of Energy Principle:
 "Your device will work because of conservation of energy. When chemical bonds break and form during your reaction, the total energy is conserved‚Äîit just transfers between the chemical system and its surroundings."
Engineering Application:
 "The key to your design is choosing a reaction where the energy difference between breaking old bonds and forming new bonds is large enough to produce a useful temperature change in a practical timeframe."
Molecular-Level Connection:
Bond breaking in reactants requires energy input (endothermic step)
Bond forming in products releases energy (exothermic step)
Net difference determines if reaction is overall exothermic or endothermic
Energy transfers to/from surroundings (the target object you're heating/cooling)
Temperature measures thermal energy, so energy transfer causes ŒîT
Purpose:
 Explicit connection between Week 2 (bonds), Week 3 (energy in reactions), and Week 4 (engineering application)

Phase 4: Station 3 - Iterate &amp; Optimize (20 points, ~14 minutes)
Central Concept
Systematic engineering iteration using evidence-based modifications. Students analyze initial design/predictions, gather peer feedback, plan chemistry-justified improvements, model modified design, and evaluate trade-offs. Demonstrates that engineering is iterative process of continuous improvement.
Instructional Focus
Primary Learning Target Addressed:
Target 4:
 Iterate Based on Evidence (primary)
Secondary Connections:
Reinforces Target 3 (design and test)
Applies Target 1 (chemistry knowledge for improvements)
Connects Target 2 (evaluating against criteria)
Core Content:
Test data analysis (Version 1 performance)
Peer feedback collection (Glow-Grow-Chemistry protocol)
Evidence-based modification planning
Chemistry-justified improvement reasoning
Version 2 design and predicted testing
Comparative evaluation (V1 vs. V2)
Trade-off analysis
Version 3 proposal (future iteration)
Station Structure
Form Organization (Inferred from HTML):
Introduction: Iteration overview
Step 1: Analyze Your Test Data (Version 1 evaluation)
Step 2: Gather &amp; Analyze Peer Feedback (collaborative critique)
Step 3: Plan &amp; Implement Improvements (Version 2 design)
Step 4: Iterate Your Improved Design (Version 2 testing and evaluation)
Engineering Iteration Philosophy
"üîÑ Plan Your Improvements" Introduction:
Engineering Reality Emphasized:
 "Think like an engineer refining a design. Look at your Station 2 predictions, consider feedback from classmates, and plan specific improvements for Version 2. Real engineers don't get it perfect the first time‚Äîthey analyze, iterate, and optimize based on evidence and feedback."
Iteration as Professional Practice:
NASA engineers building Mars rovers iterate designs
Chemical engineers designing industrial processes iterate
Pharmaceutical researchers iterate drug formulations
All professional engineering involves multiple design cycles
Key Principle:
 "The key is to use evidence to drive improvements and apply chemistry principles to explain what's happening."
Step 1: Analyze Your Test Data
Version 1 Evaluation Framework:
"Review your Station 2 results" Prompts:
Question 1: Criterion Achievement
 "Would your device achieve the temperature change you wanted? If not, why not?"
Analysis Required:
Compare predicted ŒîT to defined criterion
If insufficient: Identify root cause (too little reactant? poor heat transfer? heat loss?)
If excessive: Consider if it creates safety issues (burns/frostbite risk?)
Quantitative gap analysis
Example Analysis:
 "My criterion was ŒîT ‚â• +20¬∞C, but my prediction shows only +18¬∞C because I used 10g CaCl‚ÇÇ. The problem is insufficient reactant quantity‚ÄîI need more to generate more energy. Gap: 2¬∞C below criterion."
Question 2: Reaction Rate
 "Is the reaction too fast, too slow, or just right?"
Analysis Required:
Compare predicted/actual reaction time to time constraint criterion
Too fast: May be hard to control, violent reaction, safety concerns
Too slow: Doesn't meet emergency response needs
Just right: Completes within acceptable timeframe
Example Analysis:
 "My NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ cooling device activates in ~10 seconds (very fast), which is good for emergencies BUT might be too violent for safe handling. Users might be startled. Ideally 30-60 seconds for controlled activation."
Question 3: Safety and Practicality
 "Might there be any safety concerns or practical problems?"
Analysis Categories:
Temperature extremes:
 Burns (&gt;50¬∞C), frostbite (&lt;0¬∞C)
Chemical exposure:
 Leaks, skin contact, ingestion
Activation difficulty:
 Too complex for untrained users
Durability:
 Container rupture under pressure/temperature
Disposal:
 Safe disposal method available?
Example Analysis:
 "My design reaches 50¬∞C, which could cause minor burns if held directly against skin. Need warning label or insulating layer. Also, double-bag might be hard to break‚Äîneed thinner inner bag or pre-scored tear line."
Question 4: Criterion Comparison
 "How does your device compare to your success criteria?"
Systematic Evaluation:
List each criterion from Station 2
Evaluate: Met, Not Met, Partially Met
Quantify gaps where possible
Prioritize which criteria most important to improve

Common Design Problems &amp; Solutions Table:
Comprehensive Troubleshooting Reference Provided:
Problem
Chemistry Explanation
Possible Solution
ŒîT too small
Not enough reactants or poor heat transfer
Increase quantities; improve contact
Reaction too fast
High concentration or surface area

Reaction too slow

Problem 1: ŒîT Too Small
 
Chemistry Explanation:
 "Not enough reactants or poor heat transfer"
Root Causes:
Insufficient reactant quantity ‚Üí less total energy released/absorbed
Poor thermal contact between device and target ‚Üí energy doesn't transfer efficiently
Heat loss to environment ‚Üí energy escapes before reaching target
Possible Solutions:
Increase reactant quantities (more bonds breaking/forming = more energy)
Improve contact surface area (larger contact = faster/better heat transfer)
Add insulation (reduces energy loss to air)
Use thinner container walls (faster conduction)
Chemistry Reasoning Required:
 "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g will increase ŒîT because more ionic bonds dissociate, releasing proportionally more energy. If 10g gives +18¬∞C, then 15g should give approximately +27¬∞C (assuming linear scaling and no heat capacity limits)."
Problem 2: Reaction Too Fast
 
Chemistry Explanation:
 "High concentration or surface area"
Root Causes (from Week 3 reaction rates):
High concentration ‚Üí more particles ‚Üí more collisions ‚Üí faster reaction
High surface area ‚Üí more exposed molecules ‚Üí more reaction sites ‚Üí faster reaction
High temperature ‚Üí faster particle motion ‚Üí more frequent/energetic collisions
Possible Solutions:
Dilute reactants (lower concentration ‚Üí fewer collisions)
Use larger particles instead of powder (lower surface area)
Control temperature (start cooler if possible)
Slow mixing (gradual reactant combination)
Chemistry Reasoning Required:
 "Diluting NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ solution from 100% saturated to 50% concentration will reduce collision frequency between NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫, NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª, and H‚ÇÇO, slowing dissolution rate. Reaction will still complete but over 60 seconds instead of 10 seconds."
Problem 3: Reaction Too Slow
 
Chemistry Explanation:
 "Low concentration or limited mixing"
Root Causes:
Low concentration ‚Üí fewer particles ‚Üí fewer collisions
Poor mixing ‚Üí reactants don't contact each other
Low temperature ‚Üí slower particle motion
Large particle size ‚Üí low surface area
Possible Solutions:
Increase concentration (more particles available)
Improve mixing method (shaking, stirring)
Use smaller particles or powder (higher surface area)
Pre-warm reactants (higher starting temperature)
Chemistry Reasoning Required:
 "Using powdered CaCl‚ÇÇ instead of pellets increases surface area by ~100x, exposing more ions for immediate dissolution. This should reduce reaction time from 5 minutes to ~30 seconds while maintaining same total ŒîT."
Problem 4: Heat Loss to Environment
 
Chemistry Explanation:
 "Poor insulation"
Root Causes:
Large surface area exposed to air ‚Üí more heat transfer to surroundings
Thin or no insulation ‚Üí high thermal conductivity to environment
Long reaction time ‚Üí more time for heat to escape
Air convection ‚Üí moving air carries heat away
Possible Solutions:
Add insulation layer (foam, fabric, air gap)
Reduce exposed surface area (compact design)
Increase reaction speed (less time to lose heat)
Seal device in insulated container
Chemistry Reasoning Required:
 "Wrapping device in 1cm foam reduces heat transfer rate to environment by ~90%, allowing exothermic reaction heat to stay concentrated near target. This should extend effective heating duration from 5 minutes to ~20 minutes."
Step 2: Gather &amp; Analyze Peer Feedback
Collaborative Engineering Emphasized:
Rationale Provided:
 "Engineers work in teams and critique each other's designs constructively."
Peer Feedback Protocol:
Activity Structure:
Share your design:
 Explain device and test results to partner
Receive feedback:
 Listen to partner's observations/suggestions
Review their design:
 Provide thoughtful, constructive feedback
Identify patterns:
 Compare solutions across designs (did other teams solve similar problems differently?)
Glow-Grow-Chemistry Feedback Framework:
Visual Format (HTML):
 Boxed section with teal border (#38B2AC)
üí¨ Peer Feedback Protocol:
Glow: "What's working really well? Be specific!"
Identify strengths in partner's design
Explain WHY those elements work
Provide encouragement and recognition
Example Glow:
 "Your double-bag activation method is really clever! Breaking the inner bag is intuitive and safe. This design eliminates need for measuring or pouring, which is perfect for emergency responders who might not have chemistry training."
Grow: "What could be improved? Suggest specific changes."
Identify weaknesses or challenges
Propose concrete, actionable improvements
Maintain constructive, supportive tone
Example Grow:
 "Your predicted ŒîT (+18¬∞C) is close but doesn't quite meet your +20¬∞C criterion. Consider increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ quantity from 10g to 12-13g. This should provide the extra 2-3¬∞C needed while staying within your cost and size constraints."
Chemistry Connection: "How could chemistry principles help solve the problem?"
Connect improvements to bond energy, reaction rates, energy transfer
Use Week 1-3 concepts explicitly
Explain molecular-level reasoning
Example Chemistry Connection:
 "To fix the slow reaction (5 minutes), use powdered iron instead of iron filings. Higher surface area means more iron atoms exposed to water and oxygen simultaneously. From Week 3, we know increasing surface area increases reaction rate by providing more collision sites. This should reduce time to ~2 minutes."
Pedagogical Purpose:
Develops peer collaboration skills
Exposes students to alternative solutions
Identifies patterns (common problems, creative solutions)
Reinforces chemistry concepts through explaining to peers
Builds professional engineering communication
Step 3: Plan &amp; Implement Improvements
Evidence-Based Modification Planning:
"Based on your data analysis and peer feedback, plan specific modifications"
Modification Category Framework:
Table Provided:
Modification Category
Examples of Changes
Chemical Adjustments
Change quantities, switch chemicals, adjust concentrations
Design Changes
Different container, improved insulation, better mixing method
Process Improvements
Timing of activation, order of mixing, pre-cooling/heating
Category 1: Chemical Adjustments
Types of Modifications:
Quantity changes:
 More/less reactant (affects ŒîT magnitude)
Chemical substitution:
 Switch to different reaction from Station 1 (affects ŒîT, rate, safety)
Concentration adjustments:
 Dilute or concentrate solutions (affects rate)
Ratio modifications:
 Change stoichiometric ratios (though this is advanced for 7th grade)
Example Chemical Adjustment:
 "Increase CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g while keeping water at 100mL. More calcium chloride means more ionic dissociations, releasing more energy when Ca¬≤‚Å∫ and Cl‚Åª form hydration shells. Predicted new ŒîT: (15g/10g) √ó 18¬∞C = 27¬∞C."
Category 2: Design Changes
Types of Modifications:
Container material:
 Different plastic (thicker/thinner), different shape
Insulation:
 Add foam, fabric, air gap, reflective layer
Mixing mechanism:
 Improve activation method (easier break, better mixing)
Size/shape:
 Optimize for heat transfer or portability
Example Design Change:
 "Replace double-bag with squeeze bottle that has internal membrane separator. Squeezing bottle breaks membrane and simultaneously mixes reactants through bottle design. This provides better control than breaking bag and reduces leak risk."
Category 3: Process Improvements
Types of Modifications:
Activation timing:
 When to trigger reaction relative to application
Mixing order:
 Which reactant added first (if external mixing)
Pre-treatment:
 Pre-cooling/heating reactants before activation
Application method:
 How device contacts target (direct, wrapped, etc.)
Example Process Improvement:
 "Pre-cool water to 10¬∞C before adding to NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ. This lowers starting temperature, giving larger total ŒîT (10¬∞C ‚Üí -5¬∞C = ŒîT of -15¬∞C total cooling instead of 20¬∞C ‚Üí 5¬∞C). More effective for cooling medical supplies."
Critical Chemistry Reasoning Requirement:
Emphasized in HTML (red text, italics):
 "Critical: For EACH modification, explain the chemistry reasoning. Don't just say 'I'll add more salt'‚Äîexplain how adding more CaCl‚ÇÇ will increase the number of ionic dissociations and release more energy."
Example Complete Modification with Reasoning:
Modification:
 Increase CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g
Chemistry Reasoning:
 "CaCl‚ÇÇ ‚Üí Ca¬≤‚Å∫ + 2Cl‚Åª is an exothermic dissociation. Each formula unit that dissociates releases energy when forming ion-dipole interactions with water. More CaCl‚ÇÇ means:
More Ca¬≤‚Å∫ ions forming hydration shells (each releases energy)
More Cl‚Åª ions forming hydration shells (each releases energy)
Proportionally more total energy released
If 10g gives ŒîT = +18¬∞C, then 15g should give approximately ŒîT = +27¬∞C (50% more reactant, 50% more energy, 50% larger ŒîT)
This should now meet my criterion of ŒîT ‚â• +20¬∞C"
Purpose:
 Ensures students connect modifications to molecular-level understanding, not just trial-and-error
Step 4: Iterate Your Improved Design
Version 2 Testing and Evaluation:
"Model and test your modified device. Document:"
Documentation Requirements:
1. Quantitative Comparison:
 "How did ŒîT change from Version 1 to Version 2?"
Expected Response Format:
Version 1: Predicted/Actual ŒîT = X¬∞C
Version 2: Predicted/Actual ŒîT = Y¬∞C
Change: ŒîŒîT = Y - X
Percent improvement: [(Y-X)/X] √ó 100%
Explanation: Why did it change? (chemistry reasoning)
Example:
 "Version 1: ŒîT = +18¬∞C (10g CaCl‚ÇÇ). Version 2: ŒîT = +26¬∞C (15g CaCl‚ÇÇ). Improvement: ŒîŒîT = +8¬∞C (44% increase). This matches prediction because 50% more reactant released approximately 50% more energy (18¬∞C ‚Üí 27¬∞C predicted; 26¬∞C actual is close, 4% less than predicted possibly due to heat loss during mixing)."
2. Time Comparison:
 "Was the reaction faster or slower? By how much?"
Expected Response Format:
Version 1: Reaction time = X minutes/seconds
Version 2: Reaction time = Y minutes/seconds
Change: Œît = Y - X
Faster or slower?
Why? (chemistry reasoning based on Week 3 rate factors)
Example:
 "Version 1: Reaction time = 5 minutes (iron filings). Version 2: Reaction time = 2 minutes (powdered iron). Improvement: 3 minutes faster (60% reduction). This occurred because powdered iron has ~100√ó higher surface area than filings, dramatically increasing collision sites for oxidation reaction. More surface area ‚Üí more simultaneous reactions ‚Üí faster completion."
3. Criteria Evaluation:
 "Did your modifications help you better meet your criteria?"
Systematic Re-Evaluation:
Review all criteria from Station 2
Compare V1 vs. V2 performance for each
Identify which criteria improved, which didn't
Determine if device now meets all criteria
Example Criteria Re-Evaluation:

4. Trade-Offs:
 "Did improving one aspect make something else worse?"
Trade-Off Categories:
Performance vs. Safety:
 Higher ŒîT but more burn risk
Performance vs. Cost:
 Better results but more expensive
Performance vs. Practicality:
 Stronger effect but harder to use
Speed vs. Control:
 Faster but less controllable
Size vs. Effectiveness:
 More compact but less powerful
Example Trade-Off Analysis:
 "Increasing CaCl‚ÇÇ quantity improved ŒîT (18¬∞C ‚Üí 26¬∞C, good!) BUT:
Cost increased ($0.40 ‚Üí $0.60 per device, 50% more expensive)
Mixture became more viscous, reducing flow and slowing heat transfer to target
Device slightly larger (4.0in ‚Üí 4.5in), reducing portability marginally
Overall Assessment:
 The ŒîT improvement was critical (now meets criterion), and the trade-offs are acceptable. Cost is still low (&lt;$1), viscosity issue could be fixed by adding more water (diluting slightly), and size increase is minimal."
5. Final Assessment:
 "Is your device now ready for deployment?"
Readiness Evaluation:
Do all criteria met?
Are trade-offs acceptable?
Are there remaining safety concerns?
Could untrained users operate it successfully?
Is it practical for emergency scenarios?
Example Final Assessment:
 "My Version 2 heating device meets all criteria: ŒîT = 26¬∞C (exceeds 20¬∞C requirement), activates in 2 minutes (within 5-minute limit), safe temperature (46¬∞C won't cause burns), portable (4.5 inches), and costs $0.60 (affordable for disaster relief). The device is ready for deployment with minor recommendation: include clear pictorial instructions for activation since it's designed for untrained emergency responders."

Assessment Type

 Summative assessment (comprehensive, checks all four learning targets)

 20 points (22.2% of week total)
Time Allocation:

Instructional Purpose
Primary Goals:
Synthesize all station work into cohesive engineering report
Demonstrate integration of all Weeks 1-4 chemistry concepts
Assess mastery of all four learning targets
Provide complete documentation of design process
Showcase engineering thinking and scientific reasoning
Assessment Philosophy Reinforcement:
 "This project isn't about building the 'perfect' device‚Äîit's about demonstrating that you understand the chemistry and can apply it to solve real problems. Focus on clear explanations, quantitative evidence, and showing your thinking process."
Professional Engineering Connection:
 Engineering reports document entire design process for stakeholders, team members, and regulatory bodies. Students creating same type of comprehensive documentation.
Content Coverage
Complete Report Structure:
Section 1: Investigation Summary (Target 1)
Content Required:
Which reactions tested in Station 1? (All six: A-F)
Temperature changes (ŒîT) for each reaction (quantitative data)
Classification: exothermic vs. endothermic
Molecular-level explanation: bond breaking/forming energy
Example Expected Response:
 "I tested six reactions in Station 1:
Exothermic: (A) CaCl‚ÇÇ + water (ŒîT = +25¬∞C), (B) Iron + water + salt (ŒîT = +30¬∞C), (C) Vinegar + steel wool (ŒîT = +10¬∞C)
Endothermic: (D) NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ + water (ŒîT = -15¬∞C), (E) Baking soda + vinegar (ŒîT = -3¬∞C), (F) Citric acid + baking soda (ŒîT = -5¬∞C)
Exothermic reactions release heat because the energy released when forming new bonds (hydration shells around ions or new compounds) is GREATER than the energy needed to break the original bonds (ionic lattices, molecular bonds). The excess energy transfers to surroundings as heat, increasing temperature.
Endothermic reactions absorb heat because the energy needed to break original bonds is GREATER than the energy released forming new bonds. The energy deficit is pulled from surroundings, decreasing temperature."
Target 1 Assessment:
 Quantitative data collection, classification accuracy, molecular-level explanation quality
Section 2: Design Problem Definition (Target 2)
Content Required:
Device type choice (heating or cooling) with justification
Specific success criteria (minimum 3, quantified)
Constraints identified (minimum 5, specific)
Chemical reaction selection decision process
Example Expected Response:
 "I designed a HEATING device to warm food/water in emergency scenarios.
Success Criteria:
ŒîT ‚â• +20¬∞C (from 20¬∞C ambient to minimum 40¬∞C)
Activation time ‚â§ 5 minutes (emergency scenarios need quick response)
Safe handling (maximum 50¬∞C to prevent burns)
Portable (‚â§6 inches, fits in emergency kit)
Affordable (‚â§$1 per device for scalability)
Constraints:
Materials: Limited to Station 1 reactions only
Cost: Must use cheap, accessible chemicals
Size: Must be hand-held and packable
Time: Reaction must complete within 5 minutes
Safety: No hazardous chemicals requiring specialized equipment
I selected Reaction A (CaCl‚ÇÇ + water) because: (1) ŒîT = +25¬∞C exceeds my +20¬∞C criterion, (2) Reaction is rapid (seconds to activate, meets time constraint), (3) CaCl‚ÇÇ is safe to handle with basic precautions, (4) Materials are inexpensive and widely available, (5) Commonly used in commercial hand warmers (proven effectiveness). Although Reaction B gives higher ŒîT (+30¬∞C), iron oxidation is slower and harder to control, making CaCl‚ÇÇ more reliable for emergency use."
Target 2 Assessment:
 Criteria specificity, constraint comprehensiveness, trade-off understanding, decision justification quality
Section 3: Design &amp; Testing (Target 3)
Content Required:
Detailed device design description (all 5 elements from Station 2)
Initial test results (quantitative data: starting temp, final temp, ŒîT, time)
Criterion evaluation (did device meet all criteria?)
Chemistry explanation of device function
Example Expected Response:
 "Device Design:
Chemical Reaction: 15g CaCl‚ÇÇ + 100mL water
Container: Double-chamber plastic bag (4√ó6 inches) - inner bag holds CaCl‚ÇÇ powder, outer bag holds water
Activation: Squeeze to break inner bag, shake to mix
Heat Transfer: Thin plastic allows conduction to target (food, water bottle); wrap both in towel for insulation
Safety: Maximum temp 46¬∞C (safe); double-bag prevents leaks; clear warning label
Initial Test Results:
Starting temperature: 20¬∞C (room temperature)
Final temperature: 46¬∞C
ŒîT: +26¬∞C
Time to peak: 2 minutes
Duration above 40¬∞C: ~15 minutes with insulation
Criterion Evaluation: ‚úì ŒîT = +26¬∞C (exceeds +20¬∞C criterion) ‚úì Time = 2 minutes (within 5-minute limit) ‚úì Safe = 46¬∞C (below 50¬∞C burn threshold) ‚úì Portable = 4√ó6 inches (within size constraint) ‚úì Cost = ~$0.60 per device (within budget) All criteria met!
Chemistry Explanation: When CaCl‚ÇÇ dissolves, ionic bonds in the crystal lattice break (requires energy), but then Ca¬≤‚Å∫ and Cl‚Åª ions form very strong ion-dipole interactions with water molecules (releases MORE energy). Each ion is surrounded by multiple water molecules (hydration shell), and the energy released from all these interactions exceeds the lattice energy. This excess energy transfers to the water and bag, increasing temperature by 26¬∞C. The energy comes from bond formation, demonstrating that chemical energy can be converted to thermal energy."
Target 3 Assessment:
 Design detail, quantitative data quality, criterion mapping, chemistry explanation accuracy
Section 4: Iteration &amp; Optimization (Target 4)
Content Required:
Modifications made between Version 1 and Version 2
Chemistry reasoning for each change
Performance comparison (V1 vs. V2 data)
Trade-off analysis
Version 3 proposal (future improvements)
Example Expected Response:
 "Modifications (V1 ‚Üí V2):
Modification 1: Increased CaCl‚ÇÇ from 10g to 15g Chemistry Reasoning: More CaCl‚ÇÇ means more Ca¬≤‚Å∫ and Cl‚Åª ions available for hydration. Since each ion releases energy when forming hydration shell, 50% more ions should release approximately 50% more total energy. Predicted ŒîT increase from +18¬∞C to +27¬∞C. Result: Actual V2 ŒîT = +26¬∞C (prediction accurate!)
Modification 2: Changed from single bag to double-bag design Chemistry Reasoning: Separate storage of CaCl‚ÇÇ and water prevents premature reaction. Activation is controlled and predictable. This doesn't change chemistry but improves reliability. Result: Easier activation, more consistent ŒîT
Modification 3: Added towel insulation Chemistry Reasoning: Insulation reduces heat transfer to surrounding air. Since Q = mcŒîT, reducing heat loss means more thermal energy stays in device/target, maintaining higher temperature longer. Result: Duration above 40¬∞C increased from 5 minutes to 15 minutes

Section 5: Real-World Application
Content Required:
Emergency deployment scenario description
Necessary improvements for real use
Chemistry learning reflections
Engineering process connections
Example Expected Response:
 "Real-World Emergency Use: My heating device could warm baby formula or medical IV fluids in disaster scenarios where electricity is unavailable. Hurricane evacuees, earthquake survivors, or wilderness medical emergencies could use this. To deploy:
Activate device (break inner bag, shake)
Place against bottle of liquid
Wrap both in towel/blanket for insulation
Wait 3-5 minutes for heating
Monitor temperature (don't overheat)
Improvements Needed for Deployment:
Standardized manufacturing (consistent CaCl‚ÇÇ quantity, reliable bags)
Shelf-stable packaging (moisture-proof, long-term storage)
Multilingual pictorial instructions (for international disaster relief)
Temperature indicator (visual cue when safe temperature reached)
Batch testing (quality control for each production run)
Environmental disposal protocol (safe, eco-friendly cleanup)
Chemistry Learning: I learned that chemical energy stored in bonds can be harnessed to do useful work (heating/cooling). Understanding bond breaking (requires energy) vs. bond forming (releases energy) helped me predict and explain device behavior. Conservation of energy means the energy doesn't disappear‚Äîit just transfers from chemical bonds to thermal energy in surroundings. This connects everything: atoms (Week 1), bonds (Week 2), reactions (Week 3), and now engineering application (Week 4).
Engineering Connection: This project showed me that engineering isn't just building things‚Äîit's systematic problem-solving using science. Real engineers:
Define problems precisely (criteria and constraints)
Use scientific principles to make predictions
Test and collect data
Analyze results and identify improvements
Iterate designs based on evidence
Consider trade-offs and make justifiable decisions This is what NASA engineers, chemical engineers, and medical device designers do every day. Now I understand how chemistry knowledge translates to real-world problem-solving."
Comprehensive Learning Assessment:
 Application thinking, realistic deployment considerations, metacognitive awareness, career connections

"üî¨ Key Chemistry Concepts to Include" Section:
Visual Grid Format (6 concepts in auto-fit grid):
‚úì Bond breaking requires energy
 ‚úì Bond forming releases energy
 ‚úì Energy is conserved (transferred)
 ‚úì Temperature measures thermal energy
 ‚úì Reaction rates affect design
 ‚úì Quantity affects magnitude of ŒîT
Purpose:
 Explicit checklist of chemistry concepts that should appear throughout report
Final Success Check:
Pre-Submission Self-Assessment:
‚úì Have you included ALL quantitative data from Stations 1-3?
‚úì Did you explain energy transfer using bond breaking/forming for every reaction?
‚úì Have you justified every design decision with chemistry reasoning?
‚úì Did you compare Version 1 vs. Version 2 with specific evidence?
‚úì Have you demonstrated understanding of criteria, constraints, and trade-offs?

Philosophical Reminder
"üí° Remember" Section (at end):
Key Message:
 "This project isn't about building the 'perfect' device‚Äîit's about demonstrating that you understand the chemistry and can apply it to solve real problems. Focus on clear explanations, quantitative evidence, and showing your thinking process."
Engineering Reality:
 "Professional engineers iterate constantly, and their first designs rarely work perfectly. What matters is that you can use chemistry to explain what happened and make evidence-based improvements!"
Purpose:
Reduce performance anxiety
Emphasize process over product
Normalize iteration and imperfection
Value scientific reasoning over "right answers"
What Was NOT Assessed

Week 2 isolated concepts (ionic vs. covalent distinction) - only if relevant to bond energy
Week 3 isolated concepts (physical vs. chemical changes, conservation of mass) - only if relevant to device
Advanced Chemistry:
Quantitative thermochemistry (ŒîH, ŒîS, ŒîG calculations)

Electrochemistry
Nuclear chemistry
Advanced Engineering:
Professional documentation standards (formal reports)
CAD drawings or technical specifications
Manufacturing processes
Regulatory compliance
Economic analysis (detailed cost-benefit)

Laboratory Skills:
Physical construction techniques
Measurement precision and accuracy
Safety protocol execution
Experimental design (controlled variables)
Error analysis and uncertainty quantification
Real-World Application Connections
Explicitly Mentioned Career/Application Domains
1. Military Applications
MREs (Meals Ready to Eat):
U.S. military uses flameless heaters in field rations for soldiers
Exothermic reactions (typically magnesium-based or iron oxidation) heat pre-cooked meals
No external power source needed (critical in combat zones)
Lightweight, portable, single-use design
Connection to Student Learning:
 Students designing heating devices solving identical engineering problem as military contractors
2. Emergency Medical Services
Instant Cold Packs:
Athletic trainers and paramedics use endothermic reactions for immediate injury treatment
Typically ammonium nitrate dissolution (ŒîT ‚âà -15¬∞C)
Critical for treating sprains, strains, burns within "golden hour"
Lightweight, activatable on demand, no refrigeration needed
Connection to Student Learning:
 Station 1 analyzes same reaction (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ + water) used in commercial cold packs
3. Disaster Relief and Emergency Response
Hurricane Relief Scenario (Anchor Phenomenon):
Hurricane Maria (2017): Puerto Rico without power for months
Emergency responders needed heating/cooling without electricity
Medical supply refrigeration (insulin, vaccines)
Food/water heating for victims
Warming hypothermia victims
Connection to Student Learning:
 Entire Week 4 framed as solving real disaster relief challenge; emphasizes humanitarian impact of chemistry knowledge
4. Space Exploration
NASA Applications:
Chemical heating/cooling systems in spacecraft and space suits
No convection in zero gravity (must rely on conduction)
Battery-free solutions critical for weight reduction
Life support and food preparation systems
Connection to Student Learning:
 Demonstrates that same chemistry principles apply in extreme environments; engineering iteration (mentioned in Station 3) is how NASA develops Mars rovers
5. Outdoor Recreation and Survival
Camping and Hiking:
Hand warmers for cold weather activities
Instant cold packs for injuries on trail
Food/water heating without fire (fire bans, stealth camping)
Survival kits for emergency situations
Connection to Student Learning:
 Portability and activation constraints in student designs mirror real outdoor product requirements
6. Chemical Engineering
Industrial Process Design:
Large-scale exothermic/endothermic reactor design
Heat management in chemical plants
Energy recovery and efficiency optimization
Safety systems for runaway reactions
Connection to Student Learning:
 Understanding exothermic/endothermic reactions is foundational for chemical engineering careers; iteration and optimization (Station 3) mirrors industrial practice
Everyday Phenomena Explained
1. Hand Warmers (Commercial Products)
Chemistry:
Iron + oxygen + water + salt ‚Üí iron oxide (rust) + heat
Exothermic oxidation: ŒîT ‚âà +30¬∞C
Reaction C in Station 1 demonstrates this chemistry
How It Works:
Sealed pouch contains iron powder, salt, activated carbon, vermiculite
Opening pouch allows air (oxygen) to enter
Salt acts as catalyst, water (from air or included) allows reaction
Reaction sustains heat for 4-8 hours
Student Connection:
 Reaction B (iron + water + salt) in Station 1 is same chemistry as commercial hand warmers; students can design similar device
2. Instant Cold Packs (Athletic/Medical)
Chemistry:
Ammonium nitrate dissolution: NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ(s) ‚Üí NH‚ÇÑ‚Å∫(aq) + NO‚ÇÉ‚Åª(aq)
Endothermic process: ŒîT ‚âà -15¬∞C to -25¬∞C
Reaction D in Station 1
How It Works:
Two-compartment bag: water in outer, NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ in inner
Breaking inner pouch mixes reactants
Lattice energy of NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ &gt; hydration energy ‚Üí absorbs heat from surroundings
Cooling effect lasts 15-30 minutes
Student Connection:
 Direct application of Station 1 Reaction D; double-bag activation method in student designs identical to commercial products
3. Self-Heating Beverages (Coffee, Cocoa)
Chemistry:
Calcium oxide + water: CaO + H‚ÇÇO ‚Üí Ca(OH)‚ÇÇ + heat
Exothermic reaction: ŒîT ‚âà +40¬∞C
Similar to calcium chloride reaction in Station 1 (Reaction A)
How It Works:
Can has double wall with CaO in chamber
Twisting can breaks water pouch
Exothermic reaction heats beverage in inner container
Reaches drinking temperature in 2-3 minutes
Student Connection:
 Demonstrates engineering principle: choosing reaction based on criteria (high ŒîT, fast activation, safe chemicals)
4. Ice/Hot Packs (Reusable Gel Packs)
Chemistry:
Sodium acetate supersaturated solution
Crystallization releases heat (exothermic)
Melting absorbs heat (endothermic)
Reversible process via boiling to re-dissolve crystals
How It Works:
Gel pack contains sodium acetate trihydrate solution
Flexing metal disc triggers crystallization (exothermic, heating mode)
Boiling pack re-dissolves crystals (ready for reuse)
Can switch to freezer use (endothermic, cooling mode)
Student Connection:
 Advanced application showing reversibility; demonstrates that exothermic/endothermic processes can be reversed by changing conditions
5. Self-Cooling Beverage Cans
Chemistry:
Ammonium nitrate or evaporative cooling
Endothermic dissolution or phase change
Similar principles to instant cold packs
How It Works:
Can with separate cooling chamber containing endothermic reactants
Activating mechanism mixes reactants or releases compressed CO‚ÇÇ
Beverage cooled to refrigerator temperature in minutes
Single-use design
Student Connection:
 Engineering challenge of cooling liquid quickly mirrors student design constraints (time, temperature change, safety)
