Blended Learning Model
Model Type:
 Sequential Station Progression with Embedded Digital Assessment
Structure:
Hook (whole class, individual digital response)
Station 1 (individual work, ~14 min, digital form)
Station 2 (individual work, ~14 min, digital form)
Station 3 (individual + collaborative peer feedback, ~14 min, digital form)
Exit Ticket (individual synthesis, ~18 min, digital form)
Pedagogical Rationale:
Sequential model necessary due to conceptual dependencies (investigation ‚Üí design ‚Üí iteration)
Digital forms enable complex, multi-part responses
Asynchronous peer feedback possible through shared design documentation
Individual accountability with collaborative elements

Ensure accessibility (complex tools create barriers)
Emphasize content over production (chemistry understanding, not digital artifact creation)
Forms provide sufficient structure for comprehensive assessment

Assessment Type Categorization
Formative Assessments:
Hook (pre-instruction design thinking baseline)
Station self-checks implied in success criteria
Peer feedback in Station 3 (qualitative, formative)
Formative Summative Assessments:
Station 1 form (checks Target 1, informs instruction on energy understanding)
Station 2 form (checks Targets 2-3, informs instruction on design thinking)
Station 3 form (checks Target 4, informs instruction on iteration quality)
Summative Assessment:
Exit Ticket (comprehensive check of all targets; determines Week 4 mastery; contributes to cycle grade)
Engineering Design Assessment:
Process-oriented (design thinking, reasoning) rather than product-oriented (physical device quality)
Documentation quality emphasized over construction quality
Scientific reasoning valued over "correct" design choices
Evidence Types Collected
1. Pre-Instruction Design Thinking (Hook)
Initial engineering hypotheses
Device type preferences
Predicted challenges
Prior knowledge activation (Weeks 1-3 connections)
Question generation
2. Chemistry Content Knowledge (Station 1)
Quantitative data analysis (ŒîT values)
Classification accuracy (exothermic/endothermic)
Comparative analysis (ranking reactions by effectiveness)
Molecular-level explanations (bond energy reasoning)
Engineering decision-making (reaction selection with trade-offs)
3. Engineering Design Documentation (Station 2)
Criteria and constraints specification
Design element descriptions (5 required elements)
Chemistry-based predictions (temperatures, times)
Justification quality (evidence-based reasoning)
Problem anticipation (failure mode analysis)
4. Iteration and Optimization (Station 3)
Data analysis quality (Version 1 problem identification)
Peer feedback integration
Modification planning with chemistry reasoning
Quantitative comparison (V1 vs. V2)
Trade-off analysis sophistication
Future thinking (Version 3 proposals)
5. Comprehensive Synthesis (Exit Ticket)
Complete design process documentation
Integration of all chemistry concepts (Weeks 1-4)
Quantitative data throughout
Professional-quality engineering report
Real-world application thinking
Metacognitive awareness (learning reflections)
Grading Philosophy
Engineering Design Assessment Principles:
Emphasized in HTML (Multiple Locations):
"This is a DESIGN PROJECT‚Äîthere's no single 'right answer'!"
"Focus on using chemistry concepts to explain your design choices"
"We're looking at how well you use chemistry to explain things, back up your choices with data, and improve based on what you learn"
"If your device doesn't work perfectly, that's totally okay‚Äîwhat matters is that you can explain why using chemistry and show how you'd make it better"
Process Over Product:
Scientific reasoning valued over device effectiveness
Justification quality more important than optimal design
Evidence-based decision-making emphasized
Iteration and improvement demonstrated
Standards-Based Approach:
Four learning targets assessed separately
Specific feedback per target
Opportunities for targeted remediation
Target mastery tracked individually
Iteration Encouraged:
Week 5 designated as catch-up week
Retakes and revisions normalized
Feedback-driven improvement supported
Multiple pathways to demonstrating mastery
Chemistry Understanding Prioritized:
Every design decision must have chemistry justification
Bond energy explanations required throughout
Quantitative data must be connected to molecular-level reasoning
Week 1-4 integration assessed
Partial Credit Philosophy (Implied):
Multiple criteria allow partial success (not all-or-nothing)
Trade-off analysis credited (acknowledging limitations shows sophistication)
Evidence-based failed designs valued (if reasoning is sound)
Iteration valued even if final version imperfect
Differentiation in Assessment
Multiple Entry Points:
Hook:
Open-ended (all design ideas welcomed)
No "wrong answers" (reduces anxiety)
Choice (heating vs. cooling)
Station 1:
Six reactions provide range of ŒîT values
Multiple "correct" reaction selections possible (depends on criteria prioritization)
Quantitative and qualitative analysis options
Station 2:
Student-defined criteria (adjustable difficulty)
Constraint flexibility (work within available materials)
Design creativity valued
Station 3:
Self-paced improvement (some students may make more/fewer modifications)
Peer feedback collaborative support
Version 3 proposals allow advanced thinking without execution pressure
Exit Ticket:
Comprehensive but scaffolded by prior station work
Synthesis allows multiple valid approaches
Reflection questions support metacognitive differentiation
Challenge Levels (Not Explicit but Built-In):
Basic Level:
Select high ŒîT reaction (simple decision)
Simple container design (bag)
Single modification (quantity change)
Basic chemistry explanations (bond breaking/forming mentioned)
Proficient Level:
Balance multiple criteria in selection (trade-off analysis)
Sophisticated container design (multiple features)
Multiple coordinated modifications
Detailed chemistry explanations (hydration energy, lattice energy, energy conservation)
Advanced Level:
Optimize trade-offs quantitatively
Innovative design features (beyond provided examples)
Chemistry-driven iteration (predict outcomes before testing)
Professional-quality documentation
Version 3 proposals with detailed implementation plans
Assessment Validity and Alignment
Target 1 ‚Üí Station 1 ‚Üí Exit Ticket Section 1:
Direct assessment of energy transfer investigation
Quantitative data analysis required
Molecular-level explanation required
Consistent across both assessments
Target 2 ‚Üí Station 2 ‚Üí Exit Ticket Section 2:
Criteria and constraints definition in both
Justification required in both
Trade-off analysis emphasized in both
Target 3 ‚Üí Station 2 ‚Üí Exit Ticket Section 3:
Design documentation structure consistent
Chemistry reasoning required throughout
Quantitative predictions emphasized
Device function explanation required
Target 4 ‚Üí Station 3 ‚Üí Exit Ticket Section 4:
Evidence-based modification required
V1 vs. V2 comparison quantitative
Trade-off analysis sophisticated
Future improvements demonstrate iteration mindset
Three-Dimensional Assessment Throughout:
SEP Assessment:
SEP-4 (Analyzing Data): Station 1, comparative analysis in Station 3
SEP-6 (Designing Solutions): Stations 2-3, Exit Ticket comprehensively
DCI Assessment:
PS1.B (Chemical Reactions): Energy transfer explanations throughout
PS1.A (Properties of Matter): Heat transfer considerations in design
CCC Assessment:
CCC-5 (Energy and Matter): Central theme in all stations; energy conservation and transfer
CCC-2 (Cause and Effect): Design choices ‚Üí outcomes; chemistry ‚Üí predictions
Pedagogical Strategies Employed
Cognitive Science Applications
1. Retrieval Practice
Implementation:
Hook:
 "What You Already Know" section explicitly retrieves Weeks 1-3 concepts
Bridging Statements:
 Weeks 1‚Üí4, 2‚Üí4, 3‚Üí4 connections stated
Station 1:
 Requires recall of bond energy concepts (Week 2-3)
Station 2:
 Applies reaction rate knowledge (Week 3)
Exit Ticket:
 Comprehensive retrieval across all four weeks
Rationale:
Strengthens long-term retention through active recall
Creates "hooks" for new learning (engineering concepts)
Demonstrates cumulative knowledge building
Combats forgetting through distributed practice
Evidence of Effectiveness:
Research shows retrieval practice more effective than re-reading (Roediger &amp; Karpicke, 2006)
Week 4 requires integration, forcing meaningful retrieval
2. Worked Examples (Expert Modeling)
Station 1: Molecular-Level Bond Energy Explanation
Exothermic Example Structure:
Energy relationship stated (breaking &lt; forming)
Step-by-step process (break bonds ‚Üí form bonds)
Net result (excess energy ‚Üí heat release)
Observable outcome (temperature increases)
Specific reaction (CaCl‚ÇÇ dissolution detailed)
Endothermic Example Structure:
Energy relationship stated (breaking &gt; forming)
Step-by-step process (break bonds ‚Üí form bonds)
Net result (energy deficit ‚Üí heat absorption)
Observable outcome (temperature decreases)
Specific reaction (NH‚ÇÑNO‚ÇÉ dissolution detailed)
Pedagogical Purpose:
Reduces cognitive load through expert reasoning display
Provides template for student explanations
Makes implicit thinking explicit
3. Metacognitive Monitoring
Embedded Throughout:
Success Check Sections:
End of each station
Pre-submission self-assessment checklists
Criterion-referenced (specific, measurable indicators)
Reflection Prompts:
"What problems might you run into?"
"Did your modifications help you better meet your criteria?"
"Is your device now ready for deployment?"
Self-Evaluation:
Compare actual vs. predicted performance
Identify gaps between criteria and achievement
Propose improvements (metacognitive planning)
Purpose:
Develops self-regulated learning
Improves estimation accuracy
Supports autonomous problem-solving
4. Productive Struggle Normalization
Explicit Messaging:
Hook:
"There's no single 'right answer' for this project"
"Engineers often have multiple valid solutions"
Station 2:
"Professional engineers spend weeks planning and documenting before building prototypes"
Design doesn't have to be perfect, just justified
Station 3:
"Real engineers don't get it perfect the first time‚Äîthey analyze, iterate, and optimize"
"NASA engineers building Mars rovers don't get it perfect on the first try"
Exit Ticket:
"This project isn't about building the 'perfect' device"
"Professional engineers iterate constantly, and their first designs rarely work perfectly"
Pedagogical Purpose:
Reduces performance anxiety
Reframes failure as part of process
Builds growth mindset
Models authentic engineering practice
5. Spaced Repetition (Weeks 1-4)
Concept: Atoms and Molecules
Week 1: Introduction (atoms compose molecules)
Week 2: Bonding (atoms connected by bonds)
Week 3: Reactions (atoms rearrange)
Week 4: Engineering (atoms conserved; energy from atomic rearrangements)
Concept: Energy
Week 1: Implicit (not focus)
Week 2: Bond energy (breaking vs. forming)
Week 3: Reaction energy (exothermic/endothermic)
Week 4: Energy transfer engineering (harnessing bond energy)
Concept: Properties
Week 1: Structure determines properties
Week 2: Bond type determines properties
Week 3: Reactions create substances with different properties
Week 4: Properties determine device function (ŒîT, safety, etc.)
Pedagogical Purpose:
Distributed practice more effective than massed practice
Each week adds complexity while reinforcing foundations
Long-term retention enhanced
6. Elaborative Interrogation ("Why" Questions)
Throughout Week 4:
Station 1:
"Explain the chemistry: Why do these reactions release or absorb energy?"
"What makes a reaction exothermic vs. endothermic?"
Station 2:
"Why did you make these choices?" (criteria, constraints, design)
"Why that amount?" (reactant quantity)
"How will it transfer heat effectively?" (mechanism)
Station 3:
"Why did you make these changes?" (modifications)
"Can you explain each modification using chemistry principles?"
Exit Ticket:
Every section requires explanatory reasoning, not just description
Pedagogical Purpose:
Deeper processing through causal explanation
Connects surface features to underlying principles
Reveals misconceptions through reasoning errors
Builds transferable understanding
7. Concrete-to-Abstract Progression
Week 4 Sequence:
Concrete:
 Hurricane disaster scenario (Hook)
Semi-Concrete:
 Reaction data table with actual ŒîT values (Station 1)
Application:
 Device design using concrete reactions (Station 2)
Abstract:
 Energy transfer principles in iteration (Station 3)
Synthesis:
 Complete engineering process documentation (Exit Ticket)
Within Stations:
Bond energy explanation starts with observable (temperature change)
Proceeds to molecular (bonds breaking/forming)
Ends with principle (energy conservation)
Scaffolding Architecture
1. Vocabulary Support
Format:
 Inline definitions with visual differentiation
Week 4 Vocabulary Grid (Hook):
6 key terms in color-coded boxes
Concise definitions (5-10 words)
Visual icons (üî• for exothermic, ‚ùÑÔ∏è for endothermic)
Embedded Definitions:
ŒîT defined when first used
System/surroundings distinguished
Criteria/constraints differentiated
Purpose:
Front-loads technical language
Provides on-demand reference
Reduces vocabulary barriers to content access
2. Structured Frameworks
Station 1: Analysis Tasks
Five specific tasks listed
Scaffolds data analysis process
Prevents overwhelming open-ended analysis
Station 2: Design Elements
Five required elements specified
Table format organizes thinking
Questions guide decision-making
Station 3: Modification Categories
Three categories with examples
Structures improvement thinking
Prevents random trial-and-error
Exit Ticket: Five Sections
Mirrors station sequence
Clear organization reduces cognitive load
Supports comprehensive synthesis
Purpose:
Reduces decision paralysis
Organizes complex tasks into manageable chunks
Provides clear expectations
3. Visual Organization
Color Gradients Distinguish Sections:
Hook: Purple gradient
Station 1: Pink-red gradient
Station 2: Blue gradient
Station 3: Pink-yellow gradient
Exit Ticket: Purple gradient
Hierarchical Typography:
h2 for major sections (stations)
h3 for subsections (steps)
Responsive sizing (clamp() functions)

Reaction data (Station 1)
Design considerations (Station 2)

Purpose:
Visual wayfinding
Chunking of information
Cognitive load reduction through organization
4. Success Criteria Transparency
Format:
 Checkbox lists at end of each station

Observable, measurable indicators
Aligned to learning targets
Actionable (students can self-assess)

"Did you collect quantitative data?"
"Can you explain temperature changes using bond breaking/forming?"
"Did you identify which reactions are exothermic vs. endothermic?"
Purpose:
Demystifies grading
Enables self-regulated learning
Focuses effort on high-priority elements
5. Worked Examples and Models
Station 1: Bond Energy Explanations
Complete exothermic reasoning modeled
Complete endothermic reasoning modeled
Students can pattern-match to structure
Station 2: Example Justifications
Provided in HTML text as quotations
Shows expected reasoning quality
Demonstrates trade-off articulation
Station 3: Common Problems Table
Chemistry explanations modeled
Solution reasoning demonstrated
Students can adapt to their specific problems
Exit Ticket: "Key Chemistry Concepts" Grid
Provides language for explanations
Models integration across weeks
Purpose:
Reduces uncertainty about expectations
Provides cognitive models for complex tasks
Lowers barrier to high-quality work
6. Gradual Release of Responsibility
Progression Across Week 4:
Hook:
Minimal scaffolding (open-ended)
Baseline assessment of independent thinking
Station 1:
Moderate scaffolding (data provided, analysis tasks structured)
Guided investigation with frameworks
Station 2:
Moderate scaffolding (design elements specified, but choices open)
Supported independence in design
Station 3:
Light scaffolding (peer feedback, problem table available)
High autonomy in iteration
Exit Ticket:
Minimal scaffolding (synthesis required)
Independent demonstration of mastery
Pedagogical Purpose:
"I do ‚Üí We do ‚Üí You do" progression
Builds confidence through structured support
Fades supports as competence develops

Humanitarian crisis framing (Hook)
Real-world applications (military, medical, disaster relief)
Career connections (chemical engineering, NASA)
Autonomy:
Choice (heating vs. cooling)
Self-defined criteria
Design creativity
Self-paced stations
Challenge:
Differentiated difficulty (basic to advanced)
Extension opportunities (Version 3)
Authentic complexity (engineering design)
Emotional Support:
Normalized iteration and imperfection
"No single right answer" messaging
Process-over-product assessment
Growth mindset language
Curricular Scope: What Was Explicitly INCLUDED (New to Week 4)
Engineering Design Concepts Introduced
1. Design Problem Definition
Components:
Criteria:
 Specific, measurable requirements for success
Temperature change targets (e.g., ŒîT ‚â• ¬±10¬∞C)
Time constraints (e.g., activation ‚â§ 5 minutes)
Safety thresholds (e.g., maximum temperature to prevent burns)
Portability requirements (size, weight)
Cost limits (affordability for deployment)
Constraints:
 Limitations and restrictions on solutions
Material availability (limited to tested reactions)
Cost constraints (budget limits)
Size constraints (portability for emergency kits)
Time constraints (reaction completion timeframe)
Safety constraints (no hazardous materials beyond lab standards)
2. Trade-Off Analysis
Concept:
 Competing priorities require balanced decisions; improving one aspect often worsens another
Trade-Off Categories Explicitly Taught:
Magnitude vs. Control (higher ŒîT may be harder to control)
Safety vs. Effectiveness (safest reactions may be weakest)
Cost vs. Performance (cheaper materials may underperform)
Speed vs. Duration (fastest reactions may not last long)
Simplicity vs. Optimization (simple designs easier but less efficient)
3. Engineering Design Cycle
Formal Cycle Introduced (Station 3):
 
Design ‚Üí Build ‚Üí Test ‚Üí Analyze ‚Üí Modify ‚Üí Retest
Key Principles:
Iteration is expected, not failure
Evidence drives improvements
Multiple cycles lead to optimization
Professional practice for all engineers
4. Prototype Design
Definition:
 Initial test model of device
Design Elements Specified:
Chemical reaction selection
Container design and materials
Activation method
Temperature transfer mechanism
Safety features
5. Version Control and Comparison
Concept:
 Tracking changes across design iterations
Systematic Comparison Required:
Version 1 vs. Version 2 quantitative data
Specific metrics (ŒîT, time, cost, size)
Performance against criteria
Trade-off evaluation
6. Peer Review Process
Glow-Grow-Chemistry Protocol:
Glow:
 Identify strengths, explain why they work
Grow:
 Suggest specific improvements
Chemistry Connection:
 Use chemistry principles to solve problems
Purpose:
 Collaborative engineering; exposes students to alternative solutions
7. Failure Mode Analysis
Concept:
 Anticipating what could go wrong before it happens
Problem Categories:
ŒîT too small (insufficient energy transfer)
Reaction too fast (safety/control issues)
Reaction too slow (doesn't meet time constraint)
Heat loss to environment (poor insulation)
8. Real-World Deployment Considerations
Factors Beyond Lab Performance:
Manufacturing consistency (quality control)
Shelf stability (long-term storage without degradation)
User interface (instructions for untrained users)
Environmental disposal (safe, eco-friendly cleanup)
Batch testing (ensuring each unit performs as expected)
Chemistry Concepts Applied (From Weeks 1-3)
Atoms and Molecules (Week 1):
Matter composed of atoms
Atoms rearrange during reactions (Week 3 reinforcement)
Chemical Bonds (Week 2):
Bond breaking requires energy input
Bond forming releases energy
Net difference determines exothermic vs. endothermic
Chemical Reactions (Week 3):
Exothermic reactions release heat (ŒîT positive)
Endothermic reactions absorb heat (ŒîT negative)
Energy conserved (transferred between system and surroundings)
Reaction rates affected by temperature, concentration, surface area (applied in modifications)
New Chemistry Applications
Energy Transfer in Designed Systems:
Harnessing exothermic reactions for controlled heating
Harnessing endothermic reactions for controlled cooling
Managing heat transfer to/from target
Minimizing energy loss to environment (insulation)
System-Surroundings Energy Exchange:
System: Chemical reaction
Surroundings: Target object (food, medical supplies, injury)
Energy flows from system to surroundings (exothermic) or surroundings to system (endothermic)
Bond Energy as Energy Source:
Chemical energy stored in bonds
Breaking old bonds + forming new bonds = net energy change
Energy difference harnessed for practical purposes
Quantitative Energy Measurement:
ŒîT as proxy for energy transfer magnitude
Larger ŒîT = more energy transferred
Relationship between reactant quantity and energy output (proportional, approximated as linear)
Real-World Engineering Applications
Military:
MRE flameless heaters (exothermic reactions)
Field operations without external power
Medical:
Instant cold packs for injury treatment (endothermic reactions)
Immediate response critical for treatment efficacy
Disaster Relief:
Hurricane emergency response (anchor phenomenon)
Heating food/water, cooling medical supplies without electricity
Space Exploration:
Chemical thermal management systems (NASA)
No convection in zero gravity; conduction-based designs
Outdoor Recreation:
Hand warmers for cold weather activities
Instant cold packs for trail injuries
Consumer Products:
Self-heating beverages (coffee, cocoa)
Self-cooling cans (experimental)
Reusable heat/cold packs
Scientific Practices Emphasized (SEPs)
SEP-4: Analyzing and Interpreting Data (Station 1)
Systematic comparison of six reactions
Quantitative data analysis (ŒîT values)
Pattern identification (exothermic vs. endothermic)
Trade-off evaluation using data
SEP-6: Constructing Explanations and Designing Solutions (Stations 2-3, Exit Ticket)
Element 1:
 Construct explanations using bond energy model
Element 3:
 Complete design cycle (define ‚Üí design ‚Üí test ‚Üí analyze ‚Üí modify ‚Üí retest)
Evidence-based decision making
Systematic iteration
SEP-2: Developing and Using Models (Throughout)
Bond energy model for explaining reactions
Mental models of device function
Energy flow diagrams (described, not drawn)
Crosscutting Concepts Emphasized (CCCs)
CCC-5: Energy and Matter (Central Theme)
Element 1:
 Matter conserved (atoms not created/destroyed)
Element 2:
 Energy transfer tracked through designed system
Energy flows from chemical bonds to thermal energy
System-surroundings exchange
Conservation of energy (total energy constant)
CCC-2: Cause and Effect (Throughout)
Design choices CAUSE predictable outcomes
Chemical selection CAUSES specific ŒîT
Modifications CAUSE performance changes
Chemistry principles explain causal relationships
Curricular Scope: What Was Explicitly EXCLUDED
NGSS Standards NOT Addressed
MS-PS1-1 (Molecular Models):
Not focus of Week 4 (addressed Week 1)
3D molecular structures not relevant to energy transfer emphasis
MS-PS1-2 (Evidence of Reactions):
Covered Week 3, not re-addressed
Evidence identification not Week 4 focus
MS-PS1-3 (Synthetic Materials):
No material design or synthesis
No polymer engineering or novel material creation
MS-PS1-4 (Temperature and State Changes):
Connected but not primary focus
Particle motion not emphasized (Week 3 coverage)
MS-PS1-5 (Conservation of Mass):
Covered Week 3, assumed knowledge
Conservation of energy emphasized instead
MS-ETS1-3 (Analyzing Testing Data):
Would require actual physical testing and data collection
Week 4 uses predicted/modeled data rather than experimental data
MS-ETS1-4 (Optimizing Design Solutions):
Iteration included but not formal optimization
No multi-variable optimization algorithms
Advanced Chemistry Concepts NOT Taught
Thermodynamics (Quantitative):
Enthalpy (ŒîH) calculations not performed
Entropy (ŒîS) not introduced
Gibbs free energy (ŒîG) not calculated
Hess's Law not applied
Bond energy values (kJ/mol) not provided
Heat capacity (specific heat) not used in calculations
Q = mcŒîT equation mentioned but not used quantitatively
Calorimetry not conducted
Thermodynamics (Conceptual):
First Law of Thermodynamics not stated formally
Enthalpy as state function not explained
Entropy as disorder not introduced
Spontaneity predictions not made (ŒîG)
Temperature dependence of spontaneity not discussed
Solution Chemistry (Detailed):
Hydration enthalpy values not quantified
Lattice energy values not quantified
Born-Haber cycles not introduced
Solvation thermodynamics not detailed
Activity coefficients not discussed
Colligative properties not addressed
Ion pairing not mentioned
Common ion effect not applied
Kinetics:
Reaction rate laws not introduced (rate = k[A]^m[B]^n)
Rate constants not calculated
Arrhenius equation not applied (temperature dependence)
Activation energy concept mentioned in Week 3 but not Week 4
Collision theory not re-emphasized (Week 3 coverage)
Reaction mechanisms not detailed
Equilibrium:
Chemical equilibrium not relevant (reactions go to completion)
Le Chatelier's Principle not applied
Equilibrium constants not introduced
Dissolution equilibria not calculated
Solubility products (Ksp) not used
Electrochemistry:
Redox reactions not identified (though iron oxidation is redox)
Oxidation states not assigned
Electrochemical cells not relevant
Galvanic cells not discussed
Advanced Engineering Concepts NOT Taught
Quantitative Engineering Analysis:
Heat Transfer:
Heat transfer rate equations not used (Q/t = kAŒîT/L)
Thermal conductivity values (k) not provided
Conduction, convection, radiation not distinguished quantitatively
Fourier's Law not applied
Newton's Law of Cooling not applied
Thermodynamic Efficiency:
Energy efficiency calculations not performed
Carnot efficiency not relevant
Work and heat not distinguished formally
Exergy not introduced
Optimization:
Mathematical optimization not performed
Linear programming not used
Multi-objective optimization not introduced
Pareto frontier analysis not conducted
Lagrange multipliers not applied
Statistical Analysis:
Uncertainty analysis not conducted
Error propagation not calculated
Confidence intervals not determined
Hypothesis testing not applied
ANOVA not used for comparing designs
Engineering Design Standards:
Professional Documentation:
Formal engineering drawing standards (ANSI, ISO) not taught
CAD software not used
Bill of materials (BOM) not created formally
Assembly instructions not generated
Technical specifications not written to industry standards
Manufacturing:
Scalability analysis not quantified
Manufacturing process design not addressed
Quality control protocols not detailed (beyond mention)
Supply chain considerations not analyzed
Cost estimation not rigorous (approximations only)
Regulatory and Safety:
Material Safety Data Sheets (MSDS/SDS) not analyzed
Hazard and Operability Studies (HAZOP) not conducted
Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (FMEA) not performed (beyond basic failure mode consideration)
Environmental impact assessment not quantified
Life cycle analysis not conducted
Regulatory compliance (FDA, EPA) not addressed
Patent searches not conducted
Intellectual property not discussed
Testing and Validation:
Test plans not formalized
Sample size determination not performed
Measurement system analysis not conducted
Calibration not addressed
Reproducibility and repeatability studies not designed
Laboratory Components NOT Included
Actual Physical Construction:
No hands-on building of devices
No manipulation of chemicals
No container assembly
No activation testing
Experimental Data Collection:
No thermometer use for actual temperature measurement
No stopwatches for reaction timing
No balance for mass measurement
No graduated cylinders for volume measurement
Laboratory Safety:
No PPE selection and use
No chemical handling techniques
No spill cleanup procedures
No disposal protocols executed
No safety data sheet (SDS) consultation
Experimental Design:
No controlled variable identification
No independent/dependent variable manipulation
No replication for reliability
No error analysis
No uncertainty quantification
Rationale for Exclusion:
Focus on design thinking and documentation
Emphasis on chemistry reasoning over lab technique
Time constraints (70 minutes insufficient for physical testing)
Safety considerations (some reactions best demonstrated rather than student-executed)
Assessment of understanding, not manual dexterity
Science and Engineering Practices NOT Explicitly Addressed
SEP-1: Asking Questions
Minimal emphasis (Hook has some question generation)
Students not developing research questions systematically
SEP-3: Planning and Carrying Out Investigations
No student-designed experiments
No procedure writing
No variable control planning
Virtual/predicted testing rather than actual investigation
SEP-5: Using Mathematics and Computational Thinking
Minimal mathematical analysis
No equation solving
No computational modeling
No programming or simulation
SEP-7: Engaging in Argument from Evidence
Peer feedback included but not formal argumentation
No structured debate or critique protocols
No rebuttal or counter-argument required
SEP-8: Obtaining, Evaluating, and Communicating Information
No research beyond provided data
No source evaluation
No literature review
No presentation creation
Crosscutting Concepts NOT Explicitly Addressed
CCC-1: Patterns
Implicit (reaction patterns in data) but not focal
Not emphasized as explicit lens
CCC-3: Scale, Proportion, and Quantity
Quantity mentioned (more reactant ‚Üí larger ŒîT)
Not quantified proportionally (no equation Y = kX)
Scaling not addressed (e.g., 10√ó batch size)
CCC-4: Systems and System Models
System-surroundings mentioned but not fully developed
System boundaries implicit but not mapped
Input-output diagrams not created
Feedback loops not identified
CCC-6: Stability and Change
Reactions represent change
Stability not contrasted (equilibrium not addressed)
Vertical Coherence (Cross-Week Integration)
Complete Week 1-4 Progression
Conceptual Arc:
Week 1:
 WHAT exists (atoms, molecules, structures, properties)
Week 2:
 WHY and HOW atoms combine (chemical bonds, electron behavior)
Week 3:
 WHAT HAPPENS when bonds break/form (chemical reactions, energy transfer, evidence)
Week 4:
 APPLY EVERYTHING to solve real problems (engineering design using chemistry)
Explicit Bridging Throughout Week 4
Week 1 ‚Üí Week 4:
Bridge Statement (Hook):
 "Atoms &amp; Molecules: Matter is made of particles that can rearrange"
Application in Week 4:
Atoms in reactants rearrange to form products
Same atoms present before and after (conservation)
Device design requires understanding atomic rearrangement
Week 2 ‚Üí Week 4:
Bridge Statement (Hook):
 "Chemical Bonds: Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
Application in Week 4:
Central chemistry principle for entire week
Bond energy difference determines exothermic vs. endothermic
Molecular-level explanations rely on Week 2 bond concepts
Every device function explanation requires bond energy reasoning
Week 3 ‚Üí Week 4:
Bridge Statement (Hook):
 "Chemical Reactions: Reactants transform into products, but mass is conserved"
 "Energy Transfer: Energy can move between substances and their surroundings"
Application in Week 4:
Six reactions from Week 3 context (exothermic/endothermic learned Week 3)
Reaction rates considered in modifications (Week 3 rate factors)
Conservation of energy (not mass) emphasized
Evidence of reactions implicit (temperature change)
Skills Progression Across Weeks
Modeling (SEP-2):
Week 1:
 Build 3D molecular models (PhET)
Week 2:
 Describe electron arrangements and bonds
Week 3:
 Use balanced equations to model atom conservation
Week 4:
 Use bond energy model to explain and predict energy transfer
Data Analysis (SEP-4):
Week 1:
 Analyze structures for geometry patterns
Week 3:
 Analyze evidence before/after reactions
Week 4:
 Systematically analyze reaction data (ŒîT comparisons), comparative evaluation (V1 vs. V2)
Explanation Construction (SEP-6):
Week 1:
 CER framework (structure ‚Üí property)
Week 2:
 Causal explanations (electron behavior ‚Üí bonding ‚Üí properties)
Week 3:
 Mechanistic explanations (bond breaking/forming ‚Üí energy/evidence)
Week 4:
 Engineering explanations (chemistry principles ‚Üí design decisions ‚Üí predicted performance)
Design Practice (SEP-6 Element 3 - NEW in Week 4):
Complete engineering design cycle
Criteria and constraints definition
Evidence-based iteration
Trade-off analysis
Vocabulary Continuity and Expansion

Chemistry Concept Integration
Bond Energy (Central Integration Point):
Week 2 Foundation:
Bonds hold atoms together
Electron sharing (covalent) or transfer (ionic)
Bond formation stabilizes atoms
Week 3 Development:
Bond breaking requires energy input
Bond forming releases energy
Exothermic: forming releases more than breaking requires
Endothermic: breaking requires more than forming releases
Week 4 Application:
Bond energy difference harnessed for heating/cooling
Quantitative predictions based on ŒîT data
Energy transfer explained molecularly
Engineering decisions based on energy considerations
Energy Flow (Progressive Understanding):
Week 1:
Not emphasized
Week 2:
Implicit (energy in bond formation)
Week 3:
Explicit (exothermic releases, endothermic absorbs)
Conservation of energy introduced
System-surroundings not clearly distinguished
Week 4:
System-surroundings boundary explicit
Energy flow tracked through designed system
Conservation of energy applied to engineering
Thermal energy transfer optimized
Horizontal Coherence (Within Week 4)
Concept Building Sequence Within Week 4
Logical Progression:
Hook:
Problem established (emergency heating/cooling need)
Prior knowledge activated (Weeks 1-3)
Design challenge introduced
Vocabulary introduced
‚Üì
Station 1:
Investigate six reactions systematically
Collect quantitative data (ŒîT values)
Classify as exothermic/endothermic
Explain molecularly (bond energy)
Select optimal reaction for device
‚Üì
Station 2:
Define design problem (criteria, constraints)
Create detailed device design
Justify all decisions with chemistry
Predict performance quantitatively
‚Üì
Station 3:
Analyze Version 1 performance
Gather peer feedback
Plan evidence-based modifications
Compare V1 vs. V2 performance
Evaluate trade-offs
‚Üì
Exit Ticket:
Synthesize entire design process
Demonstrate chemistry understanding
Document complete engineering cycle
Reflect on learning and application
Conceptual Dependencies:
Must investigate reactions BEFORE selecting one for design
Must design device BEFORE iterating improvements
Must complete stations sequentially for coherent engineering process
Consistent Threads Across Week 4
Thread 1: Bond Energy as Foundation
Present Throughout:
Hook: "Breaking bonds requires energy; forming bonds releases energy"
Station 1: Complete molecular-level explanations of exothermic/endothermic
Station 2: Device function explained through bond energy
Station 3: Modifications explained through bond energy (more reactant ‚Üí more bonds ‚Üí more energy)
Exit Ticket: Bond energy in every chemistry explanation
Thread 2: Quantitative Data
Required Throughout:
Station 1: ŒîT values for all six reactions
Station 2: Predicted starting/final temperatures, times
Station 3: V1 vs. V2 comparison tables with numbers
Exit Ticket: All quantitative data reported
Thread 3: Evidence-Based Decision Making
Emphasized Throughout:
Station 1: Data drives reaction selection
Station 2: Criteria drive design choices
Station 3: Test results drive modifications
Exit Ticket: Justifications reference specific evidence
Thread 4: Real-World Application
Maintained Throughout:
Hook: Hurricane disaster relief
Station 1: "Why Should You Care" connections
Station 2: Emergency deployment considerations
Station 3: Engineering design cycle (professional practice)
Exit Ticket: Real-world deployment section
Thread 5: Chemistry Reasoning
Required Throughout:
Every design decision must have chemistry justification
Every modification must explain molecular-level causation
Every prediction must connect to chemical principles
Assessment values reasoning over results
Problem-Solution Arc
Beginning (Hook):
Problem:
 How to heat/cool without electricity in disaster?
Challenge:
 Design device using chemical reactions
Investment:
 Humanitarian crisis framing
Middle (Stations):
Station 1:
 Which reactions could work? (investigation)
Station 2:
 How would device work? (design)
Station 3:
 How to make it better? (optimization)
End (Exit Ticket):
Solution:
 Complete device documentation
Chemistry:
 Full explanation of function
Deployment:
 Real-world feasibility assessment
Learning:
 Reflection on engineering and chemistry integration

Time Allocation Rationale
Hook (10 minutes):
Phenomenon introduction and context setting
Prior knowledge activation
Vocabulary introduction
Initial hypothesis generation (form submission)
Shortest component (low-stakes pre-assessment)
Stations 1-3 (14 minutes each):
Balanced allocation
 reflects equal importance
Station 1:
 Data analysis and chemistry explanations
Station 2:
 Design documentation (most complex conceptually)
Station 3:
 Iteration planning and peer feedback
Exit Ticket (18 minutes):
Longest single component (comprehensive synthesis)
Five sections to complete
Integration of all prior work
Reflection included
Sufficient time for thoughtful responses without rushing
Pacing Flexibility
Built-In Flexibility:
Self-Paced Digital Forms:
Fast finishers can proceed immediately
Struggling students can take extra time (up to point)
No synchronization required between students
Station Independence:
No group dependencies
Individual progression
Asynchronous completion possible
Overflow Management:
Week 5 designated as catch-up week
Incomplete work can be finished outside class
Canvas LMS provides 24/7 access to materials
Time Management Supports:
Clear time estimates provided for each component
Success criteria help students prioritize
No external dependencies (simulations, videos) that could delay

Week 4 Analysis:
Moderate total time (fits single 75-minute period with buffer)
Moderate time efficiency (0.78 min/point)
Highest conceptual complexity (integration of all prior weeks + new engineering)
Time allocation reflects depth over breadth (quality documentation over quantity)
Feasibility:
70 minutes fits within standard 75-minute block
5-minute buffer for transitions, technical issues
Self-paced format accommodates variability
Week 5 catch-up provides safety net
Language and Tone Analysis
Formality Level
Overall Register:
 Professional-casual hybrid
Characteristics:
Clear scientific vocabulary used consistently
Conversational connectors ("Now you get to think like a scientist")
Professional engineering language ("Professional engineers spend weeks planning")
Accessible academic tone (not overly technical, not overly colloquial)
Comparison to Previous Weeks:
Week 1:
 Most casual ("molecular rizz," heavy slang, "boss fight")
Week 2:
 Moderate formality (reduced slang, scientific terms emphasized)
Week 3:
 Higher formality (minimal slang, maximum scientific vocabulary)
Week 4:
 Professional engineering tone (authentic career language, minimal slang)
Week 4 Unique Features:
Engineering terminology integrated naturally
Professional practice referenced frequently
Humanitarian framing (disaster relief, life-saving)
Career pathway language (NASA, chemical engineers, designers)
Student-Centered Language Examples
Reassurance and Encouragement:
"There's no single 'right answer' for this project"
"Engineers often have multiple valid solutions to the same problem"
"If your device doesn't work perfectly, that's totally okay"
"Professional engineers iterate constantly, and their first designs rarely work perfectly"
"Now you totally understand the chemistry that makes these devices work!"
Clarity and Accessibility:
"What You'll Do:" sections at each station (explicit expectations)
"Think about it:" prompts (metacognitive scaffolding)
"Remember:" reminders (reinforcement)
"Critical:" emphasis for key points (attention direction)
Empowerment:
"Now you get to think like a scientist"
"You just designed one using the same science that professional engineers use!"
"You're working exactly like actual chemical engineers"
"This is literally how science and engineering work in the real world"
Connection to Real World:
"This is what NASA engineers, chemical engineers, and medical device designers do every day"
"You're running real experiments, designing actual devices, collecting real data"
"Now I understand how chemistry knowledge translates to real-world problem-solving"
